Intra-Hanafi Differences on Specific Animals: Chicken Shot with an Arrow and Types of Crows
Commentary on specific cases: chicken shot with arrow and classification of crows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi differences regarding a chicken shot with an arrow and the classification of crows, and identify the mufta bihi positions.

Core Idea
Even within the Hanafi school, there are legitimate differences on specific cases, such as a chicken killed by an arrow and the types of crows.

This portion of the commentary explores specific applications of the general prohibition. The commentator discusses the ruling on a chicken that is shot with an arrow rather than slaughtered, and then categorizes different types of crows, showing where there is consensus and where there is disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the chicken scenario
The chicken is shot with an arrow. Determine if it could reach its home. If not, it is halal due to inability to slaughter. If it can, then the ruling depends on where the arrow hit.
The key condition is whether the animal can be reached for regular slaughter.
2
Identify the ikhtilaf
Muhammad al-Shaybani says if the arrow hits elsewhere than the throat, it is not halal; others say it is halal.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference.
3
Determine the basis
The difference stems from whether the animal is considered properly slaughtered or killed by a means other than dhabh. Shaybani may require the cut to be in the throat.
4
Apply to crows
Crows are divided into four types by diet: grains only (halal consensus), carrion only (haram consensus), predatory (haram consensus), and mixed (halal according to Abu Hanifa, some say makruh).
The mixed crow is called 'al-'aq'uq'.
5
Note the principle
The general hadith prohibits predatory birds; the mixed crow is not a habitual predator, so it is halal unless there is another reason.
The disagreement on the mixed crow is about its classification, not the general rule.

Source Text

And not (any animal) with a fang or a claw from a beast of prey or a bird, nor insects, nor domestic donkeys, and the mule is part of its mother in reality, due to its connection to her, and legally, so that it enters into the rulings that apply to the mother regarding sale, gift, and manumission. And for the Imam (Abu Hanifa) is his statement: Indeed Allah has forbidden carrion, and it is the animal that died without slaughter, and the fetus died of its own accord, so it is forbidden by the Book. And it is disliked to slaughter a sheep when its birth is near, because it wastes what is in its belly. If a chicken is shot with an arrow and hits it, if it cannot reach its home, it is permissible to eat it, because it is unable to perform optional slaughter; but if it can reach its home, al-Faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned: if the arrow hits the throat, it is permissible; if it hits elsewhere, according to Muhammad (al-Shaybani) it is not permissible, and according to others it is permissible. [1] His statement: 'And not (any animal) with a fang...' etc.; this is an explanation of what is permissible and what is not. Know that the Prophet, peace be upon him, forbade eating any animal with a fang from the beasts of prey, and eating any bird with a talon, and similarly predatory birds and animals, not everything that has a talon or fang. And what is meant by sabu' is every animal that habitually snatches, plunders, wounds, and kills. Then the act of seizing is from the actions of birds, and plundering is from the actions of beasts. The talon for a bird is like the nail for a human, and the fang in Persian is: 'jahār dandān-i shatr' (four teeth like a camel). And the hasharaat are the small crawling creatures of the earth, plural of hashara, like the ant, the mouse, the gecko, the lizard, the snake, the frog. And al-jarith: with a fatha on the jim, and a tashdid on the ra, and ending with a tha: a type of fish, it is haram according to us, and similarly mar-mahi. And al-intihab is plundering, and al-ikhtitaf is snatching; the first is attributed to a crow-like (bird). Know that the crow is of four types: a type that eats only grains, it is halal by consensus because it is not among the predatory birds, and it does not eat... And a type that eats only carrion, it is haram by consensus. And a type that is counted among the predatory birds, it is also haram by consensus. And a type that combines grains and carrion, it is halal according to the greatest (imam), it is called al-'aq'uq, and in Persian: 'akah'; and it is said it is makruh, and it is a bird with a long tail, having black and white, it is said: the crow of the field called al-zaytun, and it is a small bird of Jannah, red-legged, black-bodied, does not eat except grains. [1] His statement: 'And the horse...' etc.; meaning the horse is not permissible. And what is understood from 'al-Majma'' is prohibition, and from 'al-Hidayah' is karahah tahrimi. It is said regarding the difference between haram and makruh tahrimi: that the doer of the former...
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn (base text) of al-Wiqayah, specifically on the section regarding permissible and impermissible animals for consumption. The commentator begins by citing the base text's prohibition of animals with fangs or claws from beasts of prey or birds, as well as insects and domestic donkeys. He then explains the ruling on the mule, which is legally considered part of its mother, thus subject to the same rulings regarding sale, gift, and manumission. Following this, a separate ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa is mentioned: the prohibition of carrion (maytah) based on the Qur'an, and the dislike (karahah) of slaughtering a ewe near parturition because it wastes the fetus. There is also a discussion about a chicken shot with an arrow: if it cannot be reached for regular slaughter, it is halal; if it can be reached, there is a difference of opinion within the Hanafi school—Muhammad al-Shaybani says it is not halal if the arrow hits elsewhere than the throat, while others say it is halal. The commentary then returns to the main prohibition, citing the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbidding the consumption of any animal with a fang from predatory beasts or any bird with a talon. The commentator defines 'sabu'' as any animal that habitually snatches, plunders, injures, and kills, and distinguishes between the actions of birds (ikhtilaf) and beasts (intihab). He then explains the terms 'hasharaat' (small crawling creatures) and provides examples that are haram. The passage also discusses the 'jarith' fish and 'mar-mahi' as haram according to the Hanafi school. Finally, the commentary addresses the different types of crows, dividing them into four categories based on diet: those that eat only grains (halal by consensus), those that eat only carrion (haram by consensus), those that are predatory (haram by consensus), and those that eat both grains and carrion (halal according to Abu Hanifa, though some say makruh). The passage concludes with the start of a discussion on horse meat, stating that it is not permissible, with a difference between 'al-Majma'' (which says haram) and 'al-Hidayah' (which says makruh tahrimi), but the text cuts off before completing the distinction.

Key Terms
ذو ناب (dhu nāb): An animal possessing a fang, i.e., a canine tooth used for predation.
ذو مخلب (dhu mikhlab): A bird possessing a talon, used for seizing prey.
السبع (al-sabu'): Any predatory animal that habitually snatches, plunders, wounds, and kills.
الحشرات (al-hasharāt): Small crawling creatures of the earth, such as ants, mice, geckos, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of crow is universally considered halal by consensus?
A) Carrion-eating crow
B) Predatory crow
C) Grain-eating crow
D) Mixed-diet crow

Key Takeaway
Even within the Hanafi school, specific cases like the chicken shot with an arrow and types of crows show nuanced internal ikhtilaf, highlighting the school's flexibility.

Acceptance of Non-Muslims' Statements in Transactions
Ruling with proof: acceptance of non-Muslims' statements in transactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts a non-Muslim's statement in transactions and distinguish between explicit and implicit establishment of rulings.

Core Idea
Necessity in frequent transactions justifies accepting even a non-Muslim's statement; the religious ruling follows implicitly.

In this passage, we examine a practical fiqh ruling: when a non-Muslim vendor tells you the meat you bought is from a Muslim or Kitabi, is his word accepted? The Hanafi school says yes, based on necessity and the principle that certain rulings are established implicitly even if they wouldn't be accepted explicitly. Let's walk through the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The matn states that the statement of a non-Muslim who says he bought meat from a Muslim or Kitabi (making it halal) or from a Magian (making it haram) is accepted in a transaction. What is the exact ruling being established?
The ruling is about acceptance (qabul) of testimony in commercial matters.
2
Understand the objection
A reader might object: permissibility of meat is a religious ruling, and in religious matters only the word of a just person is accepted. How does the commentator address this?
The objection is that a non-Muslim is not 'adl.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The commentator argues that the non-Muslim's statement is primarily about the purchase (a transaction), and the permissibility or prohibition is an implicit consequence (dhimni). Thus it is accepted implicitly even if not accepted explicitly. Find the examples given: waqf of movable property and sale of water rights.
The key principle: 'Many things are established implicitly that are not established explicitly.'
4
Extend the principle
The commentator then states the broader rule: in transactions, the report of any discerning person (mumayyiz) is accepted, regardless of religion, gender, slavery, or age. Why? Because of general necessity—people rarely find just witnesses for every transaction.
This extends beyond non-Muslims to women, slaves, children, etc.
5
Apply the ruling
Now think of a modern scenario: you buy meat from a butcher of unknown background. He says it's halal. According to this principle, his word is accepted in the transaction, and the halal status follows implicitly unless there is evidence to the contrary.
This facilitates daily commerce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقبل قول كافر قال : شريتُ اللحم من مسلم أو كتابي فحل، أو مجوسي فحرم وقول فرد كافر، أو أنثى، أو فاسق ، أو عبد، أو ضدها في المعاملات (وقبل قولُ كافر قال : شريتُ اللحم من مسلم أو كتابي فحل، أو مجوري فحرم)، فإنَّ قول الكافرِ مقبول في المعاملات ؛ للحاجة إليه إذ المعاملات كثيرة الوقوع. (وقول فرد " كافر ، أو أنثى ، أو فاسق، أو عبد ، أو ضدها) في المعاملاتِ [۱] قوله : مقبول ... الخ ؛ لا يقال : كان ينبغي أن لا يقبل قوله ؛ لأنه إخبار بأن هذا لحم حلال ، والحل والحرمة من الديانات، ولا يقبلُ في الديانات إلا قــول العــدل ، والمجوسي وكذا المشرك ليس بعدل. لانا نقول : إنّه إخبار بالشراء من يهودي أو نصراني أو مسلم، وإنه من المعاملات، وإنّما يثبتُ الحلّ في ضمنه وكذلك لو قال : اشتريته من غيرهم؛ إثباتُ الحرمة فيه ضمني، فلمّا قُبِلَ قوله في الشراء يثبتُ ما في ضمنه ؛ لأنه كم من شيء يثبتُ ضمناً ولا يثبت قصداً، كوقف المنقول ضمناً بغير المنقول، وكبيع الشرب وغيره. كذا في «الكفاية»(۲). [۲] قوله : وقـول فـرد...الخ ؛ أي قبل قول فرد...الخ، والأصل أن المعاملات يقبل فيها خبر كل مميز حراً كان أو عبداً ، مسلماً كان أو كافراً، كبيراً كان أو صغيراً ؛ لعموم الضرورة، فإنّ الإنسان قلما يجد المستجمع لشرائط العدالة ليعاينه أو يستخدمه ويبعثه إلى وكلائه ونحو ذلك، ولا دليل مع السامع سوى الخبر؛ ولأن المعاملات ليس فيها إلزام واشتراط العدالة الالزام. فلا معنى للاشتراط فيها ؛ لأنّ الحال فيه حال مسالمة، لا حال منازعة ؛ ولأنّ المعاملات كثير كما مر من الشارح الله ، فإذا قبلَ فيها قولُ المميز وكان في ضمن قبوله فيها قبوله في الديانات، يقبل في الديانات ضمناً ضرورة، وكم من شيء لا يصح قصداً يصح ضمناً ؛ ولأنّ كلّ معاملة لا تخلو عن ديانة، فلو لم يثبت فيها في ضمن المعاملات
And the statement of a non-Muslim who says: 'I bought the meat from a Muslim or a Kitabi, so it is permissible, or from a Magian, so it is forbidden' is accepted. And the statement of a single non-Muslim, or a woman, or a sinner, or a slave, or their opposites in transactions. (And the statement of a non-Muslim who says: 'I bought the meat from a Muslim or a Kitabi, so it is permissible, or from a Magian, so it is forbidden' is accepted). Indeed, the statement of a non-Muslim is accepted in transactions due to necessity, because transactions occur frequently. (And the statement of a single non-Muslim, or a woman, or a sinner, or a slave, or their opposites in transactions). [Footnote 1: His statement: 'accepted...' etc.; it should not be said that his statement should not be accepted because it is an assertion that this meat is permissible, and permissibility and prohibition are religious matters, and in religious matters only the statement of a just person is accepted, and the Magian and similarly the polytheist is not just. We respond: it is an assertion of purchase from a Jew, Christian, or Muslim, and that is from transactions, and permissibility is established as a consequence thereof. Similarly if he said: 'I bought it from someone else'; the establishment of prohibition is implicit. So since his statement is accepted in the purchase, what is implicit in it is established, because many things are established implicitly that are not established explicitly, like the waqf of movable property implicitly through immovable property, and the sale of water rights, etc. Thus in al-Kifayah. [Footnote 2: His statement: 'and the statement of a single...' etc.; meaning the statement of a single person is accepted, etc. The principle is that in transactions, the report of every discerning person is accepted, whether free or slave, Muslim or non-Muslim, adult or minor, due to the generality of necessity. For a person rarely finds someone who combines the conditions of justice to witness or employ him and send him to his agents, etc. And the listener has no evidence other than the report. Also, because transactions do not involve compulsion, and requiring justice is compulsory. So there is no meaning to stipulating it, because the situation is one of peace, not dispute. And because transactions are many, as the commentator mentioned. So when the statement of a discerning person is accepted in them, and his acceptance in them includes acceptance in religious matters, it is accepted in religious matters implicitly out of necessity. And many things that are not valid explicitly are valid implicitly. And because every transaction involves a religious aspect, if it were not established in them implicitly, it would be problematic.]
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof concerning the acceptance of testimony from non-Muslims and others in commercial transactions. The matn establishes that the statement of a non-Muslim who claims to have bought meat from a Muslim or Kitabi (making the meat permissible) or from a Magian (making it forbidden) is accepted in a transaction. This is because transactions are frequent and there is a general necessity to accept the word of the parties involved. The commentator addresses an objection: how can a non-Muslim's statement be accepted in religious matters (like permissibility of meat), when only a just Muslim is accepted? The response is that the non-Muslim's statement is primarily about the act of purchase (a transaction), and the permissibility or prohibition is an incidental consequence. What is established implicitly (dhimnan) is valid even if it would not be valid explicitly (qasdan). Examples given include the waqf of movable property as an implicit part of a waqf of immovable property, and the sale of water rights as part of land sale. Furthermore, the principle is extended: in transactions, the report of any discerning person (mumayyiz) is accepted, regardless of religion, gender, status, or age, due to the widespread need. The condition of 'adalah (justice) is not required because transactions are not coercive and involve mutual consent. This ruling highlights the Hanafi principle of necessity overriding strict evidentiary requirements.

Key Terms
قبل (qubila): Accepted as valid evidence or testimony.
معاملات (muʿāmalāt): Transactions, commercial dealings.
ضمني (ḍimnī): Implicitly, as a consequence of another established fact.
مميز (mumayyiz): A discerning person, one who has reached the age of discernment (tamyiz).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the statement of a non-Muslim accepted in transactions?
A) Non-Muslims are considered just in commercial matters.
B) Because transactions are frequent and there is general necessity.
C) Because the non-Muslim's statement is about religious matters which are always accepted.
D) Because the Hanafi school does not require any conditions for testimony.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the statement of a non-Muslim is accepted in transactions because of necessity, and the permissibility of the object is established implicitly as a consequence.

Touching Permissible Where Looking Permissible: The Hanafi Rule and Its Proof
Ruling with proof: extending looking permissibility to touching, with buying exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that touching is permissible where looking is permissible, apply the exception for buying a slave, and cite the hadith proof from the Prophet kissing Fatima.

Core Idea
Touching a mahram is permissible when free of desire; but when buying a slave, looking may be allowed with desire but touching is not.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a key Hanafi principle: the permissibility of touching follows the permissibility of looking. However, the rule is nuanced, especially when purchasing a slave. The text provides a hadith proof and additional guidelines on displaying a mature female slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State the principle: whatever is permissible to look at (from mahram or non-mahram) is permissible to touch, subject to conditions.
The rule applies to mahram relatives and, within limits, to non-mahram women.
2
Note the proof from the Prophetic example
Recall the hadith where the Prophet kissed Fatima's head without desire, establishing permissibility of touching mahram when free from desire.
The hadith is cited from Hashiyat al-Jalabi.
3
Distinguish the buying scenario
When buying a female slave, looking is allowed even with desire due to necessity, but touching is not allowed if desire is present because it is considered enjoyment.
The condition of no desire for touch remains, even in the buying context.
4
Apply the condition of no desire
Outside of buying, both looking and touching are allowed only when there is no desire. This applies to both mahram and non-mahram with their respective limits.
For non-mahram women, looking is limited to the face and hands, and touching is generally prohibited except with a barrier or medical necessity.
5
Recognize the rule on displaying a slave
A female slave who has reached puberty should not be displayed for sale in a single garment (izar) as it may be revealing.
This ensures modesty and aligns with general norms of covering.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما حل نظراً منهما ، حل مساً ، وله مس ذلك إن أراد شراءها" وإن
خاف شهوته، وأمةٌ بلغت لا تعرض في إزار واحد، ومن الأجنبية إلى وجهها [٢] قوله : حل مساً...الخ ; ومن أدلة جواز مس المحارم خاصة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان
يقبل رأس فاطمة له ، ويقول : «أجد منها ريح الجنة»، وكان لا على شهوة قطعاً،
فيجوز المس. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي
(۳) قوله : إن أراد شراءها...الخ ; قال مشايخنا : يباح النظر في حالة الشراء، وإن
اشتهى للضرورة، ولا يباح المس إذا اشتهى ، أو كان أكبر رأيه ذلك ; لأنه نوع استمتاع، وفي غير حالة الشراء يباحُ النظرُ والمس لشرط عدم الشهوة. كذا في «هداية»(۳). (٤) قوله : لا تعرض في إزار واحد ; أي لا تعرض على البيع في إزار : أي في ثوب
And whatever is permissible to look at from them, it is permissible to touch. And he may touch that if he intends to buy her, even if he fears desire. And a female slave who has reached puberty should not be displayed in a single garment. And from the non-mahram woman: to her face. ... The proof for permissibility of touching mahram specifically is that the Prophet would kiss the head of Fatima and say 'I smell the scent of Paradise from her,' and that was without any desire, so touching is permissible. ... The scholars say: Looking is permissible in the state of buying even if he feels desire due to necessity, but touching is not permissible if he feels desire or if his dominant opinion is that, because it is a form of enjoyment. Outside of buying, looking and touching are permissible subject to condition of no desire.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage extends the permissibility of looking to touching: any body part that is permissible to look at is also permissible to touch. The proof is the Prophetic example of kissing his daughter's head without desire. However, there are special rules for a buyer of a slave: when buying, looking is allowed even with desire due to need, but touching is not allowed if desire is present because it is considered enjoyment. Outside of buying, both looking and touching require absence of desire. Additionally, a female slave who has reached puberty should not be displayed for sale in a single garment (i.e., thin or revealing clothing), as that would be inappropriate. The passage ends with the start of the ruling on non-mahram women: looking is allowed only to the face, which will be detailed later.

Key Terms
مس (mass): Touching; physical contact with the skin.
شراء (shira'): Purchase; the intention to buy a slave grants concession for looking with desire but not for touching with desire.
إزار (izar): A waist-wrapper or single garment; the text forbids displaying a mature slave for sale in such minimal covering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When buying a female slave, what is the ruling on touching if the buyer feels desire?
A) Permissible because buying allows it
B) Not permissible because it is enjoyment
C) Permissible only if the slave is mahram
D) Not permissible regardless of desire

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, touching is permissible where looking is allowed, provided there is no desire; during the purchase of a slave, looking is allowed even with desire, but touching is not.

Istibra' for a Slave Woman Bought from a Child's Property
Expansion on the ruling of istibra' when buying from a minor's property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istibra' when buying a slave woman from a child's property, identify the dalil (hadith of Awtas), explain the wisdom behind the ruling, and analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute concerning istibra' for a fornicatress.

Core Idea
Istibra' is wajib upon acquisition of a slave woman, regardless of the seller's status or the woman's condition.

This passage expands on the ruling of istibra' (a waiting period to ensure the womb is empty) when a slave woman is purchased from the property of a minor. The author explains that istibra' is obligatory before intercourse, cites the prophetic hadith as proof, and discusses objections and an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether istibra' is required when the womb's occupation is from fornication. By studying this, you will understand how the Hanafi school derives rulings from textual evidence and how different jurists within the school may differ based on the underlying wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on the act of purchasing a slave woman from a child's property. The ruling is that it is unlawful (haram) for the buyer to have intercourse with her until he performs istibra'.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites the hadith of the captives of Awtas: 'Do not have intercourse with pregnant women until they deliver, nor with non-pregnant women until they have a menstrual period.' This is the primary proof.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala) according to Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition (لا توطؤوا) indicates impermissibility of intercourse without istibra'. The command implies obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul. The ruling is attached to the apparent cause of new ownership (istihdath al-milk), not to the actual possibility of pregnancy.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the womb is occupied from zina, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no istibra' is needed, while Imam Muhammad says it is required. The commentator defends the majority view by noting that the occupation may not be from zina but from a lawful husband.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text does not give explicit tarjih, but the general hadith and the principle of protecting lineage support the requirement of istibra' in all cases of new ownership. The mufta bihi position is to require istibra' regardless.

كتاب الكراهية / الأكل والشرب واللبس وغيرها
(أو من مال صبي) : أي إن كانت الأمة من مال صبي، (حَرُمَ عليه وطؤها ودواعيه حتى يستبرئ بحيضة فيمن تحيض، وبشهر في ذوات شهر، وبوضع الحمل في الحامل)، فإنَّ الحكمة في الاستبراء تعرف براءة الرحم صيانة للماء المحترم عن الاختلاط، وذلك عند أبي حنيفة له : حقيقة الشغل بماء محرم، أو توهم الشغل بماء محترم، لكنَّهُ أمر خفي، فأدير الحكم على أمر ظاهر، وهو استحداث الملك "، وإن كان عدم وطء المولى معلوماً كما في الأمور التي عدها، وهي قوله : ولو بكراً... إلى آخره. فإن الحكمة تراعى في الجنس لا في كلِّ فرد، ولكن يرد عليه " أن الحكمة لا تراعى في كل فرد، لكن تراعى في الأنواع المضبوطة، فإن كانت الأمة بكراً أو مشريَّةٌ ممن لا يثبت نسب ولدها منه، وهو أن يكون الولد ثابت النسب ينبغي أن لا يجب؛ لأنَّ عدم الشغل بالماء المحترم متيقن في هذه الأنواع. والجواب : أنه إنما يثبتُ بالنَّص لقولهِ الله في سبايا أوطاس : «ألا لا توطؤوا الحبالي حتى يضعن حملهن، ولا الحيالي حتى يستبرئن بحيضة» (١). يؤيده قول قاضي خان الله في كتاب الحظر والإباحة: إذا جومعت البكر في ما دون الفرج، فدخل المني فرجها فحبلت، وقد دنا أوان ولادتها، ينبغي أن يزال بكارتها ببيضة أو بحرفِ درهم؛ لأن خروج الولد دون ذلك لا يكون.
قيل : يرد عليه إذا كان الشغلُ من غير المولى كان من الزنا، ونكاح المزنية ووطئها جائز بلا استبراء عندهما، خلافاً لمحمد ه، فكيف يوجب توهم الشغل من الزنا الاستبراء.
ويمكن دفعه بالشغل إذا كان من غير المولى لا يجب كونه من الزنا؛ لجواز أن يكون زوجها بآخر، كما سيأتي هذا من كلام الأستاذ في «غرره».
وقوله : كما سيأتي إشارة إلى ما سنذكره في بيان معنى قول الشارح : وهو أن يكون الولد ثابت النسب، حيث قال : بأن يكون الولد ثابت النسب من غيره إن زوج المولى أمته ثم حبلت منه طلقها، وبعد انقضاء عدتها باعها من رجل. فكان ينبغي أن لا يجب الاستبراء على المشتري؛ لأنّ الحمل ثابت النسب فلا يلزمُ اختلاط المياه واشتباه الأنساب. انتهى كلام ذلك الفاضل. ونحن نقول : ليت شعري ما معنى قوله : لأن الحمل ثابت النسب بعد قوله : وبعد انقضاء عدتها باعها من رجل مع أن أولات الأحمال أجلهن أن يضعن حملهن. قاله الجلبي
Book of Disliked Matters / Eating, Drinking, Clothing, etc. (Or from the property of a child): That is, if the slave woman is from the property of a child, it is unlawful for him to have intercourse with her and its causes until he performs istibra' with one menstrual period for those who menstruate, and with one month for those who have a monthly period, and with delivery of the pregnancy for the pregnant one. The wisdom in istibra' is to know the emptiness of the womb, protecting the respected fluid (semen) from mixing. This, according to Abu Hanifa, is due to actual occupation with prohibited fluid or the supposition of occupation with respected fluid, but it is a hidden matter, so the ruling is attached to an apparent matter, which is the acquisition of ownership. Even if the master's non-intercourse is known, as in the matters he counted, which is his statement: 'even if she is a virgin...' etc. For the wisdom is considered in the species, not in every individual. But it is objected: that wisdom is not considered in every individual, but it is considered in the restricted types. If the slave woman is a virgin or purchased from someone whose lineage from her is not established—that is, the child's lineage is established—it should not be obligatory, because the absence of occupation with respected fluid is certain in these types. The answer: It is established by the text of his statement, peace be upon him, regarding the captives of Awtas: 'Do not have intercourse with pregnant women until they deliver, nor with non-pregnant women until they have a menstrual period.' This is supported by the statement of Qadi Khan in the book of Prohibitions and Permissibilities: If a virgin is copulated with in the front part, and semen enters her vagina and she becomes pregnant, and the time of her delivery approaches, it is necessary to remove her virginity with an egg or the edge of a dirham, because the child's exit without that is not possible. It is said: It is objected: if the occupation is from someone other than the master, it is from fornication, and marriage of a fornicatress and intercourse with her is permissible without istibra' according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa), contrary to Muhammad. So how does the supposition of occupation from fornication necessitate istibra'? And it can be refuted: occupation, if from other than the master, does not necessarily have to be from fornication, because it is possible that someone married her, as will come later from the statement of the teacher in his 'Ghurar'. And his statement: 'as will come later' is an indication to what we will mention in explaining the meaning of the commentator's statement: 'that is, the child's lineage is established' where he said: 'that is, the child's lineage is established from another, if the master married his slave woman, then she became pregnant from him, then he divorced her, and after the completion of her iddah he sold her to a man. Then istibra' should not be obligatory upon the buyer, because the pregnancy's lineage is established, so there is no mixing of waters or confusion of lineages.' End of the statement of that virtuous one. And we say: Would that I knew what is the meaning of his statement 'because the pregnancy's lineage is established' after his statement 'and after the completion of her iddah he sold her to a man' when the term of pregnant women is that they deliver? Al-Jalabi said this.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of istibra' (a waiting period to ensure the womb is empty) when a slave woman is purchased from the property of a child (sabi). The matn states that it is unlawful for the buyer to have intercourse with her until she undergoes istibra' – one menstrual cycle for those who menstruate, one month for those who have monthly periods (like young girls or old women), or until delivery for pregnant women. The commentator explains the wisdom: to protect the respected fluid (semen) from mixing and confusion of lineages. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the basis is either actual occupation of the womb with sperm (from a lawful or unlawful source) or the possibility of such occupation. Since the actual state of the womb is hidden, the ruling is attached to an apparent cause: the acquisition of new ownership. Even if the seller did not have intercourse, the ruling applies in general because wisdom is considered in the species, not in every individual case. An objection is raised: if the woman is a virgin or purchased from someone whose child's lineage is established (so no possibility of mixing), then istibra' should not be required. The answer is that the ruling is established by the hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding the captives of Awtas, which is a general command. This is supported by a ruling from Qadi Khan about a virgin who becomes pregnant from semen entering the front part, where removing the hymen is necessary for delivery. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is discussed: if the occupation of the womb is from someone other than the master (e.g., from zina), according to Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa, intercourse with a fornicatress is permissible without istibra', contrary to Imam Muhammad. So how can the mere supposition of occupation from zina necessitate istibra'? The response is that the occupation from someone else is not necessarily from zina; it could be from a lawful marriage (e.g., the master married her to someone else, then she was sold after divorce). In that case, istibra' is required due to the pregnancy from the lawful husband. The commentator further criticizes a previous scholar's statement about a case where a slave woman is pregnant from her husband (the master) and then sold after divorce, arguing that istibra' is still required because the pregnancy's lineage is established, but the command for pregnant women is to deliver before intercourse. This shows the complexity of the rulings.

Key Terms
استبراء (istibra'): A waiting period for a female slave after purchase to ensure the womb is empty of pregnancy, by one menstrual cycle, one month, or delivery.
ماء محترم (ma' muhtaram): Respected fluid, i.e., semen from a lawful union that could establish lineage.
استحداث الملك (istihdath al-milk): Acquisition of new ownership, which is the apparent cause that triggers the obligation of istibra'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the primary wisdom behind the obligation of istibra'?
A) To ensure the seller's rights are protected
B) To protect the respected fluid (semen) from mixing and avoid confusion of lineages
C) To allow the buyer to test the slave's fertility
D) To complete the sale contract

Key Takeaway
Istibra' is wajib upon new ownership of a slave woman to protect lineage, based on the hadith of Awtas, and it is required even if the seller is a child or the womb appears empty.

The Ruling on Istibra' When the Child's Lineage is Known: Abu Yusuf vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on istibra' when child lineage known from another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn on istibra' and apply the relied-upon Hanafi ruling to cases where a slave woman's child lineage is known from another.

Core Idea
Istibra' remains wajib even when the child's lineage is known from another, due to the general hadith of Awtas and the principle of blocking possibility of lineage mixing.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute regarding istibra' for a purchased slave woman. When the child's lineage is already established from someone other than the seller, Abu Yusuf argued that istibra' is unnecessary because the womb is certainly free of the seller's water. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) maintained that istibra' is still required, citing the general ruling of the Prophet on the captives of Awtas and the possibility that the womb could be occupied from other sources. We will explore the proofs and understand why the latter view is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
The passage asks whether istibra' is obligatory for a buyer when the purchased slave woman's child has a known lineage from someone other than the seller.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's Position
Abu Yusuf holds that istibra' is not wajib because the womb is certain to be empty of the seller's water, analogizing to a divorced woman before consummation who has no iddah.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's Position
The Sahibayn argue that even if the seller's water is not present, there is still a possibility (tawahhum) that the womb is occupied from other sources, such as accidental impregnation in a bathhouse.
4
Analyze the Dalil
The primary proof is the hadith of Awtas which generally prohibits intercourse with captives without istibra', and this is applied by qiyas to all purchased slave women.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The text shows that the Sahibayn's view is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) due to the general nature of the proof and the need to prevent lineage confusion.

Good guidance for the later parts of the commentary on Al-Wiqayah. A purchased [slave] from whom the lineage of her child is not established – meaning that the child's lineage is established – it should not be obligatory [to wait for istibra']; because certainty of the absence of occupation with respected water [semen] is definite in these types. Said al-Jalabi: [1] His statement: 'And that is... etc.' Many observers hesitated in this place regarding the reference of this pronoun, and they struggled with what they knew. What I have is that its reference is the lack of establishment derived from his statement: 'it is not established', and the preposition 'ba' is implied in 'that is'. And the phrase 'from other than the seller' after his statement 'the child's lineage is established' so the meaning is: it (i.e., the lack of establishment of lineage from the seller) is that the child's lineage is established from other than the seller. Said al-Jalabi: [1] His statement: 'For the captives... etc.' For the captives inevitably include a virgin, and a captured woman, and the like, yet the Prophet ruled a general ruling, not specified to the wisdom, just as he [the Prophet] clarified the wisdom in the prohibition of wine with His statement: 'Indeed, Satan desires to cause...' the verse, so no one can say 'I drink it such that enmity does not occur, nor does it prevent me from prayer.' So when the benefit is predominant in its prohibition, the Law prohibits generally; because in specification there is obvious confusion, and people's daring such that the wisdom is lost. So when the ruling is established regarding captives in general, it is established for all other causes of ownership by analogy, for the cause is known, and this is further supported by consensus. The pronoun, so they struggled... and what I have is that its reference is the lack of establishment derived from his statement: 'it is not established', and the preposition 'ba' is implied in 'that is'. And the phrase 'from other than the seller' after his statement 'the child's lineage is established' so the meaning is: it (i.e., the lack of establishment of lineage from the seller) is that the child's lineage is established from other than the seller. Said al-Jalabi: [1] His statement: 'For the captives... etc.' And from Abu Yusuf: It is not obligatory in these cases due to certainty of the emptiness of her womb from the seller's water, just as a woman divorced before consummation does not observe a waiting period for this same reason. And the two said: Yes, the occupation is not established here, but it is not free from a type of assumption of occupation, even if it is from other than that also, for the womb of a virgin may be occupied with semen while virginity remains, such as him snatching it in the bathhouse. Said al-Jalabi: (3). [2] His statement: 'And people's daring' means their daring and boldness to have intercourse without istibra', even if it is from places of assuming occupation, and this is the meaning of his statement: 'such that the wisdom is lost'.)
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on istibra' (waiting period to ensure the womb is empty) for a purchased slave woman. The author begins by presenting a case: when a slave woman is purchased and her child's lineage is already established from someone other than the seller, it would seem that istibra' is not required because there is no possibility of mixing the buyer's water with that of another. However, the text clarifies that the general ruling from the Prophet (PBUH) forbidding intercourse with pregnant captives until they deliver applies universally, regardless of the specific circumstances. The hadith about the captives of Autas is cited as the primary proof. The author then supports this with a statement from Qadhi Khan about a virgin whose virginity must be removed if she is pregnant to allow delivery. A question is raised: if the pregnancy (water) came from someone other than the master, it might be from fornication, and intercourse with a fornicated woman is permissible without istibra' according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (contrary to Muhammad's other view). The response is that the water could come from a lawful husband, not necessarily fornication. The passage then engages in an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that istibra' is not required in these cases because the womb is definitely free from the seller's water, just as a divorced woman before consummation does not wait. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) respond that while the occupation is not certain, there is still a possibility of mixing, citing an example of a virgin whose womb can be occupied with semen while her virginity remains, such as by accident in a bathhouse. The commentator explains that the general ruling from the Prophet is based on a wisdom (preventing mixing of lineages), and just as the prohibition of wine is not individualized to those who claim they won't be affected, the ruling on istibra' is general to avoid chaos. The reliance is placed on the general qiyas and consensus. Thus, the relied-upon position is that istibra' is required in all cases when a slave woman is purchased, even if her child's lineage is known from another, to safeguard against any possibility of mixing.

Key Terms
استبراء (istibra'): The waiting period a buyer must observe before having intercourse with a purchased slave woman to ensure her womb is not pregnant from another man.
سبايا أوطاس (sabaya Awtas): The captives taken from the battle of Hunayn (or Hawazin) at Awtas, regarding whom the Prophet forbade intercourse until after istibra'.
ماء محترم (ma' muhtaram): Semen that is respected, i.e., from a lawful marriage or ownership, which must be accounted for in lineage.
توهم الشغل (tawahhum al-shughl): The assumption or possibility that the womb is occupied (with semen/pregnancy), which is sufficient to require istibra' even if not certain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on istibra' for a purchased slave woman whose child's lineage is established from someone other than the seller?
A) Not required because the womb is definitely empty of the seller's water.
B) Required because there is still a possibility of mixing lineages, as per the general hadith of Awtas.
C) Required only if the child is from fornication.
D) Not required if the seller was her husband and she completed her waiting period.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position requires istibra' for all purchased slave women to prevent lineage mixing, even when the child's lineage is known from another.

The Obligation of Istibra' and the Timing of Menstrual Periods
Ruling on istibra' and exceptions with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general ruling of istibra' upon purchasing a slave-girl, identify when a menstrual period does not suffice, and explain the Hanafi dispute regarding pre-possession periods.

Core Idea
Istibra' cannot be fulfilled by a period before possession — the ruling cannot precede its cause.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational ruling of istibra' — a waiting period required when a female slave changes ownership. The passage clarifies which menstrual periods count and which do not, and presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (majority) against Abu Yusuf on the sufficiency of a pre-possession period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine that istibra' is wajib (obligatory) upon purchasing a female slave. Note the exceptions: when the buyer already owned a share, or in cases of return of runaway, stolen, leased, or pawned slaves.
The ruling is based on the need to ensure the womb is empty of another man's child.
2
Locate the cause (sabab)
The cause is acquisition of ownership and possession. The ruling is attributed to the proximate cause, not distant events.
Al-A'zam and Muhammad: the ruling cannot precede its cause.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that a period before possession does not count. Abu Yusuf argues it suffices due to certainty of emptiness.
The majority view is that a period after the cause but before possession is insufficient.
4
Apply to the runaway slave case
If a slave runs within Dar al-Islam, no new ownership, so no istibra'. If she runs to Dar al-Harb, Abu Hanifa says no istibra', while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require it.
This shows the cause (new ownership) is absent in the first case, disputed in the second.
5
State the mufta bihi
The position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the relied-upon opinion; pre-possession periods do not count.
This is the more cautious view adhering to the principle that the ruling follows the cause.

بعد سبب من أسباب الملك قبل القبض، ولا ولادة كذلك، وتجب في شراء أمةٍ إلا شقصاً هو له؛ لأنَّ الملك ثم له، والحكم يضاف إلى العلة القريبة، لا عند عود الآبقة، ورد المغصوبة، والمستأجرة، وفك المرهونة؛ لأنه لم يوجد استحداث الملك. وهذا عند الأعظم ومحمد خلافاً لأبي يوسف له، فإن عنده: تكفي تلك الحيضة للتيقن بفراغ الرحم، كما لو طلقها قبل الدخول لا يجب العدة كذلك. ولهما: إن السبب استحداث الملك واليد والحكم لا يسبق السبب، وكذا لم تكف بالاستبراء الحاصل قبل الإجازة في بيع الفضولي، وإن كانت في يد المشتري ولا بالحاصل بعد القبض في الشراء الفاسد قبل أن يشتريها شراء صحيحاً لما قلنا. وتجب في شراء أمة...الخ؛ لأنّ السبب محل الوطء، وهو ملك الرقبة، قد تم؛ أي بعدما ملك جميع رقبتها، وملك بعض الرقبة بمنزلة بعض العلة، وثبوت الحكم عند كمال العلة. لا عند عود الآبقة...الخ؛ لانعدام السبب، وهو استحداث الملك واليد، وهو سبب متعين، فأدير الحكم عليه وجوداً وعدماً، الآبقة التي أبقت في دار الإسلام، ثم رجعت إلى مولاها لا يجب الاستبراء، فإن أبقـت إلى دارِ الحرب ثم عادت إليه بوجه من الوجوه فكذلك عند الأعظم؛ لأنهم لا يملكونها، وعندهما: يجب الاستبراء لأنهم ملكوها. ورخص حيلة إسقاط الاستبراء عند أبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد، وأخذ بالأول إن علم عدم وطء بائعها في ذلك الطهر، وبالثاني إن قربها: وهي إن لم تكن تحتَهُ حرَّةٌ أن ينكحها، ثم يشتريها، إذ بالنكاح لا يجب الاستبراء، ثم إذا اشترى زوجته لا يجب أيضاً. وقد عطف الزيلعي: على يشتريها لفظ: يقبضها، فقال: هكذا ذكره صاحب «الهداية»، ثم قال: «وهذا لا يفيد إذا كان القبض بعد الشراء؛ لأنه بالشراء ينفسخ النكاح، فيجب الاستبراء بالقبض بحكم الشراء، وإنما يفيد لو كان القبض قبل الشراء، كيلا يوجد القبض بحكم الشراء بعد فساد النكاح. قال ظهير الدين عندي أن يشترط الدخولُ قبل الشراء؛ لأن ملك النكاح يفسد عند الشراء سابقاً على الشراء ضرورة أن ملك النكاح لا يجامع ملك اليمين، فلم تكن عند الشراء منكوحةً ولا معتدّةً، بخلاف ما إذا دخل بها قبل الشراء؛ لأنها تبقى معتدة منه بعد فساد النكاح، فلا يلزمه الاستبراء به. أقول: لو قال بدل صاحب «الهداية»: صاحب «الكافي» لكان صواباً؛ لأن لفظ: يقبضها لم يقع في «الهداية»، بل فيه، فليتأمل. إذ بالنكاح لا يجب الاستبراء؛ لأنه به يثبت له الفرش عليها، فلمّا اشتراها وهي على فراشه، وقيامُ الفرش له عليها دليل شرعي على تعيين فراغ رحمها من ماء الغير.
A menstrual period occurring during her ownership does not suffice, nor one before possession, nor a childbirth likewise. Istibra' is obligatory when buying a slave-girl except for a share that is already his [the buyer's] upon the return of a runaway slave, the return of a stolen slave, a leased slave, or the release of a pawned slave. 'Nor a menstrual period occurring during her ownership' means nor the period that occurred after a cause of ownership but before possession; nor a childbirth likewise. Istibra' is obligatory when buying a slave-girl except for a share that is already his, because ownership is established then, and the ruling is attributed to the proximate cause. Not upon the return of a runaway slave, the return of a stolen slave, a leased slave, or the release of a pawned slave, because there is no new acquisition of ownership. This [ruling] is according to al-A'zam [Abu Hanifa] and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf, for according to him that period suffices due to certainty of emptiness of the womb, just as if he divorces her before consummation, 'idda is not obligatory likewise. Their proof: The cause is acquisition of ownership and possession, and the ruling cannot precede the cause. Likewise, istibra' obtained before ratification in a fuduli sale does not suffice even if she is in the buyer's hand, nor that obtained after possession in a corrupt purchase before he buys her with a valid purchase, as we said. 'Istibra' is obligatory when buying a slave-girl...' because the cause is the locus of intercourse, which is full ownership of her person; i.e., after he owns all of her person, and owning part of her person is like part of the cause, and the ruling is established upon completion of the cause. 'Not upon the return of a runaway...' because the cause is absent, i.e., acquisition of ownership and possession, and this is the specific cause, so the ruling revolves around it in existence and non-existence. A runaway who ran away within Dar al-Islam and then returned to her master: istibra' is not obligatory. If she ran away to Dar al-Harb and then returned to him by any means, likewise according to al-A'zam because they [the enemy] do not own her. According to them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], istibra' is obligatory because they owned her. A legal stratagem to drop istibra' is permitted according to Abu Yusuf contrary to Muhammad. He [Abu Yusuf] takes the first [opinion] if he knows that the seller did not have intercourse with her in that purity period, and the second if he has approached her: that if he has no free wife, he should marry her, then buy her, for istibra' is not obligatory due to marriage, and when he buys his wife, istibra' is also not obligatory. Al-Zayla'i added: to 'yushtariha' the word 'yaqbiduha'. He said: Thus mentioned by the author of al-Hidayah, then he said: 'This is not beneficial if possession occurs after purchase, because by the purchase the marriage is dissolved, so istibra' becomes obligatory upon possession by virtue of the purchase. It is only beneficial if possession occurs before purchase, so that possession does not occur by virtue of the purchase after dissolution of the marriage. Zahir al-Din said: In my view, he should stipulate consummation before purchase, because the ownership of marriage is corrupted by the purchase prior to the purchase, necessarily because the ownership of marriage does not coexist with ownership by right hand. So she is not a wife nor in 'idda at the time of purchase, unlike if he consummated before purchase, because she remains in 'idda from him after dissolution of the marriage, so he is not obliged to perform istibra' due to it.' I say: If he had said 'the author of al-Kafi' instead of 'the author of al-Hidayah', it would be correct, because the word 'yaqbiduha' does not occur in al-Hidayah but in it, so let it be considered. 'Because istibra' is not obligatory due to marriage, for by it he is established on her bedding, so when he buys her while she is on his bedding, his bedding on her is a legal proof indicating the emptiness of her womb from another's fluid.'
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of istibra' (waiting for one menstrual period) when purchasing a female slave. It begins by stating that a menstrual period that occurred before the buyer possessed the slave does not count for istibra', nor does childbirth before possession. Istibra' is required upon buying a slave girl, except when the buyer already owned a share in her (in which case the full ownership is completed but the cause is partial). The commentary explains that the ruling is attached to the proximate cause, namely the acquisition of ownership and possession. Exceptions are made for cases where no new ownership is acquired: returning a runaway slave, getting back a stolen or leased slave, or redeeming a pawned one. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that a period before possession is insufficient, while Abu Yusuf deems it sufficient for certainty of emptiness. The proof for the majority is that the ruling cannot precede its cause. The text then discusses the case of a runaway: if she ran within Dar al-Islam, no istibra'; if she ran to Dar al-Harb, Abu Hanifa still says no because the enemy does not own her, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require istibra' because the enemy did own her. Finally, a legal stratagem (hila) permitted by Abu Yusuf (but rejected by Muhammad) is described: if the buyer has no free wife, he can marry the slave girl first, then buy her. Since marriage does not require istibra', and buying one's own wife also does not, istibra' is avoided. However, the commentary clarifies a condition: possession must occur before purchase, otherwise the purchase dissolves the marriage and istibra' becomes obligatory again. Some scholars also require consummation before purchase.

Key Terms
الاستبراء (al-istibra'): A waiting period of one menstrual cycle to ensure the womb is empty of another man's child, required when a female slave changes ownership.
الآبقة (al-ābiqa): A female runaway slave who flees from her master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, why does a menstrual period before taking possession not count for istibra'?
A) Because the seller may have lied about the period.
B) Because the ruling cannot precede its cause.
C) Because istibra' is only for the first period after purchase.
D) Because the slave may have been pregnant during the period.

Key Takeaway
Istibra' is wajib upon purchasing a slave-girl, and a menstrual period before possession does not fulfill it according to the majority Hanafi view.

The Legal Stratagem to Avoid Istibra'
Legal stratagem to drop istibra'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the legal stratagem (hila) permitted by Abu Yusuf to avoid istibra' when buying a slave-girl, and identify the conditions required for its validity.

Core Idea
Marrying then buying a slave-girl can bypass istibra', but only if possession occurs before purchase.

This lesson explores a legal stratagem (hila) that Abu Yusuf permits to avoid istibra' when purchasing a female slave. The stratagem involves marrying the slave first, then buying her. Since marriage does not require istibra', and buying one's own wife also does not, istibra' is avoided. However, the passage clarifies strict conditions, including the timing of possession and the necessity of consummation according to some scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stratagem
The hila: If the buyer has no free wife, he can marry the slave-girl, then buy her. Istibra' is not required in marriage, and buying one's wife also does not require istibra'.
This is permitted by Abu Yusuf but rejected by Muhammad.
2
Understand the condition of possession
Al-Zayla'i clarifies that possession must occur before purchase; otherwise, the purchase dissolves the marriage and istibra' becomes obligatory again.
If possession is after purchase, the marriage is nullified and the purchase triggers istibra'.
3
Analyze the requirement of consummation
Zahir al-Din adds that consummation before purchase is necessary. Without consummation, the marriage is dissolved instantly, so she is not a wife or in 'idda, and istibra' would be required.
Consummation ensures she remains in 'idda after dissolution, preventing the need for istibra'.
4
State the mufta bihi on the hila
The hila is permitted by Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad opposes it. The text does not explicitly give tarjih, but the detailed conditions suggest it is recognized in practice.
Caution is recommended; the majority view may prefer to avoid the hila.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of istibra' (waiting for one menstrual period) when purchasing a female slave. It begins by stating that a menstrual period that occurred before the buyer possessed the slave does not count for istibra', nor does childbirth before possession. Istibra' is required upon buying a slave girl, except when the buyer already owned a share in her (in which case the full ownership is completed but the cause is partial). The commentary explains that the ruling is attached to the proximate cause, namely the acquisition of ownership and possession. Exceptions are made for cases where no new ownership is acquired: returning a runaway slave, getting back a stolen or leased slave, or redeeming a pawned one. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that a period before possession is insufficient, while Abu Yusuf deems it sufficient for certainty of emptiness. The proof for the majority is that the ruling cannot precede its cause. The text then discusses the case of a runaway: if she ran within Dar al-Islam, no istibra'; if she ran to Dar al-Harb, Abu Hanifa still says no because the enemy does not own her, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require istibra' because the enemy did own her. Finally, a legal stratagem (hila) permitted by Abu Yusuf (but rejected by Muhammad) is described: if the buyer has no free wife, he can marry the slave girl first, then buy her. Since marriage does not require istibra', and buying one's own wife also does not, istibra' is avoided. However, the commentary clarifies a condition: possession must occur before purchase, otherwise the purchase dissolves the marriage and istibra' becomes obligatory again. Some scholars also require consummation before purchase.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-ḥīla): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible result while avoiding a prohibition or obligation, here to avoid istibra'.
القبض (al-qabd): Possession; taking physical control of the purchased item. In the context of the hila, possession must occur before purchase to avoid triggering istibra'.
الدخول (al-dukhl): Consummation of marriage; sexual intercourse. In the hila, consummation before purchase is required to establish 'idda and avoid istibra'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Zayla'i, why must possession occur before purchase in the stratagem?
A) Because the seller may not deliver the slave otherwise.
B) Because purchase dissolves the marriage, so possession after purchase would trigger istibra'.
C) Because possession after purchase is invalid in a sale.
D) Because the marriage needs to be established before any transaction.

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf permits a hila to avoid istibra' by marrying then buying, but only if possession and consummation occur before purchase.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced

Legal Stratagems to Avoid Istibra' in Slave Purchase
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hiyal to avoid istibra'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which Abu Yusuf permits a hila to avoid istibra' and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
A lawful stratagem can avoid istibra' if marriage precedes purchase, creating a legal bed that proves emptiness of womb.

Istibra' is a waiting period to ensure a slave woman's womb is clear of another man's child. The Hanafi school, known for its pragmatic use of legal stratagems (hiyal), debated whether a purchaser could avoid istibra' by marrying the slave woman before purchase. This lesson examines the two main positions—Abu Yusuf's permissibility and Muhammad al-Shaybani's prohibition—and the precise conditions for the stratagem to work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The stratagem differs depending on whether the purchaser has a free wife. First scenario: no free wife. Second: has a free wife. Also a third method using kitabah.
Each scenario has its own conditions and limitations.
2
Understand the first hila (no free wife)
The purchaser marries the slave woman, then purchases her. Marriage establishes a legal bed (frash), which is proof of empty womb, so no istibra' is needed. Abu Yusuf permits; Muhammad disagrees.
The author follows Abu Yusuf if no intercourse by seller in that purity period.
3
Analyze the second hila (with free wife)
The seller marries the slave to a trusted man before the purchaser buys; then the husband divorces her. Since the purchaser buys a married woman, intercourse is not permissible, so no istibra'. After divorce before consummation, she becomes lawful.
The trusted man must be reliable; an alternate is to give the purchaser authority to divorce.
4
Evaluate the kitabah stratagem
The master makes a kitabah contract, then the slave declares inability to pay, reverting to slavery. Since istibra' was dropped by kitabah, it does not return. This is preferred for rulers.
This method avoids the complications of marriage.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is the permissibility of these stratagems under the specified conditions, especially the kitabah method for rulers, as noted by al-Jalabi and Qadi Khan.
The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is resolved in favor of Abu Yusuf in practice.

And he permitted a stratagem for dropping istibra' according to Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad. And he adopted the first if he knows that the seller did not have intercourse with her in that period of purity, and the second if she approached him: that if the purchaser does not have a free wife under him, he should marry her, then buy her. For by marriage, istibra' is not required. Then when he buys his wife, it is also not required, because ownership did not arise. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), istibra' is required because they (the couple) owned her. Thus in 'Dhakhirat al-'Uqba' by al-Jalabi. His saying: 'that he marry her then buy her'; and al-Zayla'i appended to 'buy her' the word 'take possession of her'. He said: 'Thus mentioned by the author of al-Hidayah, then he said: This is not beneficial if possession occurs after purchase, because by purchase the marriage is annulled, so istibra' becomes obligatory by possession due to the purchase. It is only beneficial if possession occurs before purchase, so that possession does not occur by virtue of purchase after annulment of marriage. Zahir al-Din said: In my view, it is necessary to stipulate that intercourse occurs before purchase, because the right of marriage is corrupted at the purchase, prior to the purchase, necessarily because the right of marriage and the right of ownership cannot coexist. So at the time of purchase, she is neither a wife nor in waiting period. Contrary to if he had intercourse with her before purchase, because she remains in waiting period from him after annulment of marriage, so istibra' is not required of him. Qadi Khan mentioned it.' End of his statement. I say: If instead of 'author of al-Hidayah' he had said 'author of al-Kafi', it would be correct, because the word 'possess it' does not occur in al-Hidayah, but rather in it (al-Kafi). So let it be considered. Said by my brother Jalabi. His statement: 'For by marriage, istibra' is not required' because by it he establishes the bed (sexual right) over her. When he bought her while she was on his bed, the existence of the bed for him over her is a legal proof of the emptiness of her womb from another's fluid. And if she (the slave) is that (i.e., under him, he has a free wife), then the stratagem is that the seller marry her before the purchase, or the purchaser before his possession, to a trusted person, then he buys and takes possession, then the husband divorces. That is, if he has a free wife, then the stratagem is that the seller marry her before the purchase of the purchaser to a reliable man who will divorce her, then the purchaser buys, then the husband divorces. For then istibra' is not required because he bought the wife of another, and it is not permissible to have intercourse with her, so no istibra'. When the husband divorces her before intercourse, she becomes permissible to the purchaser, and at that time no new ownership has arisen, so no istibra'. Or the purchaser marries her before possession, then she becomes permissible to him, not renewed by owning the right of ownership (the body), because she was lawful to him before by marriage. And this is the meaning of the commentator's statement: 'then when he buys his wife, it is also not required.' Said by al-Jalabi. His statement: 'a reliable man who will divorce her' because if he is not trustworthy, it is possible that he will not divorce her, and that would be a trick against him, not for him. And the stratagem for executing this stratagem is that the purchaser give her in marriage on condition that her affair is in his hand, he divorces her whenever he wishes. Said by al-Jalabi. His statement: 'or the purchaser marries her before possession... etc.'; and if he takes possession, he sells her to another, then does what was mentioned, it is dropped. Thus in 'al-Tashil'. Qadi Khan said: Except that in this there is a type of doubt, for according to Abu Yusuf in one of the two narrations, according to Muhammad, when he buys her, istibra' is obligatory except the obligation is confirmed by possession. And marriage after purchase does not drop it, because istibra' becomes obligatory by the contract itself, unless she menstruates at the purchaser by one menstruation before the divorce. Then istibra' is not required according to their statement. And it was said that to drop it, the master must make her a kitabah (contract of manumission) and then order her to become incapable (of paying) in a gentle and kind way. When she declares herself incapable, she becomes a slave-girl, and no istibra' because it was dropped by the kitabah first, and what is dropped does not return. And this method is most suitable for rulers and sultans. Said by al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning legal stratagems (hiyal) to avoid the obligation of istibra' when purchasing a female slave (umm walad or potential concubine). Istibra' is a waiting period to ensure the womb is empty of another man's child. The text presents two scenarios based on the purchaser's marital status.

First, if the purchaser has no free wife (i.e., he is unmarried), the stratagem is to marry the slave woman first and then purchase her. By marriage, the woman becomes his wife, and istibra' is not required during marriage. When he subsequently purchases her, no new ownership is created because she is already his wife; the marriage contract prevents the need for istibra'. This is the position of Abu Yusuf, while Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees, holding that istibra' is required because ownership does occur. The author follows Abu Yusuf's view (first view) if it is known that the seller did not have intercourse with her in that purity period, and Muhammad's view (second view) if she is close (meaning likely pregnant).

Second, if the purchaser has a free wife (thus cannot marry another while she is married), the stratagem is different: the seller marries the slave woman to a trusted man before the purchase. Then the purchaser buys her, and the trusted husband divorces her before consummation. Since the purchaser bought a woman who was another's wife, istibra' is not required because intercourse is not permissible. After divorce (before consummation), she becomes lawful to the purchaser. Alternatively, the purchaser can marry her before taking possession, then buy her; but here possession must precede marriage to avoid annulment of marriage by purchase. The commentator al-Zayla'i clarifies that the wording of al-Hidayah is actually from al-Kafi. Qadi Khan adds that the best hila is to stipulate that the trusted man has full authority to divorce, ensuring the stratagem works.

Finally, the passage discusses another option: using a kitabah contract. The master writes a contract of manumission with the slave, then urges her to declare inability to pay. She becomes a slave again, and since istibra' was already dropped by the kitabah, it does not return. This is considered most suitable for rulers.

The text shows the Hanafi school's pragmatism in allowing lawful stratagems to avoid hardship, while also preserving the principle of preventing mixing of lineages.

Key Terms
استبراء (istibra'): A waiting period of one menstrual cycle for a slave woman that the purchaser must observe before intercourse to ensure the womb is empty of another man's child.
حيلة (hila): A lawful stratagem to achieve a permissible result while avoiding an obligation or prohibition, typically by altering the sequence of legal actions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the first hila (marrying before purchase) when the purchaser has no free wife?
A) It is always valid according to both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
B) It is valid only if the purchaser has intercourse with her before purchase.
C) It is valid if the purchaser knows the seller did not have intercourse with her in that purity period, according to Abu Yusuf.
D) It is invalid because marriage and ownership cannot coexist.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits certain lawful stratagems to avoid istibra' by using marriage or kitabah to establish legal proofs of empty womb, reflecting the school's pragmatic approach to legal obligations.

Selling Impure Items: Excrement, Dung, and Wine Debt
Inter-madhhab dispute with expansion and qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on selling human excrement and animal dung, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principle of benefit (intifa').

Core Idea
Impurity does not automatically preclude sale; benefit and customary valuation determine property status.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses two contentious sales: human excrement (al-'adhirah) and animal dung (al-sirqin). The Hanafi school, relying on benefit and customary practice, permits their sale when mixed or pure (for dung), while the Shafi'i school prohibits them due to inherent impurity. The passage also covers the unique rule on debt from wine sold by a disbeliever. We will unpack the dispute and the evidence, focusing on the Hanafi reliance on benefit (intifa') as the decisive factor.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds for each item: pure human excrement (makruh to sell, but benefiting is permissible), mixed human excrement (valid sale), animal dung (permissible), and debt from wine (permissible from a disbeliever).
The matn explicitly states the ruling.
2
Identify the opposing view and its basis
State al-Shafi'i's ruling: all these are invalid due to impurity. Note his reliance on the najas al-ayn principle.
The commentary quotes al-Shafi'i's proof.
3
Trace the Hanafi dalil
Understand the Hanafi proofs: benefit (intifa') makes an item property (mal), and property is saleable. The qiyas to oil mixed with impurity demonstrates the common illah of benefit.
The commentary uses the qiyas and salaf practice.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to the opponent
See how the Hanafis rebut the impurity argument: impurity does not negate benefit, and the Salaf used these items without objection.
The response focuses on customary valuation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Recognise the practical consequence: in Hanafi fiqh, these sales are valid and the price is permissible; in Shafi'i, they are void.
This difference has real-world implications for contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في البيع
وكره بيع العذرة خالصة، وصح في الصحيح مخلوطة كبيع السرقين، والانتفاع بمخلوطتها لا بخالصتها، وجاز أخذ دين على كافر من ثمن خمر باعه بخلاف المسلم.
قال الشارح: وكره بيع العذرة خالصة، وصح في الصحيح مخلوطة كبيع السرقين، والانتفاع بمخلوطتها لا بخالصتها، فإن بيع السرقين جائز عندنا، وعند الشافعي لا يجوز. وجاز أخذ دين على كافر من ثمن خمر باعه بخلاف المسلم.
وقال الشافعي: لا يجوز بيع السرقين أيضاً؛ لأنه نجس العين، فشابه العذرة الخالصة وجلد الميتة قبل الدباغ. ولنا: إنه منتفع به؛ لأنه يلقى في الأراضي لاستكثار الريع، فكان مالاً؛ لأن المال ما يتمول؛ أي يدخر لوقت الحاجة، وقد يتمول المسلمون السرقين، وانتفعوا به من غير نكير من أحد من السلف، والمال محل البيع بخلاف العذرة؛ لأنه منتفع بها مخلوطاً، ويجوز بيع المخلوط، وهو المروي عن محمد، وهو الصحيح. وكذا يجوز الانتفاع بالمخلوط لا بغير المخلوط، وفي الصحيح: المخلوط بمنزلة زيت خالطته النجاسة، فإنه يجوز بيعه ويجوز الانتفاع به بالاستصباح اتفاقاً، والعلة الجامعة كونهما منتفع بهما.
وقال في الكافي: وعن أبي حنيفة أنه لا بأس بالانتفاع بالعذرة الخالصة، وقال الشيخ: قال الزيلعي: والصحيح عند الأعظم أن الانتفاع بخالصتها يجوز.
Chapter on Sales: It is disliked to sell pure human excrement, but the correct view is that it is valid when mixed, like selling animal dung; benefiting from its mixture is permissible, not from its pure form. It is permissible to take a debt from a disbeliever for the price of wine he sold, unlike a Muslim. The commentator said: It is disliked to sell pure human excrement, but the correct view is that it is valid when mixed, like selling animal dung; benefiting from its mixture is permissible, not from its pure form, for the sale of animal dung is permissible according to us, but according to al-Shafi'i it is not permissible. It is permissible to take a debt from a disbeliever for the price of wine he sold, unlike a Muslim. Al-Shafi'i said: The sale of animal dung is also not permissible because it is impure (najas al-ayn), so it resembles pure human excrement and the skin of a dead animal before tanning. Our proof: It is beneficial because it is spread on land to increase produce, so it is property; property is that which is considered valuable, i.e., stored for times of need, and Muslims have considered animal dung valuable and benefited from it without any denial from any of the predecessors; property is a subject of sale, unlike human excrement because it is benefited from only when mixed, and selling it mixed is permissible, and this is the narration from Muhammad, and it is the correct view. Similarly, benefiting from the mixed is permissible, not from the unmixed; in the correct view, the mixed is like oil contaminated with impurity, for which sale is permissible and benefiting from it by burning is permissible by consensus; the common cause is that both are beneficial. In Al-Kafi: from Abu Hanifa that there is no harm in benefiting from pure human excrement; and the sheikh said: Al-Zayla'i said: The correct view according to the majority is that benefiting from its pure form is permissible.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah deals with two rulings in the chapter on sales: (1) selling human excrement (al-'adhirah) and animal dung (al-sirqin), and (2) taking a debt from a disbeliever for the price of wine. The passage type is an inter-madhhab dispute, as it explicitly contrasts the Hanafi position with that of al-Shafi'i. The matn states that selling pure ('adam) human excrement is disliked (makruh), but when mixed with something else, it is valid (sahih), like selling animal dung. Benefiting from the mixed is permissible, not the pure. Then it states that taking a debt from a disbeliever for wine he sold is permissible, unlike a Muslim. The commentary expands: For animal dung, the Hanafis say it is permissible to sell because it is beneficial (as fertilizer), making it property (mal) which can be sold. Al-Shafi'i argues it is impure (najas al-ayn) and thus not sellable, comparing it to pure excrement and dead animal skin. The Hanafi response: the dung is beneficial and commonly used by Muslims without objection from earlier scholars, so it is property and its sale is allowed. The pure human excrement is not beneficial alone, but mixed it becomes beneficial and thus sellable. This is attributed to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan (al-Shaybani) and is the correct view (al-sahih). The analogy is to oil contaminated with impurity, which is sellable and usable for burning by consensus. Regarding the debt: it is permissible to take payment from a disbeliever for wine he sold because the contract was valid for them (since they consider wine permissible), and the debt becomes a legitimate claim; whereas for a Muslim, the sale of wine is invalid, so no debt arises. This ruling is based on the principle that the laws of Islam apply to Muslims, while disbelievers are left to their own transactions in their dealings with each other.

Key Terms
عذرة (ʿadhirah): Human excrement, feces
سرقين (sirqīn): Animal dung, often used as fertilizer
خالصة (khāliṣah): Pure, unmixed form
مخلوطة (makhlūṭah): Mixed with something else (e.g., ash, soil)
دين (dayn): Debt; a financial obligation
خمر (khamr): Wine or intoxicating drink

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on selling animal dung (sirqin)?
A) It is prohibited because it is impure.
B) It is permitted because it is beneficial and considered property.
C) It is disliked (makruh) even if mixed.
D) It is only permitted when mixed with ash.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, benefit and customary valuation determine whether an impure item can be sold, overriding the impurity itself.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
