Ruling on Taking Debt from the Price of Wine
Ruling statement with intra-Hanafi nuance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking payment from the price of wine sold by a Muslim and explain the exception when a judge issues a decree without knowledge of the source.

Core Idea
The price of wine is unlawful because the sale is void, unless a judge rules unaware.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in sales (buyu): when a Muslim sells wine, the price he receives is considered unlawful (haram). Consequently, if he owes a debt, the creditor cannot take that unlawful wealth as payment. However, there is a reported nuance from Imam Muhammad that if a judge rules the debtor to pay that amount without knowing its source, the payment becomes permissible. Understanding this requires recognizing the void nature of a wine sale and the effect of a judge's decree.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A Muslim sells wine and has a debt. The question: can the debt be settled from the wine sale proceeds?
2
State the general ruling
No, because the sale of wine is void (batil), so the price is haram and cannot be taken.
The sale is void because wine is najis and not a lawful commodity in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Recognize the exception from Imam Muhammad
If the judge rules the debtor to pay that specific sum without knowing it is from wine, the payment becomes lawful for the creditor.
This is reported in al-'Inayah from Imam Muhammad.
4
Understand the basis of the exception
The judge's decree (qada') makes the wealth lawful for the recipient because the judge's ruling is binding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دين على المسلم ، فإِنَّهُ لا يؤخذُ من ثمن خمرٍ باعَهُ المسلم ؛ لأنَّ بيعَهُ باطل، فالثمن الذي أخذه حرام. [١] قوله : حرام.... الخ ؛ في «العناية»: وروي عن محمد [؟] أنه قال : هذا إذا كان القضاء والاقتضاء بالتراضي، فإن كان بقضاء القاضي بأن قضى عليه بهذا الثمن غيرُ عالم بكونه ثمن الخمر، طاب له ذلك بقضائه.
A debt owed by a Muslim, so it is not taken from the price of wine he sold, because his sale is void, so the price he took is unlawful. [1] He said: 'unlawful... etc.' In al-'Inayah: It is narrated from Muhammad that he said: This is when the claim and collection are by mutual consent; but if it was by a judge's decree such that the judge ruled against him for this price while unaware that it was the price of wine, then it becomes permissible for him by virtue of the judge's decree.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: If a Muslim sells wine and then owes a debt, the creditor cannot take payment from the price of that wine, because the sale of wine is void (batil). Consequently, the price (thaman) that the Muslim received is unlawful (haram). The commentary from al-'Inayah adds a detail reported from Imam Muhammad: This ruling applies when the debt is settled by mutual consent. However, if a judge, unaware that the price was from wine, rules that the debtor must pay that amount, then the creditor may lawfully take it by virtue of the judge's decree. This shows the importance of lawful acquisition of wealth.

Key Terms
ثمن الخمر (thaman al-khamr): The price received from selling wine, which is considered unlawful because the sale is void.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Permissible only if the debtor is a non-Muslim
D) Unlawful only if the wine was sold to a Muslim

Key Takeaway
The price of wine sold by a Muslim is haram because the sale is void, but if a judge rules without knowing the source, it becomes permissible for the creditor.

Purchase of Necessities for a Child: The Three Categories of Transactions
Ruling on the authority to purchase necessities for a minor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three categories of transactions concerning a minor and identify which guardians have authority for each category.

Core Idea
Only guardians with full authority may enter mixed-benefit contracts for a minor; purely beneficial acts are open to all.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya establishes a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh for transactions involving minors. The law classifies all acts done on behalf of a minor into three types: purely beneficial, purely harmful, and mixed. This classification determines who may validly act for the child and under what conditions. Understanding this principle is crucial for guardians, executors, and anyone dealing with a minor's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its justification
The text states that the father, grandfather, and the 'mother of the Muslims' (Islamic treasury) may purchase necessities for a minor. The justification is that without this authority, the child would be harmed, and harm is prohibited in Shariah.
The ruling is based on the general principle of preventing harm (la darar).
2
Understand the three categories of transactions
The text divides transactions into: (1) purely beneficial (naf' mahd) — like gifts and charity, which any caregiver or even the discerning minor may do; (2) purely harmful (darar mahd) — like manumission and divorce, which no one may do on the minor's behalf; (3) mixed benefit and harm (mutaraddad) — like sales and leases, where there is potential for both gain and loss.
This classification is the key to determining authority.
3
Apply the categories to sales and purchases
A sale or purchase of necessities is a mixed transaction because it involves both benefit (obtaining goods) and cost (paying money). Therefore, only the father, grandfather, and their executor may perform such transactions. Others, like uncles or brothers, cannot.
The term 'executor' includes testamentary guardians.
4
Recognize the guardians for each category
For purely beneficial acts, anyone (including strangers) who has the child in his care may act. For mixed, only the father, grandfather, and their executor have authority. For purely harmful, no one has authority.
The 'mother of the Muslims' refers to the Islamic treasury or state, which acts as a guardian in the absence of the father and grandfather.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi position is more restrictive than some other schools. For example, the Shafi'i school may allow the mother or other relatives to conduct mixed transactions under certain conditions. This ikhtilaf is recognized as legitimate.
The practical consequence is that a sale by a non-guardian is invalid in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشراء ما لا بد للطفل منه، وبيعه لاخ وعم وأم المسلمين [۲] قوله : وشراء ما لابد للطفل ...الخ ؛ يعني يجوز لهؤلاء الثلاثة أن يشتروا ويبيعوا للصغير ما لا بد منه ؛ وذلك مثل النفقة والكسوة ؛ ولأنه لو يكن لهم ذلك لتضرر الصغير، وهو ممنوع. والأصل فيه أن التصرفات مع الصغير على ثلاثة أقسام : نفع محض، فيملكه كل واحد هو في عياله، وليّاً كان أو أجنبياً كالهبة والصدقة، ويملكه الصبي بنفسه لو كان مميزاً وضرر محض كالعتاق والطلاق، فلا يملكه عليه أحد. (۱) ذخيرة العقبى» تكملة البحر» (۸: ۲۳۷).
And purchasing what is necessary for the child, and selling to brother, uncle, and mother of the Muslims. [2] His statement: 'And purchasing what is necessary for the child...etc.'; meaning it is permissible for these three to buy and sell for the minor what is necessary, such as maintenance and clothing; and because if they did not have that, the minor would be harmed, and harm is prohibited. The principle in this is that transactions concerning a minor are of three types: purely beneficial, which every person who has him in his care may do, whether guardian or stranger, like gift and charity, and the discerning minor may do it himself; purely harmful, like manumission and divorce, which no one may do on his behalf; and mixed between benefit and harm, like sale and lease for profit, which only the father, grandfather, and their executor may do, whether the minor is in their care or not, because they act on his behalf by virtue of guardianship. This is in al-Kafi.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on who may purchase necessary items for a minor child. The text states that the father, grandfather, and the mother of the Muslims (i.e., the Islamic treasury) may buy and sell for the minor. The commentary explains that it is permissible for these three to acquire necessities like food and clothing, because otherwise the child would suffer, and harm is forbidden. It then provides a foundational principle: transactions involving a minor fall into three categories. First, purely beneficial acts (like accepting gifts) can be done by anyone who has the child in his care, including strangers, and even the discerning minor himself. Second, purely harmful acts (like divorcing or manumitting) cannot be done by anyone on the child's behalf. Third, acts with mixed benefit and harm (like sales and leases for profit) can only be performed by the father, grandfather, or their executor, as they have legal guardianship. This principle ensures the child's welfare while preventing exploitation.

Key Terms
نفع محض (naf' mahd): Purely beneficial act for the minor, requiring no compensation.
ضرر محض (darar mahd): Purely harmful act for the minor, causing loss without benefit.
متردد بين النفع والضرر (mutaraddad bayna al-naf' wa al-darar): Mixed benefit and harm, where profit is possible but risk exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Any relative (uncle, brother)
B) The father, grandfather, and their executors
C) The mother only
D) Any stranger who wishes to benefit the child

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi law of guardianship categorizes transactions for minors into three types, with only the father, grandfather, and their executor having authority over mixed-benefit contracts, ensuring the minor's welfare.

Selling Grape Juice to a Winemaker: The Hanafi Principle of Sin Not Inhering in Lawful Commodities
Ruling on selling grape juice to a wine-maker with analogical proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lawful and unlawful commodities in sales based on the Hanafi principle of ma'siyah la taqumu bi 'ayn al-mabi' (the sin does not inhere in the substance sold).

Core Idea
The sin of the buyer does not make the seller’s lawful commodity impermissible — prohibition attaches to action, not substance.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi ruling: selling grape juice to someone known to make wine is permissible. The key insight is that the sin of wine-making attaches to the buyer's action, not to the juice itself. This principle distinguishes between items that are intrinsically permissible and those inherently tied to disobedience, such as weapons used for strife.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Read the source text to determine what act is being ruled upon and what the Hanafi hukm level is.
The act is selling grape juice to a known winemaker; the ruling is permissible (jai'iz).
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The text provides a qiyas (analogical reasoning) as proof. Identify the original case (asl), the new case (far'), and the effective cause (illa).
Asl: selling iron to people of strife. Far': selling grape juice to a winemaker. Illa: sin does not inhere in the item.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah (How the Proof Yields the Ruling)
Explain how the analogy supports the ruling. The sin does not attach to the item sold, so the sale remains lawful.
The illa (non-inherence of sin) is the decisive factor.
4
Note the Contrast with Other Sales
The text contrasts this with selling weapons to a troublemaker (makruh) and selling wine (batil). Identify the difference in rulings and the reason.
Weapons are mal mutaqawwim but sin inheres; wine is not mutaqawwim at all.
5
Evaluate the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Consider the cross-madhhab difference: Shafi'is may prohibit or dislike such sales. What practical difference does this make?
A Hanafi may sell without sin; a Shafi'i would avoid it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع العصير ممن يتخذ خمراً)، فإنَّ المعصية لا تقوم بعين العصير، بخلافِ بيع السلاح ممن يعلمُ أَنَّهُ من أهل الفتنة ، فإنّ المعصية تقوم بعينه. [۴] قوله : فإن المعصية تقوم بعينه.... الخ ؛ في «غاية البيان»: فوزان بيع العصير ممن يتخذه تمراً بيع الحديد من أهل الفتنة، فكلاهما لا يكره، ووزان بيع السلاح من أهل الفتنة بيع الخمر ؛ لأنَّ الفساد يقعُ بعين المبيع، ولكن بيع الخمر بعينه لا يجوز أصلاً ؛ لأنها ليست بمتقومة في حق المسلم، وبيع السلاح منهم يجوز، ويكره لأنَّ السلاح مال متقوم، والكراهة للمعصية المجاورة.
And selling grape juice to someone who takes it as wine, then the disobedience does not inhere in the juice itself, unlike selling weapons to someone known to be a source of strife, for the disobedience inheres in the weapon itself. [4] His statement: 'For the disobedience inheres in it itself...etc.'; in 'Ghayat al-Bayan': the weight of selling grape juice to one who takes it as vinegar is selling iron to people of strife, both are not disliked; and the weight of selling weapons to people of strife is selling wine, because corruption occurs in the very thing sold. But selling wine itself is absolutely impermissible because it is not a valuable property (ghayr mutaqawwim) for a Muslim; while selling weapons to them is permissible but disliked, because the weapon is valuable property, and the dislike is due to the accompanying disobedience.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on selling grape juice to someone who will use it to make wine. The text states that it is permissible because the sin of making wine does not attach to the juice itself. This is contrasted with selling weapons to someone known to foment strife, where the sin attaches to the weapon because it is intrinsically used for harm. The commentary from Ghayat al-Bayan provides a nuanced comparison: selling juice to one who will make vinegar is like selling iron to troublemakers—both are not disliked. Selling weapons to troublemakers is like selling wine, but wine itself is not a valuable property for a Muslim, so its sale is absolutely impermissible. Weapons, however, are valuable property, so the sale itself is valid but disliked due to the potential sin. This shows that the ruling depends on the intrinsic nature of the item: if the item is inherently tied to disobedience (like a weapon for strife), the sale is disliked; if the item itself is lawful (like grape juice), the sale is permissible even if the buyer misuses it.

Key Terms
معصية لا تقوم بعين العصير (ma'siyah la taqumu bi 'ayn al-'asir): The sin does not inhere in the physical substance of the juice; the juice itself is not unlawful.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Valuable property that is recognized in Islamic law as having commercial value and is permissible to own.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling grape juice to someone who will make wine considered permissible in the Hanafi school?
A) Because the juice will be changed into something else
B) Because the sin does not attach to the juice itself
C) Because the seller cannot be sure of the buyer's intention
D) Because wine is not haram for non-Muslims

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the permissibility of a sale depends on the intrinsic lawfulness of the commodity, not on the buyer’s sinful intention.

Carrying Wine of a Dhimmi for Hire: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hiring to carry a dhimmi's wine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding carrying wine of a dhimmi for hire and identify the basis of their disagreement.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits carrying a dhimmi's wine; the Sahibayn prohibit it as assisting in sin.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ on whether a Muslim may accept payment to carry wine belonging to a dhimmi. The disagreement hinges on the principle of assistance in sin (i'ana 'ala al-ma'siyah) and the permissibility of dealing with a dhimmi's property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two positions
Read the passage and note the act (carrying wine of a dhimmi for hire) and the two rulings: permissible according to Abu Hanifa, not permissible according to the Sahibayn.
2
Understand the basis of Abu Hanifa's permissibility
Consider why Abu Hanifa permits. The dhimmi is allowed to own and consume wine according to his faith, so carrying it may be seen as a neutral service.
The text does not provide Abu Hanifa's proof explicitly; it is inferred from general Hanafi principles.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's prohibition
The Sahibayn argue that carrying wine is assisting in sin (i'ana 'ala al-ma'siyah), and they cite the hadith cursing those involved in wine.
The hadith is incomplete in the source but is well-known: 'Allah curses wine, its drinker, its server, its carrier...'
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is that a Muslim may or may not accept such employment depending on which view he follows. The later Hanafi tradition often favors the Sahibayn's view for caution.

Source Text

وحمل خمر ذمي بأجر )، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما : لا يجوز، [5] قوله : ولا يحل له الأجر ؛ لأنه إعانة على المعصية، وقد صح أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لعن

[5] His statement: 'And the hire is not lawful for him'; because it is assistance in sin, and it has been confirmed that the Prophet, peace be upon him, cursed...
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding carrying wine belonging to a dhimmi (non-Muslim under Islamic protection) for hire. Imam Abu Hanifa rules that it is permissible for the Muslim to accept the job, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, rule that it is not permissible. The commentary explains that it is considered assistance in sin (i'ana 'ala al-ma'siyah), and cites a hadith where the Prophet cursed those involved in wine (the text cuts off). The disagreement stems from whether the act itself is inherently sinful. Abu Hanifa likely views the act of carrying as permissible since the wine is owned by a dhimmi who is allowed to consume it, while the Sahibayn view it as supporting a prohibited substance.

Key Terms
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under Islamic protection subject to jizya.
إعانة على المعصية (i'ana 'ala al-ma'siyah): Assistance in committing a sin, which is itself prohibited.

Selling Grape Juice and Carrying Dhimmi's Wine: Sin Inherence and Intent
Rules on selling grape juice to a winemaker and carrying dhimmi's wine for hire

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the Hanafi ruling on selling grape juice to a winemaker, EXPLAIN the intra-Hanafi dispute on carrying dhimmi's wine for hire, and APPLY the distinction between sin inherent in the substance vs. sin in the act.

Core Idea
A sin must inhere in the object itself to prohibit its sale; carrying wine is permissible if the sin is in the act, not the carriage.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents two nuanced rulings: one on selling grape juice, and another on carrying wine for a dhimmi. The first is straightforward, but the second reveals a key intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples. By understanding the reasoning, you will see how the school distinguishes between inherent and incidental sin, and how a hadith can be interpreted to support different positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling and its rationale
Find the statement about selling grape juice (عصير) to someone who will make it into wine. What is the ruling? Why is it not prohibited? Note the contrast with selling weapons.
The key phrase: 'المعصية لا تقوم بعين العصير' — sin does not inhere in the juice.
2
Understand the analogy and differences
The commentator (Ghayat al-Bayan) compares selling grape juice to selling iron to troublemakers, and selling weapons to selling wine. Grasp why each comparison holds or breaks.
3
State the second ruling and the disagreement
Read the portion about carrying dhimmi's wine for hire. What is Abu Hanifa's position? What is the position of the Sahibayn? Why do they differ?
The key term: 'ولا يحل له الأجر' — the fee is not lawful according to the Sahibayn.
4
Analyze the evidence: hadith and istihsan
The Sahibayn cite the hadith cursing the carrier of wine. Abu Hanifa counters that the sin is in drinking, not carrying, and the hadith is interpreted to apply only when one intends sin.
This is a classic example of istihsan overriding a hadith.
5
Conclude the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference? The permissibility of the contract and the fee. Which position is followed? (Not specified here, but the dispute is recorded.)
This shows the richness of Hanafi jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع العصير ممن يتخذ خمراً، فإنَّ المعصية لا تقوم بعين العصير، بخلافِ بيع السلاح ممن يعلمُ أَنَّهُ من أهل الفتنة ، فإنّ المعصية تقوم بعينه (وحمل خمر ذمي بأجر ) ، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له، وعندهما : لا يجوز، ولا يحل له الأجر ومتردد بين النفع والضرر ؛ مثل البيع والإجارة للاسترباح ، فلا يملكه إلا الأب والجد ووصيهما سواء كان الصغير في أيديهم أو لا ؛ لأنهم يتصرفون عليه بحكم الولاية.
هكذا في «الكافي».
[1] قوله : فإن المعصية تقوم بعينه.... الخ ؛ في «غاية البيان»: فوزان بيع العصير ممن يتخذه تمراً بيع الحديد من أهل الفتنة، فكلاهما لا يكره، ووزان بيع السلاح من أهل الفتنة بيع الخمر ؛ لأنَّ الفساد يقعُ بعين المبيع، ولكن بيع الخمر بعينه لا يجوز أصلاً ؛ لأنها ليست بمتقومة في حق المسلم، وبيع السلاح منهم يجوز، ويكره لأنَّ السلاح مال متقوم، والكراهة للمعصية المجاورة.
[2] قوله : ولا يحل له الأجر ؛ لأنه إعانة على المعصية، وقد صح أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لعن في الخمر عشراً، حاملها والمحمول إليه).
وله : إن المعصية في شربها، وهو فعلُ فاعل مختار، وليس الشرب من ضرورات الحمل، ولا يقصد به، والحديث محمول على الحمل المقرون بقصد المعصية. كذا في «الهداية».
And selling grape juice to someone who makes it into wine: because sin does not inhere in the grape juice itself, unlike selling weapons to someone known to be from people of fitna, because sin inheres in the weapon itself. (And carrying dhimmi's wine for hire): this according to Abu Hanifa is permissible, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is not permissible. And the fee is not lawful for him. And [the foundling's property] is ambiguous between benefit and harm, like sale and hire for profit, so no one may dispose of it except the father, grandfather, and their executor, whether the minor is in their custody or not, because they act for him by virtue of guardianship. Thus in al-Kafi. [1] His statement: 'because sin inheres in it...' etc. In Ghayat al-Bayan: the comparison of selling grape juice to one who makes it into wine is like selling iron to people of fitna; both are not disliked. And the comparison of selling weapons to people of fitna is like selling wine; because corruption occurs in the substance of the sale, but selling wine itself is absolutely impermissible because it is not a valuable property for a Muslim. Selling weapons to them is permissible but disliked because weapons are valuable property, and the dislike is for the accompanying sin. [2] His statement: 'and the fee is not lawful for him' because it is assistance in sin. And it is authentically reported that the Prophet (peace be upon him) cursed ten things in wine: the carrier and the one to whom it is carried. And he (Abu Hanifa) says: sin is in drinking it, which is an act of a free agent, and drinking is not a necessary consequence of carrying, nor is it intended. The hadith is interpreted as referring to carrying with the intention of sin. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings. First, selling grape juice ('asir) to someone who will make it into wine is permissible because the sin is not inherent in the juice itself; it becomes sinful only after transformation. This is contrasted with selling weapons to troublemakers, where the weapon itself is used for fighting, so it is disliked. Second, carrying a dhimmi's wine for hire: Abu Hanifa permits it because the sin (drinking) is an act of the free choice of the drinker, not inherent in the carriage. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) prohibit it as it assists sin. The text then discusses that the hadith cursing ten people includes the carrier, but Abu Hanifa reconciles it by saying it refers to carrying with the intention of sin. The last part about 'mutaraddid bayn al-naf' wa al-darar' is a separate point about the foundling's property, which may be a continuation from the previous lesson or a new topic; it says only father, grandfather, and their executor can deal with ambiguous transactions, linking it to the foundling's guardianship.

Key Terms
عصير ('asir): Fresh grape juice that is not yet wine.
حمل خمر ذمي بأجر (haml khamr dhimmi bi-ajr): Contracting to transport wine belonging to a dhimmi for a fee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, why is selling grape juice to a winemaker permissible even though the juice will become wine?
A) Because the juice is not wine yet.
B) Because the sin does not inhere in the juice itself.
C) Because the winemaker is a dhimmi.
D) Because the contract is not for wine.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of selling an object depends on whether sin inheres in its substance; carrying wine is only sinful if done with that intent.

Selling Buildings in Mecca and Accepting Gifts from a Slave Trader
Hanafi ruling on slave trader transactions with istihsan justification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on selling buildings in Mecca and accepting gifts from a slave trader, and distinguish between qiyas and istihsan in this context.

Core Idea
Istihsan permits what qiyas forbids when necessity and prophetic precedent override strict analogy.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses several transactions involving slave traders in early Islamic society. By strict qiyas, these actions are impermissible, but the Hanafi school applies istihsan based on the Prophet's example and practical necessity. However, this dispensation is restricted to specific contexts where non-Muslims predominate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their qiyas ruling
List the five acts mentioned: selling buildings in Mecca, tying the slave, accepting his gift, responding to his invitation, borrowing his animal. By qiyas, all are not permissible (la yajuz).
2
Understand the istihsan basis
The istihsan is based on the Prophet's acceptance of gifts from Salman and Barira (both slaves or former slaves). This precedent shows that such interactions are not inherently prohibited.
Also the Companions accepted invitations from slaves, reinforcing the precedent.
3
Analyze the scope and exceptions
The dispensation applies only in contexts like the sawad of Kufa, where most inhabitants are dhimmis. In Muslim-dominated areas, the original prohibition remains. The text states 'wa huwa al-asahh' (this is the most correct).
This restriction prevents misuse of the dispensation.
4
Apply the principle to similar cases
The underlying principle is necessity (darurah): traders need these social interactions. Yet, gifting dirhams is not a necessity, so the prohibition remains for monetary gifts.
Distinguishing between necessity and non-necessity is key.
5
Note the incomplete discussion
The passage cuts off at 'so it remains on', indicating the ruling on monetary gifts is not fully elaborated. The student should recognize the need to consult complete sources for the full ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع بناء بيوت مكة، وتقييد العبد، وقبول هديته تاجراً، وإجابة دعوته، واستعارة دابته، وفي القياس لا يجوز ، وجه الاستحسان : أنه قبل هدية سلمان وبريرة . قالوا : هذا كان في سوادِ الكوفة ؛ لأنَّ غالب أهلها أهل الذمة، فأما في سوادنا، فأعلام الإسلام فيها ظاهرة، فلا يمكنون فيها أيضاً، وهو الأصح. [٢] قوله : وجه الاستحسان أنه قبل هدية ... الخ ؛ وأجاب الصحابة دعوة مولى أبي أسيد له، وكان عبداً ، ولأنّ في هذه الأشياء ضرورة لا يجد التاجر بداً منها ، ومَن ملك شيئاً يملك ما هو من ضروراته ، ولا ضرورة في إهداء الدراهم، فبقي على
And selling buildings of Mecca, and tying the slave, and accepting his gift as a trader, and responding to his invitation, and borrowing his animal. By analogy it is not permissible. The basis of istihsan: that he (the Prophet) accepted the gift of Salman and Barira. They said: This was in the countryside of Kufa, because most of its inhabitants were dhimmis. As for our countryside, the signs of Islam are apparent in it, so they are not allowed in it either, and this is the most correct view. (Commentary): He said: The basis of istihsan is that he accepted the gift... etc. And the Companions responded to the invitation of the master of Abu Usayd, who was a slave. And because in these matters there is a necessity that the trader cannot do without. Whoever owns something owns what is necessary for it. And there is no necessity in gifting dirhams, so it remains on (the original ruling).
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage addresses several issues: selling buildings in Mecca, tying a slave (as security?), accepting a gift from a trader slave, responding to his invitation, and borrowing his animal. The text states that by qiyas (analogy) these actions are not permissible, but by istihsan (juristic preference) they are allowed. The istihsan is based on the Prophet's acceptance of gifts from Salman (the Persian) and from Barira (a freed slave). The note adds that this ruling applied in the sawad of Kufa where most inhabitants were dhimmis; in our sawad where Islam dominates, these permissions are not given. The commentary then expands on the istihsan for accepting gifts: the Prophet accepted gifts and the Companions accepted invitations from slaves. The reason is necessity: traders need these social interactions. The principle is: if one owns something, one owns its necessities. Gifting dirhams is not a necessity, so the original prohibition remains. The text cuts off at 'so it remains on', indicating the discussion is incomplete.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; departing from an established qiyas (analogy) in favor of a more suitable ruling due to a stronger proof, custom, or necessity.
هدية (hadiyyah): Gift, present.
تاجر (tājir): Trader, merchant. Here refers to a slave who is a trader (mukatab or similar?).
ضرورة (ḍarūrah): Necessity; a situation where something is needed to avoid hardship or to fulfill a legitimate need.

B) Because the slave trader is the rightful owner.
C) Because the qiyas permits it directly.
D) Because the gift is a necessity for the slave.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan permits what qiyas forbids when necessity and prophetic precedent align, but this dispensation is context-dependent.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Selling Land in Mecca
Intra-Hanafi dispute on selling Mecca land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling land and buildings in Mecca and identify the positions of the three Hanafi masters.

Core Idea
Selling buildings in Mecca is permitted, but selling the land is disliked (makruh) due to its sanctity, unlike buildings which are private property.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of land and buildings in Mecca. Abu Hanifa holds that selling the land is disliked because Mecca is sacred, while his students Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that it is permissible. The commentator sides with Abu Hanifa, providing evidence from hadith and analogy to the courtyard of the Kaaba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on buildings
The text begins by stating that selling the buildings of Mecca is permitted. Note that this is not disputed.
The dispute is only about the land, not the building.
2
State the positions on land
Abu Hanifa says selling the land is disliked (makruh). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say there is no harm in selling it. A narration from Abu Hanifa also permits it.
Identify the three positions.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
He cites the hadith: 'Indeed Mecca is sacred; its dwellings are not sold nor inherited.' He also analogizes the land to the courtyard of the Kaaba, which is venerated.
This is a dalil naqli.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
They argue that the land is owned by individuals, as evidenced by inheritance practices. Thus, it can be sold like buildings.
Their proof is based on ownership rights.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentator states that Abu Hanifa's view is the most correct (al-ashah). Thus, selling the land is disliked.
This is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع بناء بيوت مكة، وتقييد العبد، وقبول هديته تاجراً، وإجابة دعوته، واستعارة دابته، وفي القياس لا يجوز ، وجه الاستحسان : أنه قبل هدية سلمان وبريرة . قالوا : هذا كان في سوادِ الكوفة ؛ لأنَّ غالب أهلها أهل الذمة، فأما في سوادنا، فأعلام الإسلام فيها ظاهرة، فلا يمكنون فيها أيضاً، وهو الأصح.
[١]قوله : وبيع بناء بيوت مكة ؛ أي جازَ بيع بناء بيوت مكة ، قال في «الهداية» : ويكره بيع أرضها، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وقالا : لا بأس ببيع أرضها أيضاً، وهذا رواية عن أبي حنيفة له ؛ لأنها مملوكة لهم ؛ لظهور الاختصاص الشرعي بها، كالتوارث في الأرض، وقسمتها في المواريث، فصار كالبناء.
ولأبي حنيفة له قوله : «ألا إنّ مكة حرامٌ لا تباع رباعها، ولا تورث ولأنها حرّة محترمة ؛ لأنها فناء الكعبة، وقد ظهر أثر التعظيم فيها، فكذا في حق البيع بخلاف البناء ؛ لأنه خالص ملك الباني، ألا ترى أنه لو بنى في المستأجر أو في الوقف صار البناء ملكاً وجاز بيعه.
[٢] قوله : وجه الاستحسان أنه قبل هدية ... الخ ؛ وأجاب الصحابة دعوة مولى أبي أسيد له، وكان عبداً ، ولأنّ في هذه الأشياء ضرورة لا يجد التاجر بداً منها ، ومَن ملك شيئاً يملك ما هو من ضروراته ، ولا ضرورة في إهداء الدراهم، فبقي على أصل القياس. قاله الجلبي
And selling the buildings of the houses of Mecca, and tying up the slave, and accepting his gift when he is a trader, and answering his invitation, and borrowing his animal. In qiyas it is not permissible. The basis of istihsan: that he accepted the gift of Salman and Burayra. They said: This was in the Sawad of Kufa, because most of its inhabitants were dhimmis. As for our Sawad, the signs of Islam are apparent there, so they are not permitted there either, and this is the most correct. [1] His statement: And selling the buildings of the houses of Mecca, i.e., selling the buildings of the houses of Mecca is permitted. He said in al-Hidayah: Selling its land is disliked, and this is according to Abu Hanifa. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: There is no harm in selling its land as well, and this is a narration from Abu Hanifa, because it is owned by them due to the appearance of legal specification in it, like inheritance of land and its division in inheritance, so it becomes like the building. The proof for Abu Hanifa is his statement: 'Indeed, Mecca is sacred; its dwellings are not sold nor inherited.' And because it is free and honored, as it is the courtyard of the Kaaba, and the effect of veneration has appeared in it, so likewise regarding sale, unlike the building because it is the pure ownership of the builder. Do you not see that if one builds on rented land or on waqf, the building becomes ownership and its sale is permitted. [2] His statement: The basis of istihsan is that he accepted the gift... etc.: And the Companions answered the invitation of the freed slave of Abu Usayd, and he was a slave. And because in these things there is a necessity that the trader cannot do without, and whoever owns something owns that which is from its necessities, and there is no necessity in gifting dirhams, so it remains on the original qiyas. Al-Jalabi said it.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah addresses the ruling on selling buildings in Mecca and interactions with non-Muslim traders in the Sawad of Kufa. It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of land in Mecca. The matn states a general permission for various transactions with a dhimmi trader, but the commentator explains that the basis for this permission is istihsan, as practiced by the Companions who accepted gifts from non-Muslims. The dispute centers on whether the land of Mecca can be sold. Abu Hanifa holds that selling the land is disliked (makruh) because Mecca is sacred and its dwellings cannot be traded or inherited, as per the hadith. He compares the land to the courtyard of the Kaaba, which is venerated. However, buildings constructed on the land are pure property of their builders and can be sold, similar to buildings on rented or waqf land. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the land is owned and can be sold, citing inheritance practices. The commentator prefers Abu Hanifa's view as the most correct (al-ashah). The second part of the passage clarifies that the istihsan for transactions with a dhimmi trader applies only when there is genuine necessity, such as in Kufa where dhimmis were the majority. In lands where Islam is dominant, such permissions do not apply.

Key Terms
رباع (ribāʿ): Dwellings or land properties in Mecca.
فناء الكعبة (fināʾ al-kaʿbah): The courtyard or precinct of the Kaaba, considered sacred.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in cases of necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the intra-Hanafi dispute in this passage, what is the ruling on selling the land of Mecca according to the most correct position?
A) Permissible (ja'iz)
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Forbidden (haram)
D) Recommended (mustahabb)

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, selling the land of Mecca is disliked (makruh) due to its sanctity, while selling buildings on it is permitted.

Scope of Istihsan in Transactions with a Dhimmi Trader
Ruling on transactions with non-Muslim traders and the role of istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the application of istihsan in permitting transactions with a dhimmi trader and identify the condition of necessity that limits this permission.

Core Idea
Istihsan permits accepting gifts from a dhimmi trader when there is necessity, but not in lands where Islam dominates.

This passage explains that while qiyas (analogy) would prohibit transactions like accepting gifts from a dhimmi trader, istihsan (juristic preference) overrides this due to the Sunnah and necessity. However, this ruling is contextual: it applied in the Sawad of Kufa where dhimmis were the majority, but not in lands where Islam is dominant. This lesson explores the balance between principle and practical need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qiyas ruling
The text says that by qiyas, these transactions are not permissible. Determine the underlying analogy.
Qiyas would compare the dhimmi trader to other non-Muslims with whom transactions are restricted.
2
Understand the istihsan basis
The Prophet (ﷺ) accepted gifts from non-Muslims, such as from Salman and Burayra. This sets a precedent.
The Sunnah overrides the qiyas.
3
Apply the condition of necessity
The istihsan is justified by necessity: the trader cannot do without these interactions. Necessity makes permissible what would otherwise be prohibited.
Principle: 'Whoever owns something owns its necessities.'
4
Distinguish between contexts
In the Sawad of Kufa, where dhimmis are the majority, the necessity is clear. In lands where Islam dominates, the necessity is not present, so the original qiyas applies.
The ruling is context-dependent.
5
Formulate the final ruling
Thus, accepting gifts from a dhimmi trader is permissible only under necessity in non-Muslim majority areas.
This is the mufta bihi according to the text.

Hoarding (Ihtikar): Definition, Ruling, and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains ruling on hoarding food and goods, with intra-Hanafi disagreement on scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hoarding, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, and apply the criteria of harm and staple food to determine what constitutes ihtikar.

Core Idea
Hoarding is makruh tahrimi when it harms the public; the key dispute is which goods trigger the prohibition.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on ihtikar (hoarding) — the act of withholding goods from sale to drive up prices. While the school agrees that hoarding essential foodstuffs is blameworthy, a well-known intra-Hanafi disagreement exists on the scope: does the prohibition extend to all harmful goods or only to staple foods? We will unpack the positions of the three eponymous imams, the evidence they rely on, and the practical consequences of their differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue of the dispute
Pinpoint what exactly is being contested: the types of goods that fall under the prohibition of ihtikar. Note that all imams agree on the basic ruling (makruh tahrimi) when harm occurs.
The dispute is about scope, not the ruling itself.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa and its basis
Abu Hanifa restricts hoarding to staple food (qut) of humans and animals. Understand his reasoning: public need is primarily in food, and other goods are not essential for survival.
The hadith 'al-jalibu marzuq...' is cited as evidence, but Abu Hanifa focuses on the concept of necessity.
3
State the position of Abu Yusuf and its basis
Abu Yusuf defines hoarding by the criterion of harm: any good whose withholding causes general hardship is ihtikar. Grasp his broader view that the public's right is not limited to food.
Abu Yusuf's position aligns with a more harm-based approach.
4
State the position of Muhammad al-Shaybani and its qualification
Muhammad excludes clothes from hoarding, but al-Quduri qualifies: if the item (e.g., rice) is a staple in a particular region, it is indeed hoarding. So Muhammad's view is context-dependent.
The example of rice in Tabaristan shows that local dietary habits matter.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position
According to al-Hidayah, the position of Abu Hanifa is the operative one for fatwa. Understand that even though Abu Yusuf's view has logical strength, the school follows Abu Hanifa on this issue.
The tarjih is based on the principle that the original ruling (zahir al-riwayah) is given preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتكار قوتِ البشر والبهائم في بلد يضر بأهله. التَّخصيص بالقوتِ قول أبي حنيفة وعند أبي يوسف : كل ما أضرَّ بالعامَّةِ حبسه فهو احتکار، وعن محمد ه : لا احتکار في الثياب. قوله : واحتكار القوت .... الخ ؛ أي يكره احتكار القوت ... الخ ؛ والأصل فيه قوله : الجالب مرزوق والمحتكر ملعون ) ، أخرجه ابن ماجـة عـن عمـر بـن الخطاب ؛ ولأنه تعلّق به حق العامة، وفي الامتناع عن البيع إبطال حقهم، وتضييق الأمر عليهم، فيكره إذا كان يضرّ بهم ذلك بأن كان المصر صغيراً، بخلاف إذا لم يضرّ ؛ لأنه حابس ملكه من غير إضرار بغيره. قوله : لا احتكار في الثياب ؛ فإنّ الحاجة اللازمة الدائمة في الأقوات دون غيرها، والثيابُ لا يقفُ قيام الأبدان، وبقاء الحياة عليها ، وقوتُ الحيوان ما كان قوامه به من المأكول. وقال القُدُوري في «شرح مختصر الكرخي : وأما قول محمد له : إن حبس الأرز ليس باحتكار، فهو محمول على البلاد التي لا يتقوتون به، أما في الموضع الذي هو قوتهم مثل طبرستان، فهو احتكار. كذا في «غاية البيان». قوله : ومدة الحبس...الخ ؛ أي مدة الاحتكار التي يمنع فيها مقدرة بأربعين يوماً ؛ لقوله : «من احتكر طعاماً أربعين ليلة فقد برئ من الله تعالى وبرى الله (۲)، رواه أحمد في مسنده (۳) عن ابن عمر منه. قوله : وقيل : بالشهر ؛ لأنّ ما دونه قليل عاجل، والشهر وما فوقه كثير آجل، وقد مرّ في مواضع عديدة، قال في «الهداية (4) : ويقع التفاوت في الماثم بين أن يتربص العدة، وبين أن يتربّص القحط. والعياذ بالله. قوله : هذا عند أبي حنيفة .. الخ ؛ أي عدم الكراهة فيهما عند أبي حنيفة له، أما في الأول ؛ فلأنّه خالص حقه، لم يتعلّق حق العامة به، ألا ترى أنّ له أن لا يزرع ، فكذا لأن لا بيع، وأما في الثاني ؛ فلان حق العامة إنما يتعلق بما جمع من المصر وجلب إلى فنائها.
And hoarding the food of humans and animals in a country that harms its people. The specification of food is the saying of Abu Hanifa, and according to Abu Yusuf: everything whose withholding harms the public is hoarding, and from Muhammad: there is no hoarding in clothes. His saying: 'and hoarding food... etc.' meaning: hoarding food is disliked... and its basis is his saying: 'the importer is blessed and the hoarder is cursed' reported by Ibn Majah from Umar ibn al-Khattab; and because the right of the public is attached to it, and in refraining from selling there is nullification of their right and causing difficulty for them, so it is disliked if it harms them, as when the town is small, unlike if it does not harm; because he is withholding his property without harming others. His saying: 'no hoarding in clothes' because the necessary and continuous need is in foodstuffs, not other things, and clothes are not essential for the survival of bodies and life, and the food of animals is that by which they are sustained from edibles. And al-Quduri said in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Karkhi: 'As for Muhammad's saying: withholding rice is not hoarding, it is interpreted as referring to countries where they do not use it as staple food; but in a place where it is their staple like Tabaristan, it is hoarding.' So in Ghayat al-Bayan. His saying: 'and the duration of withholding... etc.' meaning the duration of hoarding during which one prevents (sale) is estimated at forty days; due to his saying: 'Whoever hoards food for forty nights has disassociated from Allah and Allah disassociates from him' reported by Ahmad in his Musnad from Ibn Umar. His saying: 'and it is said: a month' because less than that is a short immediate period, and a month and above is a long deferred period, and it has been mentioned in many places. He said in al-Hidayah: 'And the difference in sin occurs between waiting for the usual price and waiting for drought – may Allah protect us.' His saying: 'this according to Abu Hanifa... etc.' meaning the absence of dislike in both cases according to Abu Hanifa. As for the first: because it is his pure right, the right of the public is not attached to it; do you not see that he may choose not to plant, so similarly he may choose not to sell. As for the second: because the right of the public is only attached to what is gathered from the city and brought to its precincts.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the ruling of ihtikar (hoarding). It begins with the matn stating that hoarding the staple food of humans and animals is blameworthy when it harms the people of the town. The commentary then details the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of hoarding. Abu Hanifa restricts the prohibition to essential foodstuffs (qut). Abu Yusuf extends it to any goods whose withholding causes general harm. Muhammad al-Shaybani makes an exception for clothes, but the commentator al-Quduri clarifies that Muhammad's view applies only where the item is not a staple; if it is a staple like rice in Tabaristan, it is indeed hoarding. The passage provides the evidentiary basis: a hadith (though weak) and the principle of public harm. It also discusses the duration of hoarding that triggers sin: some say forty days, others say one month, and the sin increases if one waits for a drought. Finally, it clarifies that Abu Hanifa does not consider withholding the produce of one's own land or goods brought from another town as hoarding, because the public's right is not attached there; one has the right not to plant or not to sell. This shows the nuanced Hanafi approach balancing property rights with public welfare.

Key Terms
احتكار (ihtikar): Withholding goods from sale to drive up prices, causing harm to the public.
قوت (qut): Staple food that sustains life.
الجالب مرزوق والمحتكر ملعون (al-jalibu marzuq wa al-muhtakiru mal'un): Hadith: The importer is blessed, the hoarder is cursed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the defining criterion for an act to be considered ihtikar?
A) That the withheld good is a staple food (qut) and hoarding causes public harm.
B) That the withholding of any good causes public harm.
C) That the good is withheld for more than forty days.
D) That the good is brought from another town.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers hoarding (ihtikar) to be makruh tahrimi, but the relied-upon opinion limits it to withholding staple food that causes public harm.

Duration of Hoarding and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Land Produce
Discussion of hoarding duration and Hanafi disagreement on land produce and imports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sin and worldly punishment for hoarding, and explain the three Hanafi positions on hoarding one's own land produce and imports.

Core Idea
Sin is immediate; duration limits apply only to worldly punishment, and the judge may sell hoarded goods.

This passage from a commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies that hoarding is sinful from the moment it begins, regardless of how long the goods are held. The forty-day or one-month limit mentioned by some scholars refers to the judge's discretionary punishment (ta'zir), not the sin itself. The text then addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: is it disliked (makruh) to hoard the yield of one's own land or goods imported from another country? The three eponyms of the school give different answers, and the practical consequence of their disagreement matters for farmers and merchants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
Hoarding (ihtikar) is detaining food or necessities to sell at a high price during shortage. It is generally considered makruh tahrimi if it harms the public.
The passage focuses on the judge's intervention, not the basic ruling.
2
Distinguish between sin and worldly punishment
The text explicitly states 'he sins even if the duration is short' (يأثم وإن قلت المدة). The forty days or one month mentioned are estimates for the judge to impose punishment, not conditions for the sin.
This distinction is crucial: the sin is timeless; punishment is discretionary.
3
Understand the judge's duty and authority
The judge must order the hoarder to sell surplus beyond his family's needs. If the hoarder refuses, the judge may discipline him (ta'zir) and, according to the correct view (as-sahih), may sell the goods directly.
This is the mufta bihi position: the judge can force a sale.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on land produce and imports
Abu Hanifa holds that hoarding one's own land produce or imported goods is not disliked because it is private property with no public right attached. Abu Yusuf considers both disliked. Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguishes: if the good is commonly imported, it is treated like city goods (hence disliked); otherwise, no dislike.
The key is whether the public has a right to expect the good to be available in the market.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
For a farmer hoarding his own wheat: Abu Hanifa allows it; Abu Yusuf prohibits it; Muhammad allows it if that type of wheat is not commonly imported from elsewhere. The judge's ruling will differ depending on which position is followed.
The text does not explicitly prefer one view, but the mufta bihi regarding judge's sale is settled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومدة الحبس قيل : مقدّرة بأربعين يوماً ، وقيل : بالشهر، وهذا في حق المعاقبة في الدنيا، لكن يأثم وإن قلت المدة، ويجب أن يأمرَهُ القاضي ببيع ما فضل عن قوتِهِ وقوتِ أهله ، فإن لم يفعل عزّرَه ، والصحيح أن القاضي يبيع إن امتنع اتفاقا، ( لا غلة أرضه، ومجلوبه من بلد آخر ) هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وعند أبي يوسف : كل ذلك يكره ، وعند محمد : كل ما يجلب منه إلى المصر غالباً فهو حكم المصر.
The duration of detention (hoarding) is said: estimated at forty days, and said: a month, and this is regarding punishment in the world, but he sins even if the duration is less. And the judge must order him to sell what is beyond his sustenance and his family's sustenance. If he does not do so, the judge may discipline him. The correct view is that the judge sells if he refuses, by agreement. (The yield of his land and his imported goods from another country) This is according to Abu Hanifa. According to Abu Yusuf: All of that is disliked. According to Muhammad: Whatever is commonly imported from it to the city, it has the same ruling as the city.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah discusses the ruling of hoarding (ihtikar). It begins by addressing the duration of hoarding that incurs sin. Two opinions are mentioned: forty days or a month, but the text clarifies that this is regarding worldly punishment by the judge; the sin occurs regardless of duration. The judge is obligated to order the hoarder to sell any surplus beyond his own and his family's needs. If he refuses, the judge may discipline (ta'zir) him, and the correct position is that the judge may sell the goods even without the owner's consent. Then the text turns to a ruling from the matn: 'The yield of his land and his imported goods from another country.' This phrase is explained through an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Abu Hanifa holds that there is no dislike (karahah) in hoarding one's own land produce or goods imported from abroad, since they are his private property with no public right attached. Abu Yusuf, however, considers both types disliked. Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguishes: if the good is commonly imported, it is treated like city goods; otherwise, it is like private property. The mufta bihi is that the judge may sell upon refusal, as stated.

Key Terms
احتكار (ihtikār): Hoarding: detaining foodstuffs or necessities to sell at high prices during shortage.
تعزير (taʿzīr): Discretionary punishment by the judge for sins without fixed hadd penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when does a hoarder become sinful?
A) After 40 days
B) After one month
C) Immediately upon hoarding
D) Only if the judge orders him to sell

Key Takeaway
Hoarding is sinful from the start; the judge may force sale of surplus, and the ruling on hoarding one's own produce differs among the Hanafi imams.

When May the Ruler Set Prices?
Explanation of the condition for the ruler to set prices

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default Hanafi ruling on tasʿir, identify the condition that permits it, and apply the definition of fahish.

Core Idea
Price fixing is prohibited by default, but allowed when sellers double prices.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on government price controls (tasʿir). While the general rule is prohibition based on a prophetic hadith, the school recognizes an exception when sellers engage in gross exploitation. We will explore the proof, the condition for permissibility, and how scholars defined the threshold of 'fahish'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
Determine what tasʿir means and state the default hukm (prohibition).
The Arabic text begins 'ولا يسعر حاكم' meaning the ruler does not set prices (generally).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the hadith cited in the commentary: 'لا تسعروا...' and note its source (Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi).
The hadith is the primary proof for prohibition.
3
Understand the exception
Identify the condition that overrides the default: 'إلا إذا تعدى الأرباب عن القيمة فاحشاً' – when owners grossly exceed the value.
The phrase 'فاحشاً' is key: grossly excessive.
4
Define fahish
Note the Hanafi definition: selling at double the purchase price.
The text says 'وقد قدروا الفحش ببيع ما يبيع بضعف ما يشتريه'.
5
Note the procedural requirement
The ruler must set prices 'بمشورة أهل الرأي' – with consultation of people of opinion (experts).
This ensures justice and avoids arbitrary pricing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسعر حاكم إلا إذا تعدى الأرباب عن القيمة فاحشاً فيسعر بمشورة أهل الرأي. قوله : ولا يسعر...الخ ; لقوله : « لا تسعروا ، فإن الله هو المسعر القابض الباسط الرزاق » ، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي في «البيوع» عن أنس. قال أخي : والمسعر واجد أسعار الطعام، والتسعير: تقديره. قوله : فاحشاً ; وقد قدروا الفحش ببيع ما يبيع بضعف ما يشتريه.
The ruler may not set prices unless the owners exceed the value grossly; then he sets prices with the consultation of people of opinion. His statement: 'and he does not set prices' etc.; due to his statement (PBUH): 'Do not set prices, for Allah is the Price-Setter, the Constrictor, the Expander, the Provider.' Reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi in the Book of Sales from Anas. He said: 'al-musaʿir is the one who fixes food prices, and al-tasʿir is its estimation.' His statement: 'grossly' – and they estimated grossness as selling at twice the purchase price.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage from the commentary discusses the ruling on price fixing (tasʿir). The matn states: 'The ruler does not set prices unless the owners transgress the value grossly; then he sets prices with the consultation of people of opinion.' The commentary explains this by citing the hadith prohibiting tasʿir, as it is Allah's prerogative. However, an exception is made when owners engage in gross exploitation (fahish). The term 'fahish' is defined by later scholars as selling at double the purchase price. The hadith is from Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi. This shows that in the Hanafi school, tasʿir is generally prohibited but permitted under specific conditions of necessity and with expert counsel.

Key Terms
تسعير (tasʿīr): The act of the government setting fixed prices for goods.
فاحش (fāḥish): Grossly excessive; defined here as selling at double the purchase price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever he wishes
B) Only when goods are scarce
C) Only when owners charge double the purchase price (fahish) and with consultation
D) Never, under any circumstances

Key Takeaway
Price fixing is prohibited by default based on the hadith, but the Hanafi school permits it only when sellers double the price and with expert consultation.

Price Fixing (Tas'ir) by the Ruler: General Prohibition and Conditional Permission
Stating the ruling and condition for ruler-imposed price controls

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on price fixing (tas'ir) and identify the condition that permits the ruler to impose price controls.

Core Idea
Price fixing is generally prohibited, but permissible only when sellers charge double the fair price and after consultation with experts.

The Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) prohibited price fixing, declaring that Allah alone controls prices. However, the Hanafi school recognizes that extreme exploitation by sellers may necessitate state intervention. This lesson examines the precise condition—defined as selling at double the cost—under which a ruler may set prices, and the requirement of consulting with knowledgeable advisors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling and Its Proof
Note the initial prohibition of tas'ir based on the hadith 'La tusa'iru...' and understand that the ruler should not fix prices as a default.
The hadith is reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi.
2
Locate the Exception Condition
The matn states 'illa idha ta'adda al-arbab 'an al-qimah fahshan' — unless the sellers transgress excessively. This is the key exception that triggers permissibility.
The condition is not just any price increase but excessive transgression.
3
Understand the Definition of 'Fahsh' (Excessive)
The commentary defines fahsh as selling for double the purchase price. This is a concrete threshold.
Double the purchase price is the Hanafi criterion; other schools may differ.
4
Recognize the Procedural Requirement
Even when the condition is met, the ruler fixes prices 'bi-mashwara ahl al-ra'y' — with the counsel of people of opinion (experts).
Consultation ensures fairness and avoids arbitrary decision-making.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسعر حاكم إلا إذا تعدى الأرباب عن القيمة فاحشاً فيسعر بمشورة أهل الرأي. [١] قوله: ولا يسعر...الخ؛ لقوله: «لا تسعروا، فإن الله هو المسعر القابض الباسط الرزاق»، أخرجه أبو داود والترمذي في «البيوع» عن أنس. قال أخي: والمسعر واجد أسعار الطعام، والتسعير: تقديره. [٢] قوله: فاحشاً؛ وقد قدروا الفحش ببيع ما يبيع بضعف ما يشتريه.
And a ruler should not fix prices unless the owners [sellers] transgress excessively from the [fair] price, then he fixes prices with the counsel of people of opinion. [1] His statement: 'and a ruler should not fix prices... etc.' because of his saying: 'Do not fix prices, for Allah is the One Who fixes prices, the Withholder, the Extender, the Provider,' reported by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi in 'al-Buyu' from Anas. My brother said: 'al-musa'ir is the one who finds prices of food, and tas'ir is estimation.' [2] His statement: 'excessively'; and they have defined the excess as selling what one sells for double what he buys.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding price fixing (tas'ir) by the ruler. The matn states that the ruler should not impose fixed prices except when sellers transgress excessively from the fair price, and even then, he should only do so after consulting with knowledgeable advisors. The commentator explains that the proof is the hadith of the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) prohibiting price fixing, as it is Allah who controls sustenance. The term 'fahish' (excessive) is defined by the scholars as selling an item for double its purchase price. This reflects the Hanafi principle of allowing limited government intervention in the market only in cases of severe exploitation, preserving the natural freedom of trade.

Key Terms
تسعير (tas'ir): Price fixing by the ruler, setting a maximum price for goods.
فحش (fahsh): Excessive overpricing; defined here as selling at double the purchase price.

C) Only with the consent of all sellers.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
The ruler may fix prices only when sellers charge double the fair price and after consulting experts.

Ownership of an Embankment: Landowner vs River Owner's Harim
Determining ownership of an embankment when not in possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on ownership of a musannah (embankment) and distinguish when each applies based on possession and river type.

Core Idea
An embankment belongs to the landowner unless the river owner shows possession, but the river may still have a harim.

Imagine a river owned by one person flows past another person's land. Between them is an embankment (musannah) that neither is actively using. Who owns it? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement among its three founding imams. This lesson unpacks their positions and introduces a practical resolution based on river size.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Understand what a musannah is: an embankment situated between a river belonging to one person and the land of another. The base text from Al-Jami' al-Saghir sets the stage: the embankment is not in anyone's possession.
This is the key condition: no one has taken physical control.
2
Understand the Condition 'Not with Either'
The commentator clarifies that 'not with either' means neither party has planted trees or dumped soil on the embankment. Such actions (shughl) indicate possession and would change the ruling.
'Shughl' (work) establishes a prior right.
3
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa rules that the embankment belongs to the landowner. The reasoning is that by default, the land extends to the river, and the river owner has no claim without evidence of possession.
This is the position recorded in Al-Jami' al-Saghir, a Zahir al-Riwayah text.
4
State Abu Yusuf's Position
Abu Yusuf holds that the river owner has a right to a harim (ancillary strip) of half the river's width on each side of the river, which would include the embankment area.
Harim is necessary for river maintenance and access.
5
State Muhammad's Position
Muhammad ibn al-Hasan extends the harim to the full width of the river on each side, giving the river owner more land for protection.
The dispute is only about the extent of harim, not its existence.
6
Apply the Resolution Based on River Size
The commentator notes that the dispute applies to large rivers that do not need frequent dredging. For small rivers needing constant maintenance, there is consensus (ittifaq) that a harim is obligatory, resolving the disagreement.
This distinction is from Al-Nihayah; al-Jalabi notes a contradiction with Hidayah/Wiqayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَمُسَنَّاةٌ بين نهر رجل وأرض الآخر، وليست مع أحدٍ لصاحب الأرض : أي إن لم يكن لأحدهما عليها غرس أو طينٌ مُلقى فهي لصاحب الأرض عند أبي حنيفة، وإن كان فصاحب الشغل هو صاحب اليد، وعند أبي يوسف له حريمه مقدار نصف بطنِ النهر من كل جانب، وعند محمد له مقدار بطنِ النهر من كل جانب.
[١] قوله : فمُسناة بين نهر ... الخ ؛ صورته ما في «الجامع الصغير»: نهر لرجل إلى مسناة، والآخر خلف المسناة أرض تلزقها، وليست المسناة في يد أحد، فهي لصاحب الأرض.
وقوله : وليست مع أحد معناه ليس لأحدهما عليه غرس ولا طين يلقى، كما بينه الشارح له فينكشف بها للفظ موضع الخلاف، وهو أن يكون الحريم موازياً للأرض لا فاصل بينهما، وأن لا يكون مشغولاً بحق أحدهما. كذا في بعض الحواشي.
[٢] قوله : فهي لصاحب الأرض...الخ ؛ وهذا تصريح منه بعدم لزوم الحريم للنهر، قيل : هذا الخلاف في نهر كبير لا يحتاج فيه إلى الكري في كل حين، أما الأنهار الصغار التي يحتاج إلى كريها في كلِّ وقتِ فلها حريم بالاتفاق، هكذا ذكر في «النهاية»، وظاهر كلام «الهداية» و«الوقاية» ينافيه، قاله الجلبي
An embankment between a man's river and another's land, and it is not with either of them: it belongs to the landowner. That is, if neither of them has trees or dumped soil on it, then it belongs to the landowner according to Abu Hanifa. But if there is such, then the one who has the work is the possessor. According to Abu Yusuf, he has its ḥarīm to the extent of half the river's width on each side. According to Muhammad, he has the extent of the river's width on each side.
[1] His statement: 'A musannah between a river...' etc. Its scenario as in Al-Jami' al-Saghir: a river belonging to a man up to the embankment, and behind the embankment there is another's land adjacent to it, and the embankment is not in anyone's possession, so it belongs to the landowner.
His statement: 'and it is not with either of them' means neither has trees or dumped soil on it, as the commentator clarified, thereby revealing the point of dispute, which is that the ḥarīm is adjacent to the land with no separator between them, and that it is not occupied by a right of either. So in some marginal notes.
[2] His statement: 'it belongs to the landowner' etc. This is an explicit statement that the river does not have a ḥarīm. It is said: this dispute is concerning a large river that does not need dredging every now and then. As for small rivers that need dredging at all times, they have a ḥarīm by consensus. This is mentioned in Al-Nihayah. But the apparent wording of Al-Hidayah and Al-Wiqayah contradicts it, as stated by al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ownership of a musannah (embankment) situated between a river belonging to one person and the land of another, when the embankment is not in anyone's actual possession. The base text from Al-Jami' al-Saghir states that it belongs to the landowner. The commentator clarifies this applies only when neither party has placed trees or deposited soil on it, as such actions indicate possession. If there is such attachment, the one who performed the work (sahib al-shughl) is considered the possessor. Abu Hanifa's view is that the embankment belongs to the landowner (absent signs of possession). Abu Yusuf assigns a ḥarīm (ancillary strip) to the river owner equal to half the river's width on each side. Muhammad assigns the full river's width on each side. The commentator notes that this dispute applies to large rivers that do not require frequent dredging; for small rivers requiring constant maintenance, there is consensus that they have a ḥarīm. Al-Jalabi points out a potential contradiction with the apparent wording of Al-Hidayah and Al-Wiqayah.

Key Terms
مُسَنَّاة (musannah): An embankment or dam built between a river and adjacent land.
حريم (ḥarīm): An ancillary strip of land around a river reserved for its maintenance and access.
الشغل (al-shughl): Physical occupation or work (e.g., planting trees, dumping soil) that indicates possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, to whom does a musannah (embankment) belong if neither party has placed trees or soil on it?
A) To the river owner
B) To the landowner
C) To both equally
D) To the government

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a disputed embankment between a river and land defaults to the landowner, but the river owner may have a harim right for maintenance, varying by river size.

Water Rights: Definitions of Shurb and Shafah and Core Rulings
Defining water rights terms and stating permissibility conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define shurb and shafah, distinguish between public and private water rights, and state the Hanafi rulings on using water from various sources.

Core Idea
Public water is shared for all; private water needs permission — water rights depend on enclosure and harm.

Water rights are fundamental to the Hanafi fiqh of transactions (buyu). This lesson clarifies the two key terms: shurb, the general share of water, and shafah, the specific right to drink. We'll also learn the basic rule: unenclosed water is common property, while enclosed water is private. The rulings on drawing water from the sea, large rivers, wells, and canals are outlined, with an emphasis on avoiding public harm.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the two key terms
Read the definitions of shurb (general water share) and shafah (right to drink using vessels). Note the Quranic evidence for shurb from Surah al-Shu'ara' (26:155).
Shafah is derived from 'lip' (shafah) and is a subset of shurb.
2
2. Distinguish public vs. private water
The text states that water not enclosed in a container (e.g., rivers, seas, lakes) is public. Water enclosed (e.g., in a well, container) is private and requires the owner's permission.
Enclosure (ihraz) is the key factor.
3
3. Understand permissibility for public water
One may water land from the sea or a large river like the Tigris. Digging a canal from such water is allowed if it does not harm the public; if it harms, it is not allowed.
Harm to the public (darar al-'ammah) is a decisive condition.
4
4. Recognize restrictions on private water
One may not water animals from a river if it would damage the river due to abundance. One may not water land or trees from another's river, qanat, or well without permission.
These rulings protect both the resource and private property rights.
5
5. Apply to a simple case
The text concludes that one may water trees or vegetables in his own house by carrying water (presumably from a public source).
This shows the outer limit of the right: you may use public water for your household needs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[فصل في الشرب] الشربُ : نصيب الماء، والشَّفَةُ شرب بني آدمَ والبهائم، ولكلِّ حقها في كلِّ ماء لم يحرز بإناء، وسقي أرضه من البحر ونهر عظيم كدجلة ونحوها ، وشق نهر لأرضه منها أو لنصب الرحى إن لم يضر بالعامة، وإن ضرّ فلا. لا سقي دوابه إن خيف تخريب النهر لكثرتها وأرضه وشجرةٍ من نهر غيره وقناته وبئره إلا بإذنه، وله سقي شجر أو خضر في داره.
[١] قوله : الشِّرب ؛ هو - بالكسر - : نصيب من الماء مطلقا، سواء استوفى بالسقاة، كشرب بني آدم والبهائم ، قال الله له في ناقة صالح الية : لَهَا شِرْبُ وَلَكُمْ شربُ يَوْمٍ مَّعْلُوم )) ، أو لسقي الأرض والشجر. وقوله : الشفة، أصلها : الشفهة، أسقطت الهاء تخفيفاً، والمراد بها هاهنا النصيبُ المخصوص منه ؛ لأنّ أهل الشفة الذين لهم حق للشرب بشفاهتهم وسقي دوابهم، والاستيفاء بالأواني دون سقي الأرض والشجر ، وفيهما عموم وخصوص مطلق. قاله الجلبي
[٢] قوله : أو خضر في داره ...الخ ؛ أي سقى خضراً وقعت في داره بحمل الماء
[Chapter on al-Shurb] Shurb: share of water; al-Shafah: drinking of humans and animals. And each has its right in every water that is not enclosed in a container. And watering his land from the sea and a large river like the Tigris and similar. And digging a river from it for his land or for setting up a watermill if it does not harm the public; if it harms, then not. He may not water his animals if damage to the river is feared due to their abundance, nor water his land and tree from another's river, qanat, or well except with his permission. And he may water trees or vegetables in his house.
[1] His statement: al-Shurb: it is (with kasra) a share of water absolutely, whether taken for drinking (like drinking of humans and animals) – Allah said concerning the she-camel of Salih: "For her is a share of water, and for you is a share on a known day" – or for irrigating land and trees. His statement: al-Shafah, its root is shafahah, the ha' dropped for lightness, and what is meant here is the specific share because the people of shafah are those who have the right to drink with their lips and water their animals, and they take it with vessels, not for irrigating land and trees. Between them is general and specific absolute. Al-Jalabi said.
[2] His statement: 'or vegetables in his house' etc.; that is, he waters vegetables that are in his house by carrying water.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the chapter on water rights (shurb). It defines two key terms: shurb (the general share of water for any use, including irrigation) and shafah (specifically the right to drink for humans and animals, originally meaning 'lip' due to drinking directly). The commentator cites the Quranic verse about Prophet Salih's she-camel to illustrate shurb. The general principle is that everyone has a right to water from any source that is not enclosed in a container (i.e., public bodies of water). Then specific rulings follow: one may water his land from the sea or a large river (like the Tigris) and may dig a canal from such water to his land or to power a mill, provided it does not harm the public. If it causes harm, it is not allowed. One may not water a large number of animals if it would damage the river. Also, one may not water his land, trees, etc. from another person's river, qanat (underground channel), or well without permission. However, he may water trees or vegetables in his own house by carrying water from public sources. The commentary on 'vegetables in his house' clarifies that this is by carrying water (not from another's source).

Key Terms
الشرب (al-shurb): A share of water for any purpose, including drinking, irrigation, or watering animals.
الشفة (al-shafah): The specific right to drink water (for humans and animals) using vessels, derived from 'lip' (shafah).
حرم (ihraz): Enclosure; water that is enclosed in a container is private property and others have no right to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is digging a canal from a large river permissible?
A) In all cases
B) Only if it does not harm the public
C) Only with government permission
D) Only for drinking, not irrigation

Key Takeaway
Public water is a shared resource; use is allowed as long as no harm results, while private water requires the owner's permission.
