The Legal Nature of Water Rights (Shurb): Inheritable but Not Saleable
Ruling on transferability of water rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that water rights (shurb) are inheritable and bequeathable but prohibited from sale, rental, gift, charity, dowry, and sulh consideration.

Core Idea
Water rights (shurb) are inheritable but not saleable—they are a right, not valuable property.

This lesson examines the legal status of water rights in Hanafi fiqh. While water is essential for agriculture, the right to use it (shurb) is treated as a non-saleable entitlement. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper estate planning and transactions involving irrigated land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
First, locate the statement that shurb is inherited and bequeathed, but not sold, rented, etc.
The phrase 'الشرب يورث ويوصى بالانتفاع ولا يباع' is the key.
2
List the prohibited transfers
Enumerate the prohibited modes: sale, rent, gift, charity, dowry, and sulh consideration.
Each prohibition is explicit in the text.
3
Determine the legal reason
Why is shurb not saleable? The commentary explains it is not valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) due to uncertainty (gharar).
This connects to usul al-fiqh principles of mal and gharar.
4
Apply to a hypothetical
Consider a scenario: A farmer wants to sell his water right separately. Apply the ruling: it is prohibited.
This shows practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحى وضع في ملكه ولا يضر بالنهر ولا بالماء، ومن توسيع فم النهر، ومن القسمة بالأيام وقد كانت بالكوى، ومن سوق شربه إلى أرض له أخرى ليس لها منه شرب، والشرب يورث ويوصى بالانتفاع ولا يباع، ولا يؤجر، ولا يوهب، ولا يتصدق به، ولا يجعل مهراً، وبدل الصلح. ولا يضمن من ملأ أرضه فنَزَّت أرض جاره، أو غرقت، ولا من سقى من شرب غيره، وهو قول الإمام المعروفِ بخواهر زاده، وفي «الجامع الصغير» للبزدوي: أنه يضمن.
A mill placed in his ownership, and it does not harm the river nor the water, and [preventing] from widening the river's mouth, and from dividing by days when it had been by openings, and from conveying his drink to another land of his that has no drink from it. And the drink-right is inherited and one may bequeath the benefit, but it is not sold, nor rented, nor gifted, nor given as charity, nor made as a dowry, nor as a settlement. And one does not guarantee who fills his land and his neighbor's land seeps or floods, nor who waters from another's drink. This is the saying of the Imam known as Khawahirzadah, and in al-Jami' al-Saghir by al-Bazdawi: that he does guarantee.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings concerning the legal nature of the water right (shurb) in Hanafi fiqh. The text first lists several prohibited actions: widening a river mouth, changing a division schedule from days to openings or vice versa, and conveying water to another plot that has no prior right—all forbidden because they alter established rights. The core ruling states that the water right (shurb) is inheritable and can be bequeathed for usufruct, but it cannot be sold, rented, gifted, given as charity, made a dowry, or used as settlement in a compromise (sulh). The reason, as explained in the commentary (al-Hidayah), is that shurb is not considered a valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) due to uncertainty (gharar) or lack of precise valuation. The analogy to inheritance: just as retaliation (qisas), debt, and wine are inherited despite being non-saleable, so too shurb is inherited. The commentary from al-'Inayah clarifies that this is based on the principle that heirs stand in the place of the deceased. On the issue of liability: if one irrigates his own land normally, and as a result his neighbor's land becomes wet or flooded, he is not liable for damages, according to the Imam Khawahirzadah. However, al-Bazdawi in al-Jami' al-Saghir holds that he is liable. The commentary from al-Kifayah explains that the cause for non-liability is that the act of watering is lawful (mubah) in one's own land, and the seepage is an accidental occurrence, not a direct consequence. The watering is merely a cause (sabab), not a direct agent, and liability only attaches if there is transgression (ta'addi). An exception: if one waters his land excessively beyond what it can normally absorb, he is liable because it is as if he deliberately directed water to the neighbor's land. This dispute is intra-Hanafi, with the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) being that of Khawahirzadah (no liability), as indicated by the phrasing 'wa huwa qawl al-imam...'.

Key Terms
الشرب (al-shurb): The right to water for irrigation, a legal entitlement to use water from a source like a river or well.
بدل الصلح (badal al-sulh): Consideration given in a compromise settlement (sulh); here, shurb cannot be given as consideration because it is not a saleable property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following transfers of shurb is permitted?
A) Sale
B) Rental
C) Inheritance
D) Gift

Key Takeaway
Water rights (shurb) are inheritable but not saleable because they are not considered valuable property in Hanafi fiqh.

Liability for Water Damage: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for water damage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi positions on liability for water seepage from normal irrigation and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
Normal watering that causes seepage is not automatically liable—only transgression triggers liability.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability when normal irrigation causes damage to a neighbor's land. The disagreement between Khawahirzadah and al-Bazdawi hinges on whether the act of watering is considered a direct cause of damage. Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for applying the correct ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario
The scenario: a person fills his own land with water, causing his neighbor's land to seep or flood.
This is normal irrigation, not excessive.
2
State the two positions
Khawahirzadah: no liability. Al-Bazdawi: liability.
They are both Hanafi scholars.
3
Analyze the reasoning
Khawahirzadah argues the act is mubah (permissible) and the damage is accidental, not a direct consequence. Al-Bazdawi argues that the watering was the cause of the damage.
The key difference is the concept of direct causation (ta'addi) vs. mere causation (sabab).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text presents Khawahirzadah's view first, suggesting it is preferred. The relied-upon position is no liability.
This is confirmed in later Hanafi works.
5
Apply to an exception
If one waters excessively, he is liable, as it is akin to directing water intentionally.
This is an exception mentioned in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحى وضع في ملكه ولا يضر بالنهر ولا بالماء، ومن توسيع فم النهر، ومن القسمة بالأيام وقد كانت بالكوى، ومن سوق شربه إلى أرض له أخرى ليس لها منه شرب، والشرب يورث ويوصى بالانتفاع ولا يباع، ولا يؤجر، ولا يوهب، ولا يتصدق به، ولا يجعل مهراً، وبدل الصلح. ولا يضمن من ملأ أرضه فنَزَّت أرض جاره، أو غرقت، ولا من سقى من شرب غيره، وهو قول الإمام المعروفِ بخواهر زاده، وفي «الجامع الصغير» للبزدوي: أنه يضمن.
A mill placed in his ownership, and it does not harm the river nor the water, and [preventing] from widening the river's mouth, and from dividing by days when it had been by openings, and from conveying his drink to another land of his that has no drink from it. And the drink-right is inherited and one may bequeath the benefit, but it is not sold, nor rented, nor gifted, nor given as charity, nor made as a dowry, nor as a settlement. And one does not guarantee who fills his land and his neighbor's land seeps or floods, nor who waters from another's drink. This is the saying of the Imam known as Khawahirzadah, and in al-Jami' al-Saghir by al-Bazdawi: that he does guarantee.

Commentary

Key Terms
يضمن (yadman): He is liable for damages; in this context, liable to compensate for harm caused to another's land.
خواهر زاده (Khawahirzadah): A Hanafi scholar known for his view that normal watering causing accidental damage does not incur liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The person is liable because he caused the damage.

Linguistic Definition of Khamr and Condition for Intoxication
Defines khamr linguistically and states the intra-Hanafi dispute on its intoxicating condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic definition of khamr and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the condition for grape juice becoming intoxicating and impure.

Core Idea
The term 'khamr' is linguistically exclusive to grape wine; the Hanafi school disputes whether foam gushing or mere strength triggers prohibition.

This passage clarifies that the Arabic word 'khamr' is not a general term for all intoxicants but specifically refers to grape wine, as agreed by lexicographers. It then presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa requires a physical sign (foam gushing) for grape juice to become intoxicating and impure, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that mere strength (ishtidad) suffices. Understanding this nuance is crucial for applying the rules of impurity and prohibition in sales and other transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic scope
The author asserts that 'khamr' by linguistic consensus is specific to a particular drink (grape wine). Understand why qiyas (analogy) does not apply in language, so the term cannot be extended to all intoxicants.
Consider the example given: a vessel is not called a bottle (qarurah) just because water settles in it.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute
Two positions: Abu Hanifa requires qadhf al-zabad (gushing of foam) for the juice to become intoxicating and impure. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider ishtidad (becoming strong/intoxicating) sufficient without foam.
This dispute affects the timing of khiyar (option) and najasah rulings.
3
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: at what point does the juice become najis (impure) and prohibited? Under Abu Hanifa's view, if juice intoxicates but has not gushed foam, it may still be considered pure (though drinking it would still be haram?).
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view as per later Hanafi authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت هذا الاسم خص هذا الشراب بإجماع أهل اللغة ولا نقول : إنّ كلَّ مسكر خمر لاشتقاقه من مخامرة العقل فإنَّ اللغة لا يجري فيها القياس فلا يُسمى الدن قارورة لقرار الماء فيه ورعاية الوضع الأول ليست لصحة الإطلاق بل لترجيح الوضع وقد حققناه في التنقيح وقذف الزبد قول أبي حنيفة وعندهما إذا اشتد صار مسكراً لا يشترط قذف الزبد
I say: This name (khamr) is specific to this drink by consensus of the linguists, and we do not say that every intoxicant is khamr due to its derivation from mukhamara (covering) of the intellect, because analogy does not apply in language, so a vessel is not called a bottle due to water settling in it, and consideration of the original coinage is not for correctness of usage but for preference of coinage, and we have verified this in al-Tanqih. And the gushing of foam is the statement of Abu Hanifa, while according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), when it becomes strong and intoxicating, the gushing of foam is not a condition.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage discusses the linguistic definition of khamr (wine) and the Hanafi internal dispute regarding when grape juice becomes intoxicating and thus impure. The author begins by asserting that the term 'khamr' is exclusively applied to wine made from grapes, based on the consensus of lexicographers. He rejects the notion that every intoxicant can be called khamr by linguistic analogy, since language is not based on qiyas. The word 'khamr' derives from 'mukhamara' (covering the intellect), but this meaning does not extend to all intoxicating drinks linguistically. Then he presents the ruling: the condition for wine becoming intoxicating according to Abu Hanifa is the gushing of foam (after it becomes strong), whereas according to the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it becomes intoxicating once it becomes strong (i.e., when it ferments and reaches the point of intoxication), and the gushing of foam is not required. The mufta bihi (relied upon) position is that of Abu Hanifa, as stated in other sources. This distinction affects when the prohibitions and impurities apply.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Specifically grape wine, not any intoxicant, by linguistic consensus.
قذف الزبد (qadhf al-zabad): The gushing of foam (from grape juice when it ferments).
اشتداد (ishtidad): Becoming strong (in the context of fermentation, reaching a point of intoxication).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the condition for grape juice to become impermissible and impure?
A) When it becomes strong (ishtadda)
B) When it gushes with foam (qadhf al-zabad)
C) When it reaches the point of intoxication
D) When two-thirds of it evaporates

Key Takeaway
The term 'khamr' is linguistically restricted to grape wine, and the Hanafi mufta bihi requires foam gushing for it to become impure.

The Ten Rulings of Khamr and Definition of Tila'
A list of ten rulings on khamr and definition of tila'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ten rulings of khamr, define tila' according to the Hanafi school, and justify the complete prohibition and impurity of khamr using the Quranic term 'rijs' and consensus.

Core Idea
Khamr is entirely haram and najis; its ten rulings eliminate any permissible use.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya enumerates the severe legal consequences of khamr (wine) in the Hanafi school. It begins by defining tila'—cooked grape juice—and then lists ten rulings that establish its absolute prohibition and impurity. The author also refutes a minority view that only the intoxicating part is forbidden, grounding the Hanafi position in the Quranic term 'rijs' and the consensus of the Ummah.

Guided Path
1
Define tila' and identify its legal status
Understand the definition of tila' as grape juice cooked until less than two-thirds of it has evaporated. This substance is classified as khamr and therefore subject to all ten rulings.
There is a minor intra-Hanafi dispute on the exact fraction; here the majority view is given.
2
List the ten rulings
Enumerate each ruling from the passage: (1) tila' definition, (2) it is impure (najis), (3) it is entirely haram, (4) its considerer becomes kafir, (5) its value falls away, (6) it is not property, (7) benefiting from it is haram, (8) drinker receives hadd even if not intoxicated, (9) cooking does not remove impurity, (10) it may be turned into vinegar, contrary to al-Shafi'i.
The rulings are not numbered in the text; this enumeration is derived.
3
Identify the proof for complete prohibition
Locate the proof: Allah called khamr 'rijs' (abomination) in the Quran, and the Ummah has consensus (ijma) that it is impure and forbidden entirely.
The refutation of those who limit prohibition to intoxication is based on this proof.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah of 'rijs'
Explain how the word 'rijs' indicates that the substance itself is impure and forbidden, not just the state of intoxication. The term refers to the physical entity.
This is a qat'i dalala in Hanafi usul.
5
Note the ikhtilaf with al-Shafi'i on takhleel
Contrast the Hanafi allowance of turning khamr into vinegar (takhleel) with al-Shafi'i's prohibition. The Hanafi view permits it under certain conditions, while al-Shafi'i holds that even the vinegar derived from a prohibited substance remains impure.
This is a practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) affecting trade and use.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه عشرة أحكام. كالطلاء وهو ماء عنب قد طُبخَ فذهب أقل من ثلثيه وغلظاً نجاسة ومن الناس من قال السكر منها حرام وهذا مدفوع بأن الله تعالى سماها رجساً وعليه انعقد إجماع الأمة ثمّ يكفر مستحلها وسقط تقومها لا ماليتها عنها ويحرم الانتفاع بها ويحد شاربها وإن لم يسكر ولا يؤثر فيها الطبخ ويجوز تخليلها خلافا للشافعي
These are ten rulings: like tila' - and it is grape water that has been cooked until less than two-thirds of it has gone - and in terms of severity, it is an impurity. Some people said that only the intoxicating part of it is forbidden, but this is refuted because Allah called it 'rijs' (abomination) and the consensus of the Ummah has been established upon that. Then its considerer becomes an unbeliever. Its value (for compensation) falls away, but not its property (i.e., it has no monetary value). It is unlawful to benefit from it. Its drinker is punished with the hadd punishment even if he did not become intoxicated. Cooking it does not affect its impurity. It is permissible to turn it into vinegar, contrary to al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage enumerates ten legal rulings pertaining to khamr (wine) and defines 'tila'' (a cooked grape juice). The first ruling defines tila' as grape juice cooked until less than two-thirds of it evaporates; this is the Hanafi position (though there is a dispute with other sources). The second ruling establishes that the entire substance is impure (najis ghaliz). The author refutes those (like some scholars) who claim only the intoxicating part is prohibited, citing the Quranic verse that calls it 'rijs' (abomination) and the consensus (ijma) of the Ummah. The subsequent rulings: (1) the one who considers it permissible (mustahill) becomes an unbeliever (kafir), (2) it has no monetary value (i.e., compensation for damage is not required, but the property itself is not considered valuable), (3) any benefit from it is prohibited, (4) the drinker receives the hadd punishment even if not intoxicated, (5) cooking it does not remove its impurity, and (6) it is permissible to turn it into vinegar (takhleel), in contrast to Imam al-Shafi'i who prohibits it. These rulings highlight the severe status of khamr in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
طلاء (tila'): Grape juice cooked until less than two-thirds of it has evaporated.
رجس (rijs): Filth or abomination; used in Quran to describe khamr, indicating it is impure in substance.
تخليل (takhleel): The process of turning khamr into vinegar.

Key Takeaway
Khamr is entirely haram and najis; the ten rulings reinforce its absolute prohibition and impurity, supported by the Quran and unanimous consensus.

The Hanafi Ruling on Fermented Date and Raisin Infusions
Dispute over permissibility of fermented date/raisin infusions with inter-madhhab proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on intoxicating nabidh, identify the opposing views of al-Awzāʿī and Shuraik, and explain the Hanafi response to the Quranic verse 16:67.

Core Idea
Date and raisin infusions become haram when intoxicating; Hanafis rebut permissive views with hadith and ijma.

This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute over the permissibility of fermented date and raisin infusions (naqīʿ). The Hanafi school prohibits them once they become intoxicating, while al-Awzāʿī permits tilāʾ and raisin infusion, and Shuraik permits sakar. You will learn the Hanafi proofs based on a hadith and the consensus of the Companions, and how the Hanafis interpret the Quranic verse that the opponents cite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act under discussion? It is the infusion (naqīʿ) of dates and raisins, raw (uncooked). The Hanafi ruling: these become haram when they boil, become strong (ferment), and – according to Abu Hanifa – throw up foam.
Abu Hanifa's conditions are: ghalyan (boiling), ishtidad (strength), and qadhf bil-zabad (throwing foam). The Sahibayn only require ishtidad.
2
Locate the opposing views
Al-Awzāʿī permits tilāʾ (cooked wine, bādhiq) and raisin infusion absolutely. Shuraik permits sakar (date infusion) absolutely.
Both opponents rely on Quran 16:67.
3
Analyze the opponent's dalil
The opponents cite Quran 16:67: 'You take from it sakar and goodly provision.' They argue that sakar (an intoxicating drink from dates) is made permissible by this verse.
Sakar is interpreted by al-Jawharī and al-Hidāyah as date nabīdh.
4
Study the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response to the verse is threefold: (1) the verse is abrogated (mansūkhah) by later revelation; (2) sakar refers to nabīdh that is permissible only when not intoxicating; (3) the verse is a rebuke, not a permission – i.e., 'You take from it [forbidden] sakar and leave goodly provision.'
The reconciliation between verse and hadith is crucial for Hanafi usul.
5
Examine the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'Khamr is from these two trees' (referring to dates and grapes) and the consensus (ijmāʿ) of the Companions. The hadith extends the prohibition of khamr to intoxicating infusions from these sources.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qatiʿ al-dalalah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وحرمة الخمر أقوى، فيكفر مستحلها فقط. [۱] : أي بطبخ ماء العنب حتى يذهب ثلثاه وبقي ثلثه ، ثم يوضع حتى يغلي ويشتد ويقذف بالزبد ، وكذا إن صب فيه الماء حتى يرق بعدما ذهب ثلثاه " ، ثمّ يطبخ أدنى طبخة، ثمّ يتركُ إلى أن يغلي ويشتد ويقذف بالزبد [۲] وجواب الآية : إنّها منسوخة على مذهب الحنفي وغيره، كما صرح به في الكشاف»، أو نقول : السَّكَرُ النبيذ، وهو عصير العنب والزبيب، والتمر إذا بقي ثلاثة حلال عند الأعظم إلى حد السكر مجتمعه، بهذه الآية. كذا في «الكشاف». ويحمل السكر المذكور في الآية على هذا توفيقاً بين الآية والحديث ، وقيل : المراد منه التوبيخ لا الامتنان ، يعني : أنتم لسفاهتكم تتَّخذون منه سكراً حراماً، وتتركون رزقاً حسناً، قاله الجلبي (٢) قوله : وحلّ المثلث العنبي..الخ ؛ أي ما لم يبلغ حد السكر ؛ لأن المقصود بیان تغيير حكمه، وهو حرمة الفطرة منه بتغير معنى الخمر، وإنما خص بالذكر المثلث العنبي ؛ لأنّ ما عداه صار حلالاً بأدنى طبيخة ، وإن لم يذهب عشره، كما يفصح منه قوله : ونبيذ التمر ... الخ . على المثلث ، قاله الجلبي عطفاً . (A) [١] قوله : بعدما ذهب ثلثاه ؛ لأنّ صب الماء لا يزيد إلا ضعفاً، بخلاف ما إذا صب قبل الطبخ ؛ لأنَّ الماء يذهب أولا عنده للطافته، أو يذهب منهما، فلا يكون الذاهب ثلثي ماء العنب. قاله الجلبي (۳)
And the infusion of dates, and the infusion of raisins, raw, when they boil and become strong, and the infusion of dates: i.e., sakar, and the infusion of raisins raw, when they boil and become strong, the pronoun refers to the tilā' and the infusion of dates and the infusion of raisins. And according to al-Awzā‘ī, the tilā‘, which is bādhiq, is permissible, and likewise the infusion of raisins. And according to Shuraik ibn ‘Abdillāh, sakar is permissible, due to His saying: 'You take from it sakar and goodly provision.' (1) [1] His saying: 'infusion of dates... etc.'; some commentators said: the two infusions are in the genitive case, conjoined to tilā', and it is possible they are in the nominative case, conjoined to khamr, as my brother Jalabī said. [2] His saying: 'i.e., sakar'; it is with two fathas. al-Jawharī interpreted it as date nabīdh. And in al-Hidāyah (3): sakar is that which is from the water of dates; i.e., ripe dates. In al-‘Ināyah: Indeed he interpreted dates as ripe dates because that which is taken from dates is called date nabīdh except sakar, and it is permissible according to Abū Hanīfah and Abū Yūsuf (may Allah have mercy on them). Thus between al-Jawharī's statement and al-Hidāyah there is a type of disagreement, so reflect. Jalabī said it. [3] His saying: 'raw'; is a state (ḥāl) of the two infusions; to indicate the reality, not for restriction, because the absence of cooking is considered in the concept of infusion (naqī‘) in the well-known view, just as its presence is considered in the concept of nabīdh. And he brought this state in the dual form, while the state of the two nabīdhs was in the singular, where he said: 'cooked', although each of them is about two things, because the two infusions are both mentioned, but of the two nabīdhs only one is mentioned. Know that these drinks are only prohibited according to Abū Hanīfah (may Allah have mercy on him) when they boil, become strong, and throw up foam; and according to the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad), mere fermentation suffices, as in khamr. (And the prohibition of khamr is stronger, so only the one who deems it permissible becomes a disbeliever.) [1] That is: by cooking grape water until two-thirds go and one-third remains, then it is left until it boils, becomes strong, and throws up foam, and likewise if water is poured into it until it becomes thin after two-thirds have gone, then it is cooked a minimal cooking, then left until it boils, becomes strong, and throws up foam. [2] And the answer to the verse: it is abrogated according to the Ḥanafī school and others, as stated in al-Kashshāf, or we say: sakar is nabīdh, which is the juice of grapes, raisins, and dates, when three remain, permissible according to the great imam up to the limit of intoxication; gathered, by this verse. Thus in al-Kashshāf. And sakar mentioned in the verse is interpreted accordingly, reconciling the verse and the hadith. And it is said: its meaning is rebuke not favor, i.e., you, due to your foolishness, take from it forbidden sakar and leave goodly provision. Jalabī said it. (2) His saying: 'And the permissibility of the cubic (muthallath) of grapes...etc.'; i.e., as long as it has not reached the limit of intoxication, because the purpose is to express the change of its ruling, and it is the prohibition of the innate nature from it due to the change of the meaning of khamr. And he specified the cubic of grapes because everything else becomes permissible with the slightest cooking, even if one-tenth has not gone, as is evident from his saying: 'and the date nabīdh...etc.' conjoined to the cubic. Jalabī said it. (A) [1] His saying: 'after its two-thirds have gone'; because pouring water only adds weakness, unlike if poured before cooking, because the water goes first in his view due to its lightness, or it goes from both, so the one gone is not two-thirds of the grape water. Jalabī said it. (3)
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exploration of the rulings on intoxicating drinks (ashribah), focusing on the infusion of dates (naqī‘ al-tamr) and raisins (naqī‘ al-zabīb). The author, a Hanafi commentator, begins by stating the core ruling: these infusions, when raw (i.e., uncooked), become forbidden once they boil and become strong (i.e., ferment). The commentary clarifies that the term 'raw' (niyyayn) indicates the natural state of these infusions, as being uncooked is inherent in the concept of naqī‘. The discussion then shifts to the positions of non-Hanafi scholars: al-Awzā‘ī permits tilā‘ (a type of cooked wine called bādhiq) and raisin infusion, while Shuraik permits sakar (date infusion), both citing the Quranic verse 16:67, 'You take from it sakar and goodly provision.' The Hanafi school responds with two proofs: first, a hadith stating 'Khamr is from these two trees' (referring to dates and grapes), and second, the consensus (ijmā‘) of the Companions on the prohibition. The commentator then addresses the interpretation of the verse, offering that it is abrogated (mansūkhah) or that sakar refers specifically to nabīdh that is permissible until it becomes intoxicating. This is a typical Hanafi reconciliation between the verse and the hadith. Following this, the passage discusses the conditions for prohibition according to Abu Hanifah and his two companions (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad). Abu Hanifah requires three signs: boiling, becoming strong, and throwing up foam; the two companions consider mere fermentation (ishtidād) sufficient, as with wine (khamr). The commentary then explains the ruling on muthallath (cooked wine that has had two-thirds evaporated): it is permissible as long as it has not reached the intoxicating limit. This is because cooking changes its nature, removing it from the category of khamr. The passage ends with a technical note on the process of making muthallath: if water is added after two-thirds have evaporated, the resulting mixture is still considered muthallath, but if water is added before cooking, the calculation becomes different. Throughout, the commentator relies on earlier authorities like al-Jawharī and al-Hidāyah, and notes from Jalabī. The ruling is clear: these drinks are forbidden only when they cause intoxication, and the Hanafi school distinguishes between the severity of khamr (which is prohibited in any quantity) and other fermented drinks (which are allowed in small amounts if not intoxicating). The authority weight is from the later Hanafi tradition (fatawa mutakhkhirin) as indicated by the references to al-Hidāyah and al-Kashshāf.

Key Terms
نقيع (naqī‘): An infusion or soak of dates or raisins in water without cooking; it is considered raw.
طلاء (tilā‘): Cooked grape juice, also called bādhiq, which is mildly cooked and becomes strong.
سكر (sakar): An intoxicating drink made from dates; in the verse it is interpreted as nabīdh.
مثلث (muthallath): Grape juice cooked until two-thirds evaporate, leaving one-third; it is permissible even if strong, as long as not intoxicating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When it ferments (ishtadda) only
C) When it boils, becomes strong, and throws up foam
D) When it is consumed in large quantity

Key Takeaway
Fermented date and raisin infusions become haram when intoxicating, as proven by the hadith 'Khamr is from these two trees' and the consensus of the Companions.

Ruling on Dardi al-Khamr and the Hadd Dispute
States the ruling on dardi al-khamr and the dispute with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school does not impose the hadd punishment for drinking dardi al-khamr without intoxication, and distinguish this from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Dardi al-khamr is haram but its hadd requires intoxication; the lees do not invite more drinking, unlike pure wine.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the ruling on dardi al-khamr (lees of wine). While the matn says it is 'makruh', the commentator explains this means 'haram' because it contains parts of wine. The key dispute with the Shafi'i school revolves around whether drinking dardi without intoxication incurs the hadd punishment. The Hanafi school argues that the hadd only applies upon actual intoxication, as a small amount of dardi does not lead to more consumption due to natural aversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on dardi
Read the matn statement: 'Wa kariha shurbu durdi al-khamr'. The commentator clarifies that 'karahah' here means 'hurmah' because it contains parts of wine. Note the use of 'karahah' due to lack of a definitive text.
2
Understand the hadd principle for pure wine
For pure wine, even a small amount triggers the hadd because a little wine leads to much (qawluhu: qalil al-khamr yad'u ila al-kathir). This is the established rule.
3
Examine the Shafi'i counter-position
Al-Shafi'i holds that since dardi is a part of wine, drinking it should incur the hadd, just as a little wine does. Identify his dalil: he drank a part of wine.
Opposing school: Imam al-Shafi'i
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response contains two proofs: (1) A little dardi does not call to much because of natural aversion, so it resembles other non-intoxicating drinks where hadd only applies upon intoxication. (2) The predominant part of dardi is lees (thifl), so it is analogous to wine overwhelmed by water, where the ruling follows the predominant element.
Qiyas: dardi is like non-wine drinks; also like diluted wine.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: A person who drinks dardi without intoxication is not subject to the fixed hadd (80 lashes) according to the Hanafi school, but would receive it according to the Shafi'i school.
Mufta bihi: Hanafi position is relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكرة شربُ دُرْدِي الخمر، والامتشاط به، ولا يحد شاربه بلا سكر. المراد بالكراهة الحرمة؛ لأن فيه أجزاء الخمر، إلا أنه ذكر لفظ الكراهة لا الحرمة؛ لعدم النص القاطع فيه. ولا يحد شاربه بلا سكر؛ فإن في الخمر إنما يحد بشرب القليل؛ لأن قليل الخمر يدعو إلى الكثير، ولا كذلك في الدردي فاعتبر حقيقة السكر. فإنما نهيتكم ليوسع موسعكم على فقيركم، وعن النبيذ في الدباء والحنتم والمزفّت فاشربوا في كل ظرف، فإن الظرف لا يحل شيئا ولا يحرمه، ولا تشربوا المسكر وقال ذلك بعد ما أخبر عن النهي عنه، فكان ناسخا له. هذا عندنا: أما عند الشافعي فيحد؛ لأنه شرب جزء من الخمر. ولنا: إن قليله لا يدعو إلى كثيره، لما في الطباع من النتوءة عنه، فكان ناقصا فأشبه غير الخمر من الأشربة ولا حد فيها إلا بالسكر، ولأن الغالب عليه الثفل، فصار كما إذا غلب عليه الماء بالامتزاج.
And it is disliked (karahah) to drink the lees (dardi) of wine, and to use it for combing the hair, and its drinker is not subject to the hadd penalty unless intoxicated. What is meant by karahah is prohibition (hurmah), because it contains parts of wine, except that the term karahah is used instead of hurmah due to the absence of a definitive text on it. And its drinker is not subjected to hadd without intoxication, because in wine, hadd is imposed for drinking even a little, since a little wine calls to much, but not so with dardi, so actual intoxication is considered. Indeed, I forbade you to expand upon your poor, and concerning nabidh in gourds, green jars, and pitch-lined vessels, drink in any vessel, for the vessel does not make something lawful or unlawful, and do not drink intoxicants. He said that after informing about the prohibition, so it was abrogating. This is our position: as for al-Shafi'i, he imposes hadd because he drank a part of wine. Our proof: a little of it does not call to much, because of the aversion in human nature, so it is deficient, thus resembling non-wine drinks in which there is no hadd except for intoxication, and because the predominant part is lees, so it is like when water overwhelms through mixing.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal classification (hukm) of dardi al-khamr—the lees or sediment left after wine has been clarified. The author begins by restating the matn that drinking dardi is 'makruh' and combing hair with it is also makruh, and that the drinker is not subject to the hadd punishment unless he becomes intoxicated. The commentator immediately clarifies that 'karahah' here means 'hurmah' (prohibition), not mere dislike. The reason karahah is used instead of hurmah is that there is no definitive text (nass qati) explicitly declaring this specific substance haram; rather, it is haram by virtue of containing parts of wine, which is itself haram. The ruling on hadd is then explained: for pure wine, even a small amount triggers the hadd because the Shari'ah recognises that a little wine leads to much, but for dardi, the hadd is only applied if actual intoxication occurs. This distinction is based on a principle of gradual legislation: initially, prohibitions are strict to encourage abandonment, but once the goal is achieved, the strictness is relaxed. The commentator then cites a hadith where the Prophet initially forbade nabidh in certain vessels, then later permitted any vessel but prohibited intoxicants, indicating abrogation. This shows the legal principle that the container does not change the ruling of the content. Then the passage presents the inter-madhhab dispute: our Hanafi position is that no hadd is imposed for drinking dardi without intoxication, while al-Shafi'i imposes hadd because it is a part of wine. The Hanafi proof is twofold: first, a little dardi does not call to much due to natural aversion, so it is like other non-wine drinks where hadd only applies upon intoxication; second, the predominant component is lees, so it is analogous to wine that has been overwhelmingly mixed with water, where the ruling follows the predominant element.

Key Terms
دُرْدِي (durdi): The lees or sediment that settles at the bottom of wine after fermentation and clarification.
الكراهة (al-karahah): The term used in the matn to indicate the ruling on dardi; the commentator clarifies it means legal prohibition (hurmah) due to the absence of a definitive text.
الحد (al-hadd): A fixed corporal punishment prescribed by the Shari'ah for specific offenses; here it refers to the hadd for drinking wine (80 lashes for a freeman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on drinking dardi al-khamr (lees of wine) without becoming intoxicated?
A) It is halal and no punishment is incurred.
B) It is makruh tanzihi and no hadd is imposed.
C) It is haram but no hadd is imposed unless intoxication occurs.
D) It is haram and the hadd for wine is imposed regardless of intoxication.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school differentiates between pure wine and its lees: both are haram, but the hadd penalty requires actual intoxication for dardi because it does not lead to further consumption.

Ruling on Eating an Animal with Severe Cuts or Severed Head
States rulings on eating an animal with severe cuts or severed head.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating an animal that has been severely cut or had its head severed, and apply the condition of life possibility to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
If the cut renders the animal unable to survive beyond normal slaughter time, the whole is halal; otherwise, only the cut part is haram.

This lesson examines a detailed Hanafi ruling regarding the permissibility of eating an animal that has been severely cut, such that a part is separated or the head is severed. The ruling hinges on whether the animal could have survived after the cut for a longer time than a properly slaughtered animal. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the hadith 'What is cut off from a living animal is dead' and its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the cut by proportion
Identify whether the cut separates the animal into parts with specific proportions (e.g., one-third on head side, two-thirds on tail side, or half head cut).
The proportion determines whether life is possible in the remaining part.
2
Evaluate life possibility
Determine whether the animal could survive longer than a normally slaughtered animal after the cut, considering the location and severity.
If survival is impossible, the cut is equivalent to immediate slaughter.
3
Apply the hadith and its exception
The hadith 'What is cut off from a living animal is dead' makes the cut part carrion, but it does not apply if the animal cannot survive past the normal slaughter time.
In such cases, the entire animal is treated as properly slaughtered.
4
Rule for the cut part vs. the remainder
If life was possible in the remaining part, the cut part is haram while the rest is halal; if life was impossible, the whole is halal.
This distinction applies to cuts of limbs, head, or body.
5
Consider special cases (striking neck, game limb)
For striking the neck, front strikes are halal but makruh; back strikes are only halal if the animal survives until jugular veins are cut. For game, a limb that remains attached by skin is considered whole if healing is conceivable.
These cases reinforce the principle that the ruling depends on the meaning of separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وإن ضرب عنق شاة فأبان رأسَها تحلّ ؛ لقطع الأوداج، ويكره لما فيه من زيادة الألم بإبلاغه النخاع، وإن ضربَها من قبل القفاء إن ماتت قبل قطع الأوداج لا تحل وإن لم يمت حتى قطع الأوداج حلت كما مر في «كتاب الذبائح».
ولو ضرب صيداً فقطع يده أو رجله ولم ينفصل حتى مات إن كان يتوهم التئامه
واندماله حل أكله ؛ لأنه بمنزلة سائر أجزائه، وإن كان لا يتوهم أن يبقى معلقاً بجلده حل ما سواه دونه ؛ لوجودِ الإبانة معنى، والعبرة للمعاني.
(۱) تكملة البحر (٨: ٢٦١ - ٢٦٢).
And if it is cut into thirds with most of it on the side of the tail: meaning he cut it into two pieces, such that one-third is on the head side and two-thirds on the tail side, (or if half of its head or more is cut, or if it is cut in half) all of it is eaten; because in these forms its life cannot surpass the life of the slaughtered animal, so the saying 'what is cut off from a living animal is dead' does not apply to it, contrary to when two-thirds are on the head side and one-third on the tail side due to the possibility of life in the two-thirds surpassing the life of the slaughtered animal, and contrary to when less than half the head is cut due to the possibility of life in the two-thirds surpassing the life of the slaughtered animal. From it: [1] His saying: 'And if he cuts...' etc.; meaning all of it is eaten. He said in Takmalah al-Bahr: 'Because what is separated is alive in form not in ruling, since its safety and remaining alive after this wound is not conceivable, so it becomes a valid slaughter immediately, so eating it is permissible, just as if its head was separated in voluntary slaughter.' The commentator indicated with his saying: 'so it does not apply to it...' etc. Contrary to if a hand, foot, thigh, or one-third on the leg side, or less than half the head is cut, where the separated part is prohibited, and what is cut from it is permissible; because the continuation of life in the remaining part is conceivable. He indicated with his saying: 'due to the possibility of life...' etc. And if one strikes the neck of a sheep and separates its head, it is permissible; due to cutting the jugular veins, and it is disliked due to the increased pain of reaching the spinal cord. And if one strikes it from the back of the neck, if it dies before cutting the jugular veins it is not permissible, and if it does not die until the jugular veins are cut, it is permissible as mentioned in the 'Book of Slaughtered Animals'. And if one strikes game and cuts its hand or foot and it does not separate until it dies: if healing and fusion is conceivable, its eating is permissible; because it is like its other parts. And if it is not conceivable that it remains hanging by its skin, what is other than it is permissible and it is not; due to the occurrence of separation in meaning, and consideration is given to meanings.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the permissibility of eating an animal that has been severely cut or has its head severed, with specific conditions. The author begins by stating that if an animal is cut into two pieces such that more than half (the larger portion) is on the tail side and the smaller on the head side, or if half or more of its head is cut, or if it is cut in half entirely, then the entire animal is permissible to eat. The reasoning is that in these scenarios, the animal cannot survive longer than the usual life of a properly slaughtered animal; therefore, the hadith 'that which is cut off from a living animal is dead' does not apply because the separation occurs in a manner that constitutes immediate slaughter. Conversely, if the larger portion is on the head side (i.e., more than half remains with the head), or if less than half of the head is cut, the separated part is prohibited because life is conceivable in the remaining larger portion. The commentator then clarifies that the ruling applies to all cuts: for example, if a hand, foot, thigh, or a portion near the legs is cut off, that cut part is haram, but the rest of the animal is halal. The passage also addresses a separate case: striking a sheep's neck such that the head is severed; this is halal because the jugular veins are cut, but it is disliked (makruh) due to the added pain. Striking from the back of the neck is only halal if the animal does not die before the jugular veins are cut. Finally, regarding game, if a limb is cut but remains attached by skin until death, if healing is conceivable, the whole is halal; if not, the detached part is haram while the rest is halal, because the meaning of 'separation' is considered. The footnote cites Takmalah al-Bahr as a source.

Key Terms
إبانة (ibaanah): Separation or cutting off of a limb or part of the animal.
ذكاة (dhakaat): The proper method of slaughtering an animal (cutting the throat and jugular veins).
ميتة (maytah): Carrion – an animal that died without proper slaughter or whose part is cut off while alive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter cuts a deer's leg and it remains attached by skin until death, and healing is conceivable, what is the ruling on eating the deer?
A) The whole deer is haram.
B) The leg is halal and the rest is haram.
C) The leg is haram and the rest is halal.
D) The whole deer is halal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the permissibility of eating an animal with severe cuts depends on whether the life span after the cut is shorter than that of a properly slaughtered animal; if so, the whole is halal; otherwise, only the cut part is haram.

Who Bears the Costs of a Mortgaged Property?
Ruling on allocation of maintenance costs for a mortgaged property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default rule for bearing maintenance costs of a mortgaged property and identify the exceptions when the mortgagee bears specific costs.

Core Idea
The mortgagor bears all preservation costs; the mortgagee bears certain costs only when in possession or for the storage room.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah clarifies a fundamental rule in Hanafi mortgage law: who pays for the upkeep of the mortgaged property? While the mortgagor is primarily responsible, there are important exceptions when the property is in the mortgagee's hands. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly assigning expenses in mortgage transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
The passage first states the general rule: all preservation costs (like feeding, clothing, shepherding, and irrigation) are on the mortgagor. This is the baseline.
These are called 'mu'n al-tabqit' – necessary expenses to preserve the property's substance and utility.
2
Identify the exception for costs when the mortgagee is in possession
When the property has left the mortgagor's hand and is in the mortgagee's custody, certain costs (like reward for a runaway slave or medical treatment for a wound) fall on the mortgagee, but only if the property's value equals exactly the debt.
If the value exceeds the debt, costs are divided proportionally based on the secured and trust portions.
3
Understand the proportional division when value exceeds debt
If the property's value is greater than the debt, costs like medical treatment are divided: the portion corresponding to the secured amount is on the mortgagee, and the remainder (trust portion) is on the mortgagor.
This proportional rule does not apply to all costs – see next step.
4
Recognize the special rule for storage room rent
Unlike other costs, the rent for a storage room (ujrat bayt al-hifz) is entirely on the mortgagee regardless of whether the value equals or exceeds the debt. The reason is that the mortgagee's right of retention (habs) covers the whole property, so he benefits fully from the storage.
This is mentioned explicitly as 'bikhilaf' (unlike) to other costs.
5
Confirm the obligation level
The passage concludes that the default obligation of preservation is 'wajib' upon the mortgagor, and the same applies to parts of the property in his possession.
The term 'wajib' indicates a binding religious-legal duty, though not at the level of fard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المرتهن إن خرج من يده ، كجعل الآبق ، فهو على المرتهن إذا كان قيمة الرهن مثل الدين، وكذا مؤنة ردّ جزء من الرهن إلى يد المرتهن، كمداواة الجرح إذا كان قيمته مثل الدين.
أما إذا كان قيمته أكثر منه فيقسم على المضمون والأمانة ، فما هو مضمون فعلى المرتهن، وما هو أمانةٌ فعلى الراهن، وهذا بخلاف أجرة بيت الحفظ، فإنّ تمامه على المرتهن وإن كان قيمة المرهون أكثر من الدين ؛ لأنَّ وجوب ذلك : الحبس، وحق الحبس في الكلّ ثابت له.
بسبب
(وعلى الراهن مُونُ تبقيته وإصلاح منافعه كنفقة رهيه وكسوته، وأجر راعيه، وظئر ولد الرهن ، وسقي البستان والقيام بأموره). واجب عليه، فكذا جزؤه، وفي المداواة فقط الجزء المراد. كذا في شروح «الهداية». قاله الجلبي

And upon the mortgagor is the expense of preserving it and repairing its benefits, such as the maintenance of his mortgage and its clothing, and the wage of its shepherd and the wet nurse of the child of the mortgage, and watering the orchard and attending to its affairs.
If it leaves his hand, like the reward for a runaway slave, then it is upon the mortgagee if the value of the mortgage equals the debt. Similarly, the cost of returning a part of the mortgage to the hand of the mortgagee, like treating a wound if its value equals the debt.
But if its value is more than that, then it is divided according to the secured part and the trust part. What is secured is upon the mortgagee, and what is a trust is upon the mortgagor. This is unlike the rent of the storage room, for its entirety is upon the mortgagee even if the value of the mortgaged property is more than the debt, because the obligation for that is the right of retention, and the right of retention is established for him over the whole.
Due to that, (And upon the mortgagor is the expense of preserving it and repairing its benefits, such as the maintenance of his mortgage and its clothing, and the wage of its shepherd, and the wet nurse of the child of the mortgage, and watering the orchard and attending to its affairs). [1] His saying: like the reward for a runaway slave, what is due to the worker for his work, then its usage became prevalent for the wage of returning a runaway slave. Jalabi. [2] His saying: and the wet nurse of the child of the mortgage, etc., with kasra of the ẓāʾ al-muʿjama, and sukūn of the hamza, and its end is a rāʾ muhmala: the one who undertakes the upbringing of the child.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on al-Hidayah, detailing the allocation of expenses for maintaining a mortgaged property (al-rahin). The default ruling is that the mortgagor (al-rahin) bears all preservation costs—such as feeding, clothing, wages for a shepherd or wet nurse, irrigation, and general care—as these are necessary to maintain the property's value. However, if the mortgagee (al-murtahin) takes possession of the property and its value equals exactly the amount of the debt, then certain expenses like the reward for recapturing a runaway slave or medical treatment for a wound fall on the mortgagee, because he is the one benefiting from the security. If the property's value exceeds the debt, expenses are apportioned: the portion corresponding to the secured amount is borne by the mortgagee, and the excess (trust portion) by the mortgagor. An exception is the rent for a storage room (ujrat bayt al-hifz), which is entirely on the mortgagee regardless of value disparity, since the right of retention (al-habs) covers the whole property. The passage concludes that the general obligation of maintenance is wajib upon the mortgagor, and the same applies to the part of the property in his possession; the details about medical treatment only concern the part intended for recovery. This is the established position as recorded in the commentaries of al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
مون (mu'n): Expenses or costs incurred for preservation and maintenance of the mortgaged property.
جعل الآبق (ju'l al-ābiq): The reward or wage given to someone who returns a runaway slave, used here as an example of an expense that may fall on the mortgagee.
ظئر (ẓi'r): A wet nurse employed to care for the child of the mortgagor, provided as an example of maintenance costs.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The mortgagee, the person who receives the mortgage as security for a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The mortgagor bears all preservation costs, but the mortgagee bears specific costs (like reward for runaway slave) when in possession at equal value, and storage room rent entirely, regardless of value.

Why Mortgaging a Shared Property (Mushāʿ) is Invalid
Ruling with inter-madhhab dispute on mortgaging a share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that mortgaging an undivided share (mushāʿ) is invalid and evaluate the Shafiʿi counter-argument that it is valid.

Core Idea
A mortgage requires permanent retention; a shared property cannot be permanently retained, so the contract is defective.

In this passage, the author explains the Hanafi position on mortgaging an undivided share. The core issue is the nature of mortgage: is it about eventual sale or about permanent retention? The Hanafi school insists on permanent retention (ḥabs dāʾim), which is impossible with a share. This leads to a clear dispute with Imam al-Shafiʿi, who sees mortgage as a mere prelude to sale.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: mortgaging a share (mushāʿ) is not valid; it is fasid (defective) and if possession is taken, liability for damages attaches.
Note the distinction between fasid and batil – the preferred view is fasid.
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
Imam al-Shafiʿi holds it valid. His proof: the ultimate purpose of mortgage is to sell the property upon default, and a sale of a share is permissible.
Understand that Shafiʿi analysis focuses on the end result rather than the interim security.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis rebut: the mūjib (legal effect) of mortgage is not sale but the establishment of the mortgagee's hand and entitlement to permanent retention (al-ḥabs al-dāʾim). Permanent retention is impossible with a share due to alternating use (muhāyāh).
The argument is rational (dalīl ʿaqlī): the object of the contract is unattainable.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Hanafi law, a lender cannot take a share as security; under Shafiʿi, it is valid. This affects contractual freedom and the security of lenders.
This shows how a theoretical difference impacts actual practice.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on subsequent common ownership
If a property becomes shared after the mortgage (e.g., by inheritance), the original riwāyah says the mortgage is invalidated; Abū Yūsuf holds it continues.
This is a separate but related issue. The preferred view is the original riwāyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يصح رهنه والرهن به وما لا يصح
لا يصح رهن مشاع وثمر على نخل دونه، وزرع أرض ونخل أرض دونها
[١] قوله : باب ما يصح رهنه ؛ لما ذكر مقدّمات مسائل الرهن ذكر في هذا الباب تفصيل ما يجوز رهنه والرهن به وما لا يجوز، إذ التفصيلُ إنّما يكون بعد الإجمال. كذا في «تكملة البحر). [٢]قوله : رهن مشاع ؛ أي لا يصح رهن المشاع ، فظاهره أنه لا فرق بين ما يحتملُ القسمة وما لا يحتمل، قال صاحب «العناية» (۲) : رهن المشاع قابل للقسمة وغيره فاسد يتعلق به الضمان إذا قبض. وقيل : باطل لا يتعلّق به الضمان، وليس بصحيح؛ لأنَّ الباطل منه فيما إذا لم یكن الرهن مالاً ولم يكن المقابل به مضموناً، وما نحن فيه ليس كذلك، بناءً على أنّ القبض شرط تمام العقد لا شرط جوازه ، وقال الشافعي له : يجوز؛ لأنَّ موجبه عنده بيعه، والمشاع لا يمتنع بيعه. ولنا : إنّ موجبه ثبوت يد الاستيفاء، واستحقاق الحبس الدائم، ولا يتصور الحبس الدائم فيه؛ لأنه يبطل بالمهاياة ،قبضه، كأنه رهنه يوماً ويوماً لا؛ ولذا يستوي فيه ما يقبل القسمة وما لا يقبلها، بخلاف الهبة حيث تجوزُ فيما لا يحتمل القسمة؛ لأنَّ موجبها الملك ، ولا يمتنع بالشيوع. ولا يجوز من شريكه أيضاً؛ لأنَّ ثبوت اليد في المشاع لا يتصور؛ ولأنه لو جاز لأمسكه يوماً بحكم الرهن ويوماً بحكم الملك ، فيصير كأنه رهنه يوماً ويوماً لا، بخلاف الإجارة حيث تجوز في المشاع من الشريك؛ لأنَّ حكمها التمكن من الانتفاع لا الحبس، والشريك متمكن من ذلك.
والشيوع الطارئ يمنعُ بقاء الرهن في رواية الأصل، وعن أبي يوسف له لا يمنع؛ لأنَّ حكم البقاء أسهل من الابتداء، فأشبه الهبة.
Chapter: What is valid to mortgage and with what, and what is not valid.
It is not valid to mortgage a shared item (mushāʿ), nor fruit on palm trees without the land, nor crops of land and palms of land without the land.
[1] His saying: Chapter: What is valid to mortgage; since he mentioned the preliminaries of mortgage issues, he mentions in this chapter the details of what is permissible to mortgage and what is not, for detail comes after summary. Thus in 'Takmilat al-Bahr'. [2] His saying: mortgaging a shared item; meaning: it is not valid to mortgage a shared item, and its apparent meaning is that there is no difference between what is divisible and what is not. The author of al-ʿInāyah said: Mortgaging a shared item, whether divisible or not, is fasid (defective) – it entails liability if possession is taken. And it was said: it is batil (void) and entails no liability, but this is not correct; because the batil case is when the mortgage is not property and what is in return is not guaranteed, and our case is not like that, based on the fact that possession is a condition of completion of the contract, not a condition of its validity. Al-Shafiʿi said: It is valid, because its purpose according to him is its sale, and a shared item is not prevented from sale. Our proof: Its purpose is the establishment of the hand of execution (yad al-istīfāʾ) and the entitlement to permanent retention (al-ḥabs al-dāʾim). Permanent retention is inconceivable in it, because it is invalidated by alternating use (al-muhāyāh) and its possession, as if he mortgaged it one day and not the other. Therefore, it is the same whether it is divisible or not, unlike a gift (hiba) which is valid in what is not divisible because its purpose is ownership, and that is not prevented by common ownership. Nor is it valid from one's partner either, because the establishment of a hand in a shared item is inconceivable; and because if it were valid, he would hold it one day by virtue of mortgage and one day by virtue of ownership, so it becomes as if he mortgaged it one day and not the other. This is unlike a lease (ijāra) which is valid in a shared item from a partner, because its ruling is the ability to benefit, not retention, and the partner is able to do that.
Subsequent common ownership (al-shuyūʿ al-ṭāriʾ) prevents the continuation of the mortgage in the original riwāyah. From Abū Yūsuf: it does not prevent, because the ruling of continuation is easier than initiation, so it resembles a gift.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the validity of mortgaging an undivided share (mushāʿ) of property. The Hanafi position is clear: it is not valid, regardless of whether the property is divisible or not. The author explains that the ruling is fasid (defective) and, if possession has been taken, liability for damages attaches. There is a minority view within Hanafism that it is batil (void) from the start, but the preferred view is that it is fasid. The Shafiʿi school, by contrast, holds it valid because their understanding of mortgage is that its primary purpose is ultimately a sale of the property upon default, and a sale of a share is permissible.

Hanafi reasoning (argument): The true objective (mūjib) of a mortgage is not sale but the establishment of a right to hold the property as security (yad al-istīfāʾ) and especially the right to retain it permanently (al-ḥabs al-dāʾim). With an undivided share, permanent retention is impossible because the property is jointly owned; any physical retention would be subject to alternating use (muhāyāh), which defeats the nature of a mortgage (you cannot 'hold' a share in a warehouse one day and not the next). This logic applies equally whether the property is divisible or not—unlike a gift, where ownership can transfer even of a share, because ownership is not impeded by common ownership. Additionally, the mortgage of a share from a co-owner is also invalid: if it were valid, the mortgagee would hold it one day under mortgage and one day under the co-owner's permission, nullifying the permanent retention. This contrasts with a lease (ijāra), where sharing possession of a share is acceptable because the lessee only needs temporary benefit, not permanent retention.

An intra-Hanafi dispute is also mentioned: if a shared mortgage becomes shared later (e.g., after the death of a co-owner), the original riwāyah says the mortgage is invalidated, but Abū Yūsuf holds it is not invalidated, drawing an analogy to a gift where subsequent shares are easier to accommodate than initial ones.

Key Terms
مشاع (mushāʿ): An undivided share in a jointly owned property, i.e., common ownership where each co-owner has a fractional interest but no specific portion is designated.
الحبس الدائم (al-ḥabs al-dāʾim): Permanent retention; the right of the mortgagee to hold the property as security until the debt is repaid.
مهاياة (muhāyāh): Alternating use or division of benefit in time, e.g., who uses the property on which days.
الشيوع الطارئ (al-shuyūʿ al-ṭāriʾ): Subsequent common ownership that arises after the mortgage contract, e.g., by inheritance or purchase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is mortgaging an undivided share (mushāʿ) invalid?
A) Because a share cannot be sold
B) Because the mortgagee cannot achieve permanent retention of a share
C) Because the mortgagor does not own the property
D) Because the contract is impossible to document

Key Takeaway
Mortgage of an undivided share is fasid in Hanafi law because permanent retention, the essential purpose, is impossible with common ownership.

The Invalidity of Pledging Wine and Its Guarantee Consequences
Ruling with rational explanation (ta‘līl)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledging wine, distinguish the guarantee consequences for Muslim and dhimmi parties, and explain the underlying rationale of māl mutaqawwim.

Core Idea
Wine is not property for a Muslim, so a pledge of wine is invalid, but it is property for a dhimmi, affecting guarantee.

This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on pledging wine (khamr) and highlights the crucial distinction between Muslims and dhimmis regarding the legal status of wine. The ruling turns on the concept of māl mutaqawwim (valuable property). For a Muslim, wine is not considered property, so a contract involving wine as a pledge is invalid. However, for a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state), wine is considered property, leading to different guarantee consequences when the wine is destroyed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
Determine what legal act is being ruled upon and what classification it receives. The act is a pledge (rahn) involving wine. The ruling is that such a pledge is invalid (bāṭil).
The invalidity is absolute for Muslims, both as pledgor and pledgee.
2
Locate the Rationale (Ta‘līl)
Identify the reason given for the ruling. The passage explains that the purpose of a pledge is for recovery (istīfā’) and fulfillment (īfā’). A Muslim cannot fulfill the pledge if he is pledgor (since he cannot lawfully own or transfer wine) and cannot recover it if he is pledgee (since wine is not property for him).
This rationale connects to the legal status of wine as non-property for Muslims.
3
Distinguish the Two Scenarios
Separate the case where a Muslim pledges wine to a dhimmi from the case where a dhimmi pledges wine to a Muslim. In the first, the dhimmi does not guarantee the wine to the Muslim; in the second, the Muslim guarantees it to the dhimmi.
The key is whether wine is māl mutaqawwim for the owner (the pledgor).
4
Apply the Principle of Māl Mutaqawwim
Understand that for a dhimmi, wine is māl mutaqawwim, so when a dhimmi pledges wine, the Muslim pledgee must guarantee it (as if he had usurped it). For a Muslim, wine is not property, so no guarantee is due to him.
This mirrors the rule for usurpation (ghaṣb): a dhimmi does not guarantee wine taken from a Muslim, but a Muslim does guarantee wine taken from a dhimmi.
5
Summarize the Guarantee Rules
Consolidate: If a Muslim pledges wine to a dhimmi and it is destroyed, no guarantee is due to the Muslim. If a dhimmi pledges wine to a Muslim and it is destroyed, the Muslim guarantees it to the dhimmi with the lesser of its value or the debt (by analogy to usurpation).
The marginal note adds the detail of the lesser amount.

Source Text

And there is no pledging of wine and taking it as a pledge from a Muslim or a dhimmi for the Muslim, and the Muslim does not guarantee for him if he is a dhimmi mortgagee, and in the opposite there is guarantee.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on pledging wine. The matn states that a Muslim cannot pledge wine nor take wine as a pledge from a Muslim or a dhimmi. If a Muslim pledges wine to a dhimmi and it is destroyed in the dhimmi's possession, the dhimmi does not guarantee it to the Muslim. Conversely, if a dhimmi pledges wine to a Muslim and it is destroyed, the Muslim guarantees it to the dhimmi because wine is considered valuable property (māl mutaqawwim) for the dhimmi but not for the Muslim. The commentary explains that the reason is that a pledge is for the purpose of recovery (istīfā') and fulfillment (īfā'), and a Muslim cannot own wine to fulfill the pledge if he is the pledgor, nor can he recover it if he is the pledgee. The same applies to pork. The marginal note adds that if a dhimmi mortgages wine to a Muslim and it is destroyed, the Muslim guarantees it to the dhimmi with the lesser of its value or the debt, just as he would if he had usurped it. This ruling is based on the principle that wine is not property for a Muslim but is property for a dhimmi.

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Wine, specifically intoxicating beverage made from grapes; considered najis and not property (māl) for Muslims in Hanafi fiqh.
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Valuable property that is legally permissible to benefit from according to Islamic law.
رهن (rahn): A contract where property is put as security for a debt, allowing the creditor to recover the debt from the pledged property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The dhimmi must guarantee the value of the wine to the Muslim.
B) The Muslim must guarantee the value to the dhimmi.
C) No guarantee is due to the Muslim.
D) The dhimmi must guarantee the debt amount.

Key Takeaway
Wine is not māl mutaqawwim for Muslims, making any pledge of wine invalid; but for dhimmis it is property, so they can receive guarantee when their wine is destroyed in a Muslim's possession.

Valid 'Ayn for Pledge: Types and Dispute with Zufar
Ruling on valid 'ayn for pledge and intra-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which types of 'ayn can be validly pledged according to the Hanafi school and explain the Hanafi rebuttal to Zufar's objection.

Core Idea
A pledge is valid with any 'ayn guaranteed by similar or value, even for a promised debt, because the purpose is securing, not exchange.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah clarifies the types of property that can serve as a valid pledge (rahn) in Hanafi fiqh. It presents examples of muḍman bi'l-mithl and muḍman bi'l-qīmah items, then addresses a specific intra-madhhab dispute with Zufar concerning salam, sarf, and muslam fīhi. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the fundamental distinction between a pledge as a security (istīthāq) and a direct exchange (istibdāl).

Guided Path
1
Identify valid 'ayn types
The matn states that a pledge is valid with any 'ayn (specific item) that is muḍman bi'l-mithl (guaranteed by a similar) or muḍman bi'l-qīmah (guaranteed by value). Examples include usurped property, khul' substitute, mahr, and sulh for intentional homicide.
These items are guaranteed by their like or value if destroyed.
2
Understand the guarantee condition
The pledge is valid only if the item is guaranteed. For existing items, their 'ayn is due; if destroyed, the like or value is due. A promised debt (dayn maw'ūd) also qualifies based on necessity.
If the debt exceeds the value, the pledge is only guaranteed by value.
3
Analyze Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that salam capital, sarf price, and muslam fīhi cannot be pledged because these contracts involve exchange (istibdāl) of identical items, which is prohibited in sarf and salam. The pledge would lead to recovery through exchange.
Zufar views the pledge as an exchange, not a security.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi majority responds that the pledge is a security (istīthāq), not an exchange. The pledgee holds the item as a trust (amānah), not ownership. Expenses remain on the pledgor. Recovery is through financial value, not the specific 'ayn, so no prohibited exchange occurs.
The two parties are considered the same genus in terms of value.
5
State the practical outcome
The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is adopted: such pledges are valid. Zufar's view is rejected. The thamarah al-khilaf is that Hanafis allow these pledges; Zufar prohibits them.
The mufta bihi is the majority Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح بعين مضمونة بالمثل أو بالقيمة ؛ كالمغصوب وبدل الخلع، والمهر، وبدل الصلح عن دم عمد)، فإنّ هذه الأشياء إذا كانت قائمة يجب عينها، وإن هلكت يجب المثل أو القيمة ، فيصح الرهن بها، وبالدين ولو موعوداً، بأن رهن الإجارة على ذلك باطلة، والأجرة غير مضمونة ، والرهن إذا لم يكن في مقابلته شيء مضمون كان باطلاً. كذا في «البيانية»(٢). وبالدين ولو موعوداً، بأن رهن ليقرضه كذا ، فهلكُهُ في يد المرتهن عليه بما وعده (۱) (۱) : أي إن هلك في يد المرتهن فللراهن على المرتهن المقدار الذي وَعَدَ إقراضه ، فهلكه : بالرفع مبتدأ، وفي يد المرتهن : صفته، وعليه : خبره ، واعلم أنَّ الرهن إنَّما يكون مضموناً بالدين الموعودِ إذا كان الدين مساوياً للقيمة أو أقلّ، أما إذا كان أكثر فلا يكون مضموناً بالدين بل بالقيمة، وإنما لم يذكر هذا القسم ؛ لأن الظاهر أن لا يكون الدين أكثر من قيمة الرهن، وإن كان على سبيل الندرة فحكمه يعلم مما سبق، فاعتمد على ذلك. ( وبرأس مال السلم وثمن الصرف والمسلّم فيه" [٢] قوله : والمسلّم فيه ؛ أي يجوزُ الرهن بهذه الأشياء، وقال زفر : لا يجوز ؛ لأنَّ حكمه الاستيفاء، وذلك بالاستبدال ، والاستبدال حرام في بدل الصرف والسلم. ولنا : إنه استيثاق من الوجه الذي عنى، وهو المقصود بالرهن، وإنما يصير مستوفياً بالمالية لا بالعين ؛ ولهذا يكون عينه أمانةً في يده ، حتى نفقته حياً وكفنه ميتاً على الراهن، ولو كان مستوفياً به لوجب على المرتهن، وهما من حيث المالية جنس واحد، فيجوز استيفاء لا مبادلة.
And it is valid with an 'ayn guaranteed by a similar or value, such as the usurped item, the substitute of khul', the dower, and the substitute of sulh for intentional homicide, then these things if they are existent their 'ayn is obligatory, and if destroyed the similar or value is obligatory, so pledging with them is valid. And with a debt even if promised, such as if one pledges...
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage outlines what kinds of property can be validly pledged. The matn states that any 'ayn (specific item) that is guaranteed (muḍman) by a similar (mithl) or by value (qīmah) can be pledged. Examples include usurped property, the substitute in khul' divorce, the dower (mahr), and the substitute in a settlement for intentional homicide. The commentary explains that these items, if still existent, must be returned in kind; if destroyed, their like or value must be paid. Therefore, pledging them is valid. The commentary also emphasizes that the pledge must be in exchange for something guaranteed; otherwise, the pledge is invalid. Then it addresses pledging for a promised debt: if a person pledges a property with the promise of a loan, and the pledged property is destroyed in the pledgee's hands, the pledgee becomes liable for the promised amount, because the promise is treated as existing out of necessity. However, if the debt exceeds the value, the pledge is only guaranteed by value. Finally, the passage discusses a dispute with Zufar regarding pledging the capital of a salam contract, the price in a sarf (currency exchange) contract, and the muslam fīhi (the commodity in salam). Zufar argues that this is invalid because the nature of these contracts involves exchange of identical items, which is prohibited. The Hanafi response is that the pledge is a means of securing (istīthāq), not a direct exchange. The item in the pledgee's hands is a trust (amānah) until default, so its maintenance expenses are on the pledgor. The two parties are considered of the same genus in terms of value, so recovery is valid without exchange.

Key Terms
مضمون بالمثل (muḍman bi'l-mithl): Guaranteed by a similar item, typically used for fungible goods (mithliyyāt) that can be replaced by the same type, quantity, and quality.
مضمون بالقيمة (muḍman bi'l-qīmah): Guaranteed by value, used for non-fungible or unique items (qīmiyyāt) that cannot be replaced exactly.
دين موعود (dayn maw'ūd): A promised debt, a debt that is not yet owed but promised to be given in the future.
استيثاق (istīthāq): Securing, a legal tie to ensure payment; the purpose of a pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, it is valid.
B) No, because it involves exchange of the same item.
C) Only if the salam capital is guaranteed.
D) Only if it is a debt.

Key Takeaway
A pledge is a security (istīthāq), not an exchange; therefore, any guaranteed 'ayn, even for a promised debt, is valid, and Zufar's objection is rejected.
