Destruction of Pledge After Rescission in Salam
Explains ruling on pledge destruction after rescission in salam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the destruction of a pledge after rescission of a salam contract and explain the reasoning using the concept of yad istifa'.

Core Idea
Destruction of a pledge after rescission is treated as collection of the salam commodity, requiring the mortgagee to pay its equivalent.

In salam contracts, a pledge may be given as security. If the contract is rescinded and the pledge is destroyed in the creditor's hands, how should the loss be allocated? The Hanafi school treats the destruction as if the creditor had collected the debt (the salam commodity). This lesson unpacks the ruling and its basis in the concept of yad istifa' (hand of collection).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
What is the exact situation described? A salam contract exists, the recipient pledges an item to the lender for the salam commodity, then they rescind the contract, and the pledge is destroyed in the lender's possession.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that the destruction is considered 'in exchange for' the salam commodity, i.e., the lender must pay the equivalent of the commodity to the recipient.
3
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The reasoning uses the principle that the mortgagee's hand over the pledge is a 'hand of collection' (yad istifa'), so destruction of the pledge is equivalent to actual collection of the debt. Since the debt was the salam commodity, the lender is deemed to have collected it.
Note that the destruction does not nullify the rescission; the rescission remains valid.
4
Determine the obligation
Because the rescission requires restoring both parties to pre-contract status, and the lender is deemed to have collected the commodity, he must now give the equivalent of the commodity to the recipient and take back his principal (the price he paid).
5
Distinguish from other possibilities
The pledge is considered destroyed for the food, not for the principal, because it was pledged for the food, not the principal. The destruction does not extinguish the rescission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو هلك رهنه بعد الفسخ هلك به) : أي إذا رهنَ الْمُسَلِّمُ إليه عند رب السَّلَم شيئاً بالمُسَلّم فيه ، ثمَّ فسخا عقدَ السَّلَم فهَلَكَ الرهنُ فِي يَدِ رَبِّ السَّلَم فهلكُهُ يكون بالمسلم فيه : أي يكون على ربِّ السّلم أن يؤدي إلى المُسَلَّم إليه مقدار الطعام المسلّم فيه ؛ لأنه إذا هلك الرهن صار كَأَنَّ رَبَّ السَّلَم استوفى حقٌّ المُسَلّم فيه ؛ لأن يد المرتهن على الرهن يد استيفاء، فيتقرر بالهلاك، فصار كأنَّ رب السلم استوفى المسلّم فيه، ثم فسخا العقد، فعلى ربِّ السّلَم أداء المسلّم فيه إلى المسلم إليه. [1] قوله : هلك به... الخ ؛ فعلى المرتهن أن يعطي مثل الطعام الذي كان له على المسلّم إليه، ويأخذ رأس ماله ؛ لأنه بقبض المال صارت ماليته مضمونة بطعام السلم، وقد بقي حكم الرهن إلى أن هلك فصار بهلاكِ الراهن مستوفياً طعام السلم، وهو استوفاه حقيقة قبل الإقالة، ثمّ تقايلا يلزمُهُ ردّ المستوفى لاستواء رأس المال، فكذلك هنا ؛ لأنَّ الإقالة في باب السّلم لا تحتمل الفسخ بعد ثبوتها. فهلاك الرهن لا يبطل الإقالة، وإنما جعله هالكاً بالطعام لا برأس المال ؛ لأنه ليس برهن به، لكنّه محبوس به لما قلنا ، ويجوز أن يكون الشيء محبوساً بالشيء ولا يكون مضمونا به كذا في «غاية البيان.
If his pledge is destroyed after rescission, it is destroyed in exchange for it. That is: If the person to whom salam is given pledges something to the salam lender for the salam commodity, then they rescind the salam contract, and the pledge is destroyed in the hand of the salam lender, then its destruction is considered as in exchange for the salam commodity: meaning the salam lender must pay to the salam recipient the amount of the salam food, because when the pledge is destroyed, it is as if the salam lender has collected the right of the salam recipient; indeed the hand of the mortgagee over the pledge is a hand of collection, so it is established by destruction, so it is as if the salam lender has collected the salam commodity. Then they rescind the contract, so upon the salam lender is the payment of the salam commodity to the salam recipient. [1] His statement: 'destroyed in exchange for it'... etc.; so upon the mortgagee is to give the like of the food that was owed to him by the salam recipient, and take his principal, because by receiving the money, its value became guaranteed by the salam food, and the ruling of the pledge remained until it was destroyed, so by its destruction the mortgagor becomes a collector of the salam food, and he actually collected it before rescission. Then if they mutually rescind, it is necessary for him to return what was collected for the equality of the principal. So likewise here, because rescission in the chapter of salam does not admit revocation after it is established. The destruction of the pledge does not nullify the rescission, rather it is considered destroyed in exchange for the food, not in exchange for the principal, because it is not a pledge for it, but it is held for what we said, and it is possible for something to be held for something else without being guaranteed for it, as in 'Ghayat al-Bayan'.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof from the Book of Rahn in the context of salam (forward sale). The author first states the ruling: if a pledge is destroyed after the rescission of a salam contract, it is considered destroyed in lieu of the salam commodity. The commentary explains the scenario: the salam recipient (the person who receives the price) pledges something to the salam lender (the person who gives the price) for the salam commodity. After they rescind the contract, the pledge is destroyed while in the lender's possession. The effect is that the lender is required to pay the amount of the salam food to the recipient. The reasoning is that the mortgagee's hand over the pledge is a hand of collection (yad istifa'), meaning the pledge is held as security for the debt. When the pledge is destroyed, it is as if the debt (the salam commodity) has been collected. Since the contract is rescinded after this deemed collection, the lender must now give back what he had supposedly collected. The footnote [1] elaborates: the mortgagee must give the equivalent of the food and take back his principal, because the money he gave (the price) became guaranteed by the salam food. The destruction of the pledge turns it into a collection of the food, and the subsequent rescission requires the return of the collected food to achieve equality. The rescission in salam is irrevocable once established, so the destruction does not nullify it. The passage clarifies that the pledge is considered destroyed for the food, not the principal, because it was not pledged for the principal but for the food. The Hanafi position is based on istihsan (juristic preference) considering the purpose of the pledge.

Key Terms
يد استيفاء (yad istifa'): A hand of collection; meaning the mortgagee holds the pledge with the authority to collect the debt, so destruction is equivalent to actual collection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a pledge is destroyed after rescission of a salam contract, what must the mortgagee do?
A) Keep the pledge because it is destroyed.
B) Pay the equivalent of the salam food to the mortgagor.
C) Rescind the rescission.
D) Return the principal only.

Key Takeaway
Destruction of a pledge after rescission in salam is treated as constructive collection, obligating the mortgagee to pay the equivalent of the salam commodity.

Validity of Pledge for Apparently Valid Debt
Explains that a pledge for a sale price is valid even if the item turns out to be defective, and a pledge in a settlement on denial is valid if no debt exists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a pledge given for a debt that later proves to be invalid and explain the rationale of apparent validity.

Core Idea
A pledge remains valid even if the underlying debt turns out void, because the debt was apparently valid at the time of pledge.

This lesson examines two scenarios where a pledge is given for a debt that later is discovered to be nonexistent or based on a defective sale. The Hanafi school upholds the validity of the pledge and imposes liability on the mortgagee. The key is the principle that apparent obligation suffices for the pledge's validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage: first scenario is a pledge for the price of a slave/vinegar/slaughtered animal that turns out defective; second is a pledge given in a settlement upon denial where it later appears no debt existed.
2
State the ruling for each
In both cases, the pledge is valid (sahih) and the mortgagee is liable (damman) if the pledge is destroyed. The mortgagee must pay the value of the pledge or the debt amount, whichever is less.
3
Understand the rationale
The debt was apparently obligatory (wajib zahiran) at the time of pledging. The judge would enforce it. Therefore, the pledge is valid, and the mortgagee's liability is based on the apparent trust.
The passage states: 'وجوب الدين ظاهراً يكفي لصحة الرهن'.
4
Recognize the practical consequence
If the pledge is destroyed with the mortgagee, he must compensate the mortgagor. Without this rule, many pledges could be retroactively invalidated, causing instability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبثمن عبدٍ أو خل أو ذكية إن ظهر العبد حرّاً، والخل خمراً، والذكية ميتة ويبدل صلح عن إنكار إن أقر أن لا دين [1] قوله : بحقيقة الإيفاء ؛ يعني ليس للأب أن يؤدّي دينه من الصغير، فكذا ليس له أن يدفع مال الصغير بجهة الإيفاء ؛ لأنَّ الرهن محبوس بجهة الإيفاء. [2] قوله : بدين واجب ظاهراً ؛ ألا ترى أنَّ البائع والمشتري لـو اختصما إلى القاضي قبل ظهور الحرية والاستحقاق فالقاضي يقضي بالثمن، ووجوب الدين ظاهراً يكفي لصحة الرهن، ويصير وزنه مضموناً. كذا في «العناية». (3) قوله : صالح مع إنكار ... الخ ؛ توضيح هذه الصورة: أن رجلاً ادعى ألف درهم فجحد المدعى عليه، ثم صالحه من ذلك على خمسمئة على الإنكار، وأعطاه بها رهناً يساوي خمسمئة ، فهلك الرهن عند المرتهن ثم تصادقا على أن لا دين ، فإنّ الرهن مضمون، أي فعلى المرتهن غرم خمسمئة للراهن.
And by the price of a slave or vinegar or a slaughtered animal, if the slave appears to be free, the vinegar wine, and the slaughtered animal carrion, and by the compensation of a settlement upon denial if he admits that there is no debt. [1] His statement: 'By the reality of payment' means it is not for the father to pay his debt from the minor, so likewise it is not for him to give the minor's property by way of payment, because the pledge is held by way of payment. [2] His statement: 'by an apparently obligatory debt'; do you not see that the seller and buyer, if they dispute before the judge before the appearance of freedom and entitlement, the judge decides the price, and the apparent obligation of the debt suffices for the validity of the pledge, and its value becomes guaranteed. As in al-Inaya. (3) His statement: 'he settled upon denial'... etc.; clarification of this scenario: A man claimed one thousand dirhams, the defendant denied, then he settled with him for five hundred on denial, and gave him a pledge worth five hundred, then the pledge was destroyed with the mortgagee, then they agreed that there is no debt. Then the pledge is guaranteed, i.e., upon the mortgagee is the liability of five hundred to the mortgagor.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement covering two sub-rulings. First: A pledge given for the price of a purchased item (slave, vinegar, slaughtered animal) is valid even if the item later turns out to be defective (slave free, vinegar wine, animal carrion). In such cases, the pledge is guaranteed: if it is destroyed and its value equals or exceeds the price, the mortgagee must pay the price to the mortgagor; if less, the value. The reason is that at the time of the pledge, the debt was apparently valid and the judge would enforce it. The footnote [2] explains that the apparent obligation suffices for the pledge's validity. Second: A pledge given in a sulh on denial (settlement where the defendant denies the claim but pays to avoid litigation) is valid, even if later it turns out there was no actual debt. In the scenario, if the mortgagor later admits that the debt was nonexistent, the pledge is still guaranteed, and the mortgagee must return the value. The footnote (3) clarifies that the mortgagee must pay the amount of the settlement to the mortgagor. The Hanafi position is that these pledges are enforceable despite the underlying defects or absence of debt.

Key Terms
صلح عن إنكار (sulh 'an inkar): A settlement where the defendant denies the claim but agrees to pay a sum to avoid litigation; the compromise is valid even if the claim was false.
دين واجب ظاهراً (dayn wajib zahiran): A debt that is apparently obligatory at the time of pledging, meaning a judge would enforce it based on available evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person buys a slave and gives a pledge for the price, but the slave is later found to be free, what happens to the pledge?
A) The pledge is void because the sale is void.
B) The pledge is valid and the mortgagee must pay the price if the pledge is destroyed.
C) The pledge becomes a trust (amanah) and the mortgagee is not liable.
D) The seller must return the price.

Key Takeaway
A pledge is valid as long as the debt was apparently obligatory at the time of pledging; the mortgagee is liable for the value of the pledge if it is destroyed.

Liability for a Pawned Slave's Manslaughter
Ruling and reasoning for a pawned slave's manslaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a pawned slave commits manslaughter and explain why the mortgagee is liable.

Core Idea
The mortgagee pays the blood money because the slave is in his guarantee; he cannot recover it.

A pawned slave accidentally kills someone – who is responsible? The Hanafi school answers by focusing on possession and guarantee (daman). In this passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah, the ruling is given with its reasoning. We will follow the argument and see how the mortgagee's liability arises from his control over the slave.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and primary ruling
The pawned slave commits manslaughter (jinaayah khata'). The mortgagee (murtahin) must pay the blood money (diya). He cannot recover this payment from the pawner (raahin).
2
Understand the legal basis (dalil)
The proof is istihsan based on the principle of daman al-yad (liability of possession). The slave is in the mortgagee's guarantee, so the injury occurred under his responsibility.
Istihsan is a preferred ruling over strict analogy in Hanafi usul.
3
Follow the subsidiary options
If the mortgagee refuses to pay, the pawner is given a choice: hand over the slave or pay the blood money. In either case, the debt secured by the pawn is cancelled (or reduced if the debt exceeds the slave's value).
The pawner acts to free his property, not as a volunteer.
4
Trace the reasoning for non-recovery
The mortgagee cannot recover from the pawner because the injury occurred under the mortgagee's guarantee. He is responsible for rectifying it, just as he would be for any loss during his possession.
This is consistent with the rule that the mortgagee bears the risk of damage to the pawn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن جنى الرهن خطأ فداه مرتهنه ، ولم يرجع) : أي على الراهن ؛ لأنَّ الجناية حصلت في ضمان المرتهن ، ولا يملك الدفع ؛ لأنَّ المرتهن غير مالك ، ( فإن أبي دفعه الراهن أو فداه ، وسقط الدِّين : أي إن أبى المرتهن أن يفديَهُ قيل للراهن : ادفع العبد أو افدِ عنه، وأياً فعل سقط الدين. واعلم أنَّ الدِّينَ إنَّما يسقط بتمامِهِ إذا كانَ الدِّينُ أقل من قيمة الرهن أو مساوياً، أما إذا كان أكثر يسقط من الدين مقدار قيمة العبد، ولا يسقط الباقي، لكن لم يذكر في «المتن» هذا ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر أن لا يكونَ الدِّينُ أكثر من قيمةِ الرَّهن. [١] قوله : فداه مرتهنه ؛ لأنّ العبد في ضمانه، فيقال للمرتهن : خذ العبد من الجناية، هذا إذا كان القيمة والدين سواء ، أما إذا كانت القيمة أكثر يسقط من الدين مقدار قيمة العبد، ولا يسقط الباقي، ولو فدى طهر المحل، فبقـى ديـن المرتهن على حاله ، ولم يرجع بشيء من ! الفداء على الراهن ؛ لأنّ الجناية حصلت في ضمان المرتهن، فكان عليه إصلاحها، ولا يملك المرتهن الدفع ؛ لأنه غير مالك، فلا يملك التمليك. [٢]قوله : إن أبى ؛ أي المرتهن أن يفديه، قيل للراهن.... الخ ؛ لأنّ الملك في الرقبة قائم للراهن، وإنما يحكم إلى المرتهن بالفداء ؛ لقيام حقه، فإذا أنكر عن الفداء يطالب الراهن بحكم الجناية، ومن حكمها التخيير بين الدفع والفداء، فإن اختار الدفع سقط الدين ؛ لأنه استحق بمعنى في ضمان المرتهن ، فصار كالهلاك. وكذا إن اختار الفداء ؛ لأنَّ العبد كالحاصل له بعوض كان على المرتهن، وإذا كان على المرتهن فقد أداه الراهن، فيجب للراهن على المرتهن مثل ما أدى إلى ولي الجناية، وللمرتهن على الراهن دين، فالتقيا قصاصاً، فيسلم الرهن للراهن، ولا يكون الراهن متبرعاً في أداء الفداء ؛ لأنه يسعى في تخليص ملكه، كمصير الراهن. هكذا في «الهداية» وشروحها.
If the pawned slave commits manslaughter, his mortgagee pays the blood money and does not recover (from the pawner). That is, against the pawner because the injury occurred under the guarantee of the mortgagee, and he does not have the right to hand over (the slave) because the mortgagee is not the owner. (If he refuses, the pawner hands him over or pays the blood money, and the debt is cancelled.) That is, if the mortgagee refuses to pay it, it is said to the pawner: hand over the slave or pay for him. Whichever he does, the debt is cancelled. Know that the debt is completely cancelled only if the debt is less than or equal to the value of the pawn. If it is more, the amount equivalent to the value of the slave is cancelled from the debt, and the remainder is not cancelled. But this is not mentioned in the matn because the apparent assumption is that the debt is not more than the value of the pawn. (And if the pawner dies, his executor sells his pawn and pays his debt.) This is a new case not related to the case of the injury. That is, if the pawner dies, the executor sells the pawn with the permission of the mortgagee and pays his debt. [1] His statement: 'his mortgagee pays' because the slave is under his guarantee, so it is said to the mortgagee: take the slave from the injury. This is if the value and debt are equal. If the value is greater, the amount equivalent to the slave's value is cancelled from the debt, and the remainder remains. If he pays, the debt of the mortgagee remains as it was, and he does not recover anything of the blood money from the pawner because the injury occurred under the mortgagee's guarantee, so it was upon him to rectify it, and the mortgagee does not have the right to hand over (the slave) because he is not the owner, so he does not have the right to transfer ownership. [2] His statement: 'If he refuses' i.e., the mortgagee refuses to pay it, it is said to the pawner... etc. Because ownership of the slave's body belongs to the pawner, and the mortgagee is only ordered to pay due to his right. So if he refuses, the pawner is called upon by the law of the injury, and from its ruling is the choice between handover and payment. If he chooses handover, the debt is cancelled because the slave becomes deceased in the mortgagee's guarantee, so it is as if he perished. Similarly, if he chooses payment, because the slave is as if acquired for him with a consideration that was upon the mortgagee, and since it was upon the mortgagee, the pawner has paid it, so the pawner has a claim against the mortgagee for the equivalent of what he paid to the guardian of the injury, and the mortgagee has a debt against the pawner, so they meet as compensation, so the pawn is released to the pawner, and the pawner is not a volunteer in paying the blood money because he seeks to free his property, like the situation of the pawner. Thus it is in al-Hidayah and its commentaries.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling when a pawned slave commits manslaughter (jinaayah khata'). The mortgagee (murtahin) is liable to pay the blood money (diya) because the slave is in his possession (daman al-yad). He cannot later recover this payment from the pawner (raahin). If the mortgagee refuses to pay, the pawner is given a choice: either hand over the slave to the victim's family or pay the blood money. In either case, the debt secured by the pawn is cancelled. However, if the debt exceeds the value of the pawn, only the portion equal to the slave's value is cancelled. The commentary explains the reasoning: the mortgagee's liability arises from his guarantee (daman) over the slave; the pawner retains ownership, so if the mortgagee refuses, the pawner must pay. The pawner's payment results in a set-off against the debt, and he is not considered a volunteer because he is saving his own property. This ruling is based on the principles of possession and liability, and is found in al-Hidayah and its commentaries.

Key Terms
جناية خطأ (jinaayah khata'): Accidental killing or injury for which blood money (diya) is due.
مرتهن (murtahin): The person who receives the pawn as security for a debt; the mortgagee.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee for loss or injury arising from possession.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The debt is cancelled entirely.
B) The debt remains and the mortgagee can recover from the pawner.
C) The debt is reduced by the value of the slave but not fully cancelled if the debt is larger.
D) The pawner must pay the mortgagee back.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee bears the financial liability for manslaughter by a pawned slave because of his guarantee (daman), and he cannot shift this cost to the pawner.

Executor's Duty to Sell Pawn and Judge's Role
Explains executor's duty to sell pawn and judge's role if no executor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the executor's obligation to sell the pawn after the pawner's death and identify the judge's role when no executor exists.

Core Idea
The executor must sell the pawn with mortgagee permission; the judge appoints one if absent.

When a pawner dies, the debt remains. The estate must be used to settle it, and the pawn is the primary asset. This lesson clarifies who acts to sell the pawn and pay the debt, and what happens if no executor is appointed. We also examine a critical limit on the executor's power: favoring one creditor over others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
The text rules that upon the pawner's death, the executor sells the pawn and pays the debt. This is a wajib obligation on the executor.
The act is the sale of pawn; the obligation is to fulfill the debt.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is qiyas (analogy): the executor stands in the place of the living pawner. Just as the living pawner sells with the mortgagee's permission, so does the executor. Additionally, the judge is the general supervisor for rights when the owner is incapable.
The dalil is aqli (rational) based on representation.
3
Identify the conditions and alternatives
The executor must have the mortgagee's permission to sell. If no executor exists, the judge appoints one to sell.
This ensures the mortgagee's right is protected.
4
Analyze the exception: preferential pawning
The executor cannot pawn part of the estate to one creditor if others exist. This would be favoring that creditor and is invalid. However, if the debt is paid before others object, it becomes valid.
If only one creditor, it is allowed.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is exactly as stated: executor sells with permission; judge appoints if none; no preferential pawning without consent.
This is from al-Hidayah and is zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو مات الراهن باع وصيه رهنه ، وقضى دينه) ، هذه مسألة مبتدأة لا تعلق لها بمسألة الجناية، أي إذا مات الراهن فوصى يبيعُ الرَّهن بإذن المرتهن ويقضي دينه ، كما إذا كان الرَّاهِنُ حيَّاً فله البيعُ بإذنِ المرتهن كذا هاهنا ، (فإن لم يكن له وصي نُصب وصي يبيعه). [١] قوله: نصب وصي... الخ ؛ أي نصب القاضي له وصياً يبيعُ الرهن ؛ لأنّ القاضي نُصبَ ناظراً لحقوق المسلمين إذا عجزوا عن النظر لأنفسهم، وقد تعيَّن النظر في نصب الوصي ؛ ليؤدي ما عليه لغيره، ويستوفي حقوقه من غيره، ولو كان على الميت ديـن فـرهن الوصي بعض التركة عند غريم له من غرمائه لم يجز، وللآخرين أن يردّوه ؛ لأنه إيثار لبعض الغرماء بالإيفاء الحكمي ، فأشبه الإيثار بالإيفاء الحقيقي، والجامع ما في كل واحد منهما من إبطال حق غيره من الغرماء. ألا ترى أن الميت بنفسه لا يملك ذلك بمرض موته ، فكذا من قام مقامه وإن قضى دينهم قبل أن يردّوه جاز ؛ لزوال المانع ، ووصول حقهم، ولو لم يكن للميت غريم آخر جاز الرهن اعتباراً بالإيفاء الحقيقي، وبيع في دينه ما لا يباع فيه قبل الرهن، فكذا بعده وإذا ارتهن الوصي بدين للميت على رجل جاز ؛ لأنه استيفاء، فيملكه وله أن يبيعه. هذا تلخيص ما في «الهداية»(۱).
(And if the pawner dies, his executor sells his pawn and pays his debt.) This is a new case not related to the case of the injury. That is, if the pawner dies, the executor sells the pawn with the permission of the mortgagee and pays his debt, just as when the pawner is alive, he sells with the permission of the mortgagee. (If he has no executor, an executor is appointed to sell it.) [1] His statement: 'an executor is appointed' etc.; that is, the judge appoints an executor for him to sell the pawn because the judge is appointed as a supervisor for the rights of Muslims when they are unable to look after themselves. And the need to appoint an executor is determined so that he may fulfill what is due from him to others and recover his rights from others. If the deceased owes a debt and the executor pawns some of the estate to one of his creditors, it is not permissible, and the others may reject it because it is favoring some creditors with a virtual fulfillment, similar to favoring with actual fulfillment. The common factor is the invalidation of the rights of other creditors in both. Do you not see that the deceased himself could not do that during his fatal illness, so the one who stands in his place also cannot. If he pays their debts before they reject it, it is permitted because the obstacle is removed and their right is fulfilled. If the deceased has no other creditor, the pawn is permitted considering actual fulfillment. He sells for his debt what cannot be sold before the pawn, and likewise after it. If the executor takes a pawn for a debt owed to the deceased from a man, it is permitted because it is a recovery; he owns it and may sell it. This is a summary of what is in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where the pawner (raahin) dies. The executor (wasi) is required to sell the pawned property with the mortgagee's permission to pay off the debt. If there is no executor, the judge (qadi) appoints one. The commentary then addresses a related issue: if an executor pawns part of the deceased's estate to one creditor, it is invalid if there are other creditors, as it gives preferential treatment. However, if payment is made before rejection, it is valid. If there is only one creditor, it is permitted. The executor can also take a pawn for a debt owed to the deceased and sell it. This ruling is based on the principle that the judge oversees the rights of Muslims, and the executor acts as a representative. The text summarizes from al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): The executor appointed in the pawner's will to manage his affairs after death.
غرماء (ghuramaa'): Creditors of the deceased who have claims against the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the pawner dies and has no executor?
A) The pawn is forfeited to the mortgagee.
B) The judge appoints an executor to sell the pawn.
C) The debt is cancelled.
D) The mortgagee keeps the pawn.

Key Takeaway
The executor must sell the pawn with mortgagee's permission to pay the debt; the judge appoints an executor if none exists, and preferential pawning of estate assets to one creditor is invalid if other creditors exist.

Liability for Removing a Stone: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Causation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on causation and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on liability when a moved stone causes death, and apply the Hanafi rule of causation (sabab) to distinguish between starvation and suffocation.

Core Idea
Liability for removing a stone depends on the cause of death: Abu Yusuf distinguishes starvation from suffocation, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the mover liable in all cases.

This passage introduces a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on liability (daman) for indirect causation. The scenario: a stone placed by one person is moved by another, and a third party stumbles over it and dies. The question is whether the mover or the original placer is liable. The text presents three positions from the early Hanafi masters, each based on different analyses of causation (sabab) and the causal link between the act and death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and legal question
Recognize that the act is removing a stone placed by another, and the issue is who bears liability if a third party dies as a result.
The general principle: the one whose action is the immediate cause is liable.
2
State the general rule and its analogy
The text first gives a general ruling: the mover is liable because his action supersedes the earlier placement. This is analogized to carrying something that falls on another or entering a mosque with a lantern.
The analogy shows that the later act breaks the causal nexus of the earlier act.
3
Explain Abu Yusuf's distinction
Abu Yusuf distinguishes between death by starvation (ju') and death by suffocation (ghamm from the well's air). For starvation, the mover is liable; for suffocation, the original digger is liable because the suffocation is uniquely caused by the well, not by the removal of the stone.
This distinction hinges on whether the cause (sabab) is specific to the original hazard.
4
Explain Muhammad al-Shaybani's position
Muhammad holds the mover liable in all cases, reasoning that the fall itself caused the death, since otherwise the victim could have accessed food. The removal of the stone was the necessary condition for the fall.
Muhammad emphasizes the causal chain: but for the removal, the victim would not have fallen.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf's view, if the cause of death is uncertain or if suffocation is proven, liability shifts to the original digger. Under the other views, the mover is always liable. This affects who must pay compensation (daman).
Later Hanafi authorities may choose one view as mufta bihi based on the specifics of the case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن نحى حجراً وضعه آخر فعطب به رجل ضمن؛ لأن فعل الأول منه انفسخ بفعل الثاني، فالضمان على الثاني، كمن حمل شيئاً في الطريق فسقط منه على آخر، أو دخل بحصير أو قنديل أو حصاة في مسجد غيره. وقال أبو يوسف له: إن مات جوعاً فكذلك، وإن مات غماً فالحافر ضامن له؛ لأنه لا سبب للغم سوى الوقوع، أما الجوع لا يختص بالبئر. وقال محمد: هو ضامن في الوجوه كلها؛ لأنه إنما حدث بسبب الوقوع إذ لولاه لكان الطعام قريباً منه.
And whoever moves a stone that another placed, and a man stumbles on it and dies/is harmed, he is liable; because the action of the first is superseded by the action of the second, so liability is on the second, like one who carries something in the road and it falls on another, or enters with a mat, lantern, or pebble into the mosque of someone else. And Abu Yusuf said to him: If he dies of starvation, it is the same; but if he dies of suffocation, then the digger is liable for him; because there is no cause for suffocation except the fall, whereas starvation is not specific to the well. And Muhammad said: He is liable in all cases; because it only occurred due to the fall, since otherwise food would have been near him.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when a person removes a stone placed by another, leading to a third person's death by falling into a well (or similar). The initial ruling states that the remover is liable because his action nullifies the earlier placement, analogized to carrying something that falls on someone. Abu Hanifa's position is implied as general liability. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the victim dies of starvation (ju'), the remover is liable; if of suffocation (ghamm) from the well's air, the original digger is liable because the suffocation is caused solely by the fall, not the removal of the stone. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds the remover liable in all cases, reasoning that the fall caused the death, since the victim could have accessed food otherwise. The ruling is based on qiyas (analogy) and consideration of causation (sabab). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not specified here, but later sources may prefer Abu Hanifa or Muhammad's view.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Financial liability or compensation for harm caused.
الغم (al-ghamm): Suffocation or air hunger, specifically from the air of a well.
الجوع (al-ju'): Starvation, death from lack of food.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When the victim dies of suffocation (ghamm)
B) When the victim dies of starvation (ju')
C) In all cases
D) In no case

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a later act supersedes an earlier hazard, the mover is generally liable, but Abu Yusuf introduces a nuance based on whether the cause of death is specific to the original hazard.

Debt Attaches to Slave's Person and Spreads to Child
Ruling that debt attaches to slave's person and spreads to child, but jinayat does not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling the child of an indebted ma'dhuna for her debt and explain why jinayat do not spread to the child.

Core Idea
Debt is attached to the slave's person; jinayat are on the master's liability — this difference determines what spreads.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi slave law: financial obligations (debt) are tied to the slave's person and thus affect her property, including her child, while criminal liability (jinayat) falls on the master. Understanding this distinction requires grasping the concept of saraya (legal spread) and the difference between actual and legal attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
State the case: an indebted permitted female slave (ma'dhuna) gives birth. The ruling is that the child is sold together with the mother to pay off her debt, but the child is not handed over with her to satisfy a jinayat liability.
The ruling applies specifically to a ma'dhuna, not a regular slave.
2
Understand the rationale for debt spread
Why does the debt spread to the child? Because the debt is attached to the slave's person (raqabah). The child is part of her property, so the attachment extends to the child.
The master is prevented from disposing of the slave's person until the debt is paid.
3
Understand the exception for jinayat
Why does jinayat liability not spread? Because the liability for jinayat is on the master's dhimma, not the slave's person. The slave's person is not the subject of the liability.
The master can still dispose of the slave's person despite the jinayat.
4
Distinguish actual vs. legal attributes
Debt is a legal attribute (shar'i) that can transfer conceptually, while jinayat liability is tied to the actual act and the master's responsibility. Legal attributes can shift with the subject; real attributes cannot.
This is a key usul principle applied here.
5
Confirm the practical consequence
The consequence is that the child is sold only for the debt, not for jinayat. The master must pay the jinayat from his own property.
This affects how creditors and victims can seek compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ولدت مأذونة مديونة ولداً يباع معها لدينها، ولا يدفع معها لجنايتها، فإِنَّ الدِّينَ في ذمَّةِ الأَمَةِ متعلّق برقبتها فيسري إلى الولد، وفي الجنايةِ الدفع في ذمَّةِ المولى لا في ذمتها ، وإنَّما يلاقيها أثر الفعل الحقيقي وهو الدفع، والسراية في الأمور الشرعية لا الحقيقية. [ 1 ] قوله : متعلق برقبتها ؛ حتى صار المولى ممنوعاً من التصرف في رقبتها ببيع أو هبة أو غيرهما. كذا في «العناية»). [2] قوله : لا في ذمتها ؛ حتى لا يصير المولى ممنوعاً من التصرف في رقبتها ببيع أو هبة أو غيرهما. [٣] قوله : لا الحقيقية ؛ أي دون الأوصاف الحقيقية بناءً على أن الوصف الحقيقي في محل لا يمكن أن ينتقل إلى غيره، وأما الوصف الشرعي فهو اعتباري يتحول بتحوله.
If a permitted female slave who is indebted gives birth, her child is sold along with her for her debt, and her child is not handed over along with her for her jinayat. So the debt on the slave's liability is attached to her person, so it spreads to the child. In the case of jinayat, payment is on the liability of the master, not on her liability. Rather, the effect of the actual act (payment) meets her, and spreading applies to legal matters, not actual ones. [1] His saying: attached to her person; so the master is prevented from disposing of her person by sale, gift, or otherwise. This is in al-Hidayah. [2] His saying: not on her liability; so the master does not become prevented from disposing of her person by sale, gift, or otherwise. [3] His saying: not actual; meaning without real attributes, based on the fact that a real attribute in a place cannot be transferred to another, while a legal attribute is conceptual and changes with its change.
Source pages p. 536

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation from the commentary on al-Hidayah. It addresses the case of a female slave (ma'dhuna) who is indebted and gives birth. The ruling states that the child is sold together with the mother to pay off her debt, but the child is not given over with the mother to satisfy liability for a wrongful act (jinayat) she committed. The reason is that the debt is a financial obligation on the slave's person, so it attaches to her and spreads to her child, because the child is part of her property. In contrast, the liability for jinayat is a personal obligation on the master; it does not attach to the slave's person because the slave cannot own property. The commentary clarifies that 'attached to her person' means the master loses the right to sell her until the debt is paid. The distinction between actual and legal attributes: a real attribute (like ownership) cannot transfer from the slave to another, but a legal attribute (like liability) is conceptual and can transfer. Thus, debt spreads to the child because it is a legal attachment, while jinayat does not because it is a real attribute tied to the master's liability.

Key Terms
مأذونة (ma'dhuna): A female slave who is permitted by her master to engage in trade (mudarabah).
جناية (jinayat): A wrongful act causing bodily harm or property damage, specifically here a crime requiring financial compensation (diya or arsh).
سراية (saraya): The legal spread or transfer of a ruling from one entity to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because debt is attached to the slave's person and spreads to her child, while jinayat liability is on the master and does not attach to her person.
B) Because the child is innocent of the debt and jinayat.
C) Because the master is responsible for both debt and jinayat.
D) Because the child is free from liability.

Key Takeaway
Debt attaches to the slave's person and spreads to her child; jinayat liability is on the master and does not spread.

The Regular Slave (Qin) in Repeated Ghasb: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Compensation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation distribution when a qin suffers consecutive injuries in different hands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn regarding compensation when a qin has been misappropriated and commits crimes in two separate hands, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
The order of claims determines distribution: the first victim’s priority decreases if a later victim competes.

The matn states that the qin (regular slave) is like a mudabbar in two scenarios, but the master gives the qin itself (not just its value) when the qin is still with him. The commentary reveals a dispute between Imam Muhammad and the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) on how the compensation recovered from the gh?sib is shared with the first victim. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage refers to two scenarios regarding the timing of the first injury relative to the master's ownership. In the first scenario, the first injury occurs while the slave is in the gh?sib's hand; in the opposite scenario, the first injury occurs while the slave is with the master. This distinction affects whether the master can recover compensation from the gh?sib.
The exact details of the scenarios are derived from the commentary; careful reading of the source is required.
2
State the positions of the Sahibayn and Imam Muhammad
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Sahibayn) hold that in the first scenario, the master recovers the compensation from the gh?sib and then gives it to the first victim (guardian of the first injury). In the opposite scenario, the master does not recover. Imam Muhammad holds that in both scenarios, the master keeps the recovered half of the value and does not give it to the first victim.
Imam Muhammad's view applies specifically to the first scenario where the master recovers; in the opposite scenario, there is no recovery anyway.
3
Analyze the proofs
The Sahibayn argue that the first victim's right is to the full value initially (no competition), but it is reduced by the claim of the second victim. Therefore, if there is any compensation in the master's hand (recovered from the gh?sib), the first victim may take it to complete his right. Imam Muhammad argues that the master has already compensated the first victim for his loss; giving the recovered compensation again would combine the substitute (the compensation) and the substituted (the slave) in one ownership (the victim), which is improper.
Both arguments are based on principles of justice and avoidance of unjust enrichment.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In the first scenario, according to the Sahibayn, the master recovers from the gh?sib and must give that amount to the first victim. According to Imam Muhammad, the master keeps the recovered amount and does not give it to the first victim.
The opposite scenario yields no recovery by the master in either view.
5
Address the objection about the mudabbar's value
An objection arises: The mudabbar's crime requires only one value, but here a value and a half are required. The answer is that the multiple crimes occurred in different hands (one with the owner, one with the gh?sib), not in the same hand. Hence the increased liability is justified.
This explains why the qin is 'like the mudabbar' but with a difference in the form of compensation (qin given in kind, mudabbar's value given).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقن في الفصلين كالمدبر ، لكن السيد يدفع القن وقيمة المدبر [1] ففي الصورة الأولى : يرجع وفي صورة العكس : لا ، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف ، وقال محمد الله : نصف القيمة التي رجع بها على الغاصب يُسَلِّمُ للمولى ولا يدفع إلى ولي الجناية الأولى ؛ لأنَّهُ عوَّضَ ما أخذ وليُّ الجناية الأولى، فلا يدفع إليه كيلا يجتمع البدل والمبدل في ملك شخص واحد. لهما : أن حق الأوَّلِ في جميع القيمة ؛ لأنَّهُ حين جني في حقِّهِ لا يزاحمُهُ أحد، وإنما ينتقص باعتبار مزاحمةِ الثَّاني فإذا وجد شيئا من بدل العبد في يدِ المالكِ فارغاً يأخذه منه ليتم حقه، فإذا أخذ منه يرجعُ به المولى على الغاصب ؛ لأَنَّهُ أَخذَهُ منه بسبب كان عند الغاصب، ولا يرجعُ به في صورة العكس؛ لأنَّ الجناية الأولى كانت في يد المالك. ( والقن في الفصلين كالمدير، لكن السيد يدفع القن وقيمة المدبر) : أي إذا وأورد أن هذا يناقض ما تقدم أنَّ جناية المدبر لا توجب إلا قيمة واحدة، وهنا أوجبت قيمةً ونصفاً. وأجيب : إنّ ذاك فيما إذا تعددت الجناية في يدِ شخص واحد بخلافه هنا تأمل.
The regular slave in the two scenarios is like the mudabbar, but the master gives the slave and the value of the mudabbar. So in the first scenario, the master recovers (from the gh?sib), and in the opposite scenario, he does not. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: Half of the value that he recovered from the gh?sib is delivered to the master and not given to the guardian of the first injury, because he compensated what the guardian of the first injury took, so it is not given to him so that the substitute and the substituted are not combined in the ownership of one person. For them (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): The right of the first is in all the value, because when the injury occurred in his right, no one competes with him, but it decreases due to the competition of the second. So if he finds something of the substitute of the slave in the hand of the owner free, he takes it from him to complete his right. If he takes from him, the master recovers it from the gh?sib, because he took it from him due to a cause that was with the gh?sib. And he does not recover in the opposite scenario, because the first injury was in the hand of the owner. (The regular slave in the two scenarios is like the mudabbar, but the master gives the slave and the value of the mudabbar): meaning if, and it is objected that this contradicts what preceded that the injury of the mudabbar does not necessitate except one value, but here it necessitates one and a half values. And it is answered: That is when the injuries are multiple in the hand of one person; contrary to here. Consider.
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling for a regular slave (qin) who has been misappropriated (ghasb) and commits crimes in two scenarios. The matn states: 'The qin in the two scenarios is like the mudabbar, but the master gives the qin itself and the value of the mudabbar.' The commentary then explains two scenarios: In the first scenario (where the first crime occurred while the slave was with the first owner?), the master recovers the compensation from the gh?sib; in the opposite scenario, he does not. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad disagrees: The half of the value that the master recovers from the gh?sib is handed over to the master (the slave's owner), not to the guardian of the first injury, because the master has already compensated the guardian. If the guardian receives it, he would end up with both the compensation and the slave (the substitute and the substituted). The Sahibayn argue that the first guardian's right is to the full value, as no one competed with him initially, but it is reduced due to the second competition. So if there is any compensation in the master's hand, the first guardian takes it to complete his right. Then the master recovers that from the gh?sib in the first scenario, but not in the second because the first crime occurred in the master's ownership. The commentary also addresses an objection: how can this ruling require a value and a half when the mudabbar's crime normally only requires one value? The answer is that the multiple crimes occurred in different hands here, not in the same hand. This is a nuanced ruling based on istihsan and the specific circumstances.

Key Terms
قن (qin): A regular slave, not a mudabbar or mukatab.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whom the master has promised freedom upon the master's death.
غصب (ghasb): Wrongful appropriation or usurpation of property without the owner's consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The master keeps the compensation in both views.
B) The master gives the compensation to the first victim according to the Sahibayn, but not according to Muhammad.
C) The master gives the compensation to the second victim according to the Sahibayn.
D) The master returns the compensation to the gh?sib.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute on compensation for a qin illustrates how priority of claims and avoidance of dual recovery shape liability distribution.

Mudabbir Wrongfully Possessed Twice, Crime Committed Each Time
Ruling on a mudabbir wrongfully possessed twice, committing crimes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability for crimes committed by a mudabbir during wrongful possession and explain the recovery process from the ghāsib.

Core Idea
A master must compensate for his mudabbir's crimes even when the slave is wrongfully held, then recovers from the wrongdoer.

This lesson examines a layered case: a mudabbir slave is wrongfully taken by the same person twice, and each time he commits a crime. Who bears the liability? The Hanafi ruling is clear, and the commentary clarifies an intra-Hanafi dispute. Let's follow the legal reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The mudabbir is wrongfully possessed (ghasb) twice by the same person. During each possession, the slave commits a crime (jīnāyah) incurring financial liability.
The crimes are distinct; the slave is returned to the master between wrongful possessions.
2
Determine the master's liability
The master is liable for the full value of the mudabbir, divided equally between the two crime victims. This is because the master prevents the slave's freedom by the tadbīr contract, so he must compensate for the slave's torts.
The master's liability arises from his control over the slave, not from the wrongful possession.
3
Recovery from the ghāsib
The master recovers the entire value from the wrongdoer (ghāsib) because both crimes occurred while the slave was in the ghāsib's possession. The master acts as an intermediary.
The master pays the victims first, then recovers from the ghāsib.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some shaykhs reported a difference from Imam Muhammad: that the master should not pay the first victim until after recovering from the ghāsib. However, the correct view—affirmed by Fakhr al-Islam and the author of al-Hidāyah—is that there is no difference; Muhammad mentioned the case without dispute in al-Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr.
The practical consequence: under the opposed view, the first victim might not receive compensation if the master cannot recover.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The disagreement concerns the timing of payment. The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is that the master pays half to the first victim immediately and then recovers it from the ghāsib.
This ensures the victim is compensated without delay.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مدبر غصب مرتين، فجني في كل مرة، ضَمِنَ سيده قيمة لهما، ورجع بقيمته على الغاصب، ودفع نصفها إلى الأول، ورجع به على الغاصب. (مدبر غصب مرتين، فجنى في كل مرة، ضَمِنَ سيده قيمة لهما، ورجع بقيمته على الغاصب، ودفع نصفها إلى الأول، ورجع به على الغاصب): أي مُدبَّرٌ غصبه زيد مرة فجنى عنده، ثم ردَّه على المالك، ثم غصبه فجنى عنده، فعلى المالك قيمته بينهما نصفين؛ لأنه منع رقبة واحدة بالتدبير فيجب عليه قيمته، ثم يرجع بتلك القيمة على الغاصب؛ لأنَّ الجنايتين كانتا عنده، فيدفع نصفها إلى الأول، ويرجع بهِ على الغاصب قبل دفع النّصف إلى الأول، وهذا متفق عليه، وقيل: فيه خلافُ محمد كما في تلك المسألة. [1] قوله: وقيل فيه خلاف محمد؛ يعني قال بعض المشايخ في هذه المسألة خلاف محمد أيضاً كما في المسألة الأولى، حتى يسلّم للمولى ما رجع به من القيمة على الغاصب، ولا يأخذ وليّ الجناية الأولى ما بقي من حقه. وقيل: على الاتفاق، ويأخذُ وليّ الجناية الأولى تمام حقه وهو نصف القيمة من المولى إذا رجع على الغاصب. قيل: هذا هو الصحيح؛ لأنَّ محمداً ذكر هذه المسألة في «الجامع الصغير‏» بلا خلاف كذا ذكره فخر الإسلام في شرحه فعلى هذا يحتاج إلى الفرق بين المسألتين، وقد ذكره صاحب «الهداية».
[The text:] A mudabbir is wrongfully possessed twice, and he commits a crime each time. His master is liable for the value for both crimes, and he recovers that value from the wrongdoer (ghāsib), and he pays half of it to the first [claimant] and recovers it from the wrongdoer. [Commentary:] That is, a mudabbir was wrongfully possessed by Zayd once and committed a crime while with him, then he returned him to the master, then wrongfully possessed him again and committed a crime while with him. So the master owes his value divided equally between them because he prevented a single slave by the tadbīr, so he must pay his value, then he recovers that value from the wrongdoer because both crimes occurred while he was with him. So he pays half to the first and recovers it from the wrongdoer before paying the half to the first. This is agreed upon, and it is said: there is a difference of opinion from Muhammad as in that issue. [Footnote 1:] His statement: 'and it is said, Muhammad's difference of opinion' means some shaykhs said there is a difference of opinion from Muhammad here as in the first issue, so that the master keeps what he recovered from the wrongdoer and the guardian of the first crime does not take the remainder of his right. And it is said: it is by agreement, and the guardian of the first crime takes his full right, which is half the value, from the master when he recovers from the wrongdoer. It is said: this is correct because Muhammad mentioned this issue in al-Jāmiʿ al-Ṣaghīr without difference, as Fakhr al-Islam mentioned in his commentary. So on this, one needs to distinguish between the two issues, and the author of al-Hidāyah mentioned it.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary on a specific case involving a mudabbir (a slave whose manumission is deferred after the master's death). The case: the mudabbir is wrongfully possessed (ghasb) twice by the same person (Zayd). During each wrongful possession, the mudabbir commits a crime (jīnāyah) that incurs a financial liability (e.g., killing or injuring someone). The master is liable for the value of the mudabbir to compensate the crime victims because he prevented the slave's freedom by the tadbir contract. The master pays the value equally between the two crime victims (half each). He then recovers that entire value from the wrongdoer (ghāsib) because both crimes occurred while the slave was in the wrongdoer's possession. The master pays half to the first victim and recovers that half from the wrongdoer before paying it. This is the agreed-upon position. However, there is a report that Imam Muhammad differed, holding that the master does not give the half to the first victim until after recovering it from the wrongdoer. The commentary resolves this by noting that in al-Jami' al-Saghir, Muhammad mentioned it without difference, so the correct view is that there is no difference. The distinction between the two issues is explained in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave whose master has declared that he will be free upon the master's death (tadbir).
غصب (ghasb): Wrongful possession or usurpation of property without right.
جناية (jīnāyah): A crime or tort that incurs financial liability, such as killing or injuring.

Checkpoint

Buyu — Sales and Trade

6 min
Executor's Sale of Slave in Absence of Creditors and Liability
Ruling on executor selling slave in creditors' absence and liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the validity of an executor's sale of a slave in the creditors' absence, explain the liability if the slave is reclaimed, and apply the qiyas using the executor's substitution for the testator.

Core Idea
The executor's sale is valid because the creditors' right is in the wealth, not the specific slave; the executor bears warranty but recovers from the estate as agent.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses a practical scenario: an executor sells a slave from the deceased's estate to pay debts, but the creditors are absent. Is the sale valid? And if the slave is later reclaimed from the buyer after the price has been lost, who bears the loss? We will unpack the Hanafi reasoning based on qiyas and the distinction between rights attached to the asset versus the wealth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal issues
The passage presents two distinct but connected rulings: (1) the validity of the executor's sale in the absence of creditors, and (2) the liability when the sold slave is reclaimed after the price is lost.
These issues are linked because the first establishes the sale's validity, and the second clarifies the consequences.
2
Understand the ruling on validity via qiyas
The author states the sale is valid and provides the proof: the executor stands in the place of the testator (qiyas). If the testator himself sold the slave in the creditors' absence, the sale would be valid. The executor, as his substitute, has the same authority.
The key principle is that creditors' rights are attached to the maliyyah (wealth), not to the specific form (surah). The sale transforms the asset into its price, preserving the wealth.
3
Contrast with the case of the trading slave debtor
The passage distinguishes the present case from a slave permitted to trade who is in debt. There, the creditors have a right to the slave's earnings (istis'a'), so selling the slave would nullify their right; hence the sale is not valid without their presence.
This contrast highlights that the critical factor is the nature of the creditors' right: attached to the specific item or to the wealth.
4
Analyze the liability upon reclamation
The passage explains that if the slave is reclaimed from the buyer (e.g., as stolen property), the buyer recovers the price from the executor because the executor is the contracting party and bears the warranty ('uhdah). The executor then recovers from the estate as an agent.
This follows the principle that an agent bears liability for losses incurred in executing the mandate, but then has recourse against the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح بيع الوصي عبداً من التّركة بغيبة الغرماء ، وضَمِنَ وصي باغ ما أوصى ببيعه، وتصدق ثمنه فاستحق بعد هلك ثمنه معه ، ورجع في التركة وصح بيع الوصي عبداً من التركةِ بغيبة الغرماء) : أي يجوز للوصي أن يبيع لقضاء الدين عبدا من التركةِ بغيبة الغرماء. وضَمِنَ وصي باغ ما أوصى ببيعه، وتصدق ثمنه فاستحق بعد مُلْكِ ثمنه ورجع في التركة)، أوصى الميت بأن يباع هذا العبد، ويتصدق بثمنه، فباع العبد، وقبضَ الثَّمن ، فهَلك في يده ، فاسْتُحِق العبد في يد المشتري ضَمِنَ الثمن "" : أي يرجعُ المشتري بالثَّمن على الوصي، ثُمَّ الوصي يرجع في التّركة ؛ لأنه عامل للميت. قوله : أي يجوز للوصي... الخ ؛ لأنَّ الوصي قائم مقام الموصي، ولو تولى للبيع حياً بنفسه يجوز بيعه لغير محضر من الغرماء ، وإن كان في مرض موته، فكذا إذا تولاه من قام مقامه. وهذا لأنَّ حق الغرماء متعلّق بالمالية لا بالصورة، والبيع لا يبطل المالية لفواتها إلى خلف، وهو الثمن، بخلاف العبد المأذون المديون، حيث لا يبيعه مولاه أو وصيه لغير محضر من الغرماء ؛ لأنَّ لهم حق الاستسعاء، حتى يأخذوا كسبه، فيكون البيع مبطلاً لحقهم، فلهم أن يبطلوا البيع. وهاهنا حق الغرماء في الثمن لا غير فيكون البيع محقاً لحقهم لا مبطلاً، فكان بيع الوصي بمحضر من الغرماء وغير محضر منهم سواء. كذا في «غاية البيان». قوله : ضمن الوصي الثمن ؛ أي يرجعُ المشتري بالثمن على الوصي ؛ لأنه هو العاقد، فتكون العهدة عليه ؛ لأنَّ المشتري ما رضي ببذل الثمن إلا ليسلم له المبيع، ولم يُسَلَّم ، فقد أخذ الوصي حال كونه بالغاً مال غيره بغير رضاه، فيجب عليه ردّه. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : لأنه عامل للميت ؛ فيرجعُ عليه كالوكيل، حيث يرجع على الموكل بما
It is valid for the executor to sell a slave from the estate in the absence of the creditors, and the executor who sold what he was instructed to sell is liable, and he gave in charity its price, then it was reclaimed after the destruction of its price with him, and he recovers from the estate. 'And it is valid for the executor to sell a slave from the estate in the absence of the creditors' means: it is permissible for the executor to sell a slave from the estate to repay a debt in the absence of the creditors. 'And the executor who sold what he was instructed to sell is liable, and he gave in charity its price, then it was reclaimed after the ownership of its price and he recovers from the estate': the deceased bequeathed that this slave be sold and its price given in charity. He sold the slave and received the price, then it perished in his hand, then the slave was reclaimed in the hand of the buyer, so he is liable for the price: meaning the buyer recovers the price from the executor, then the executor recovers from the estate because he is acting for the deceased. He said: 'it is permissible for the executor...' because the executor stands in the place of the testator; if he himself undertook the sale while alive, his sale in the absence of the creditors would be valid, even if he were in his death-illness; similarly if one who stands in his place undertakes it. This is because the right of the creditors is attached to the wealth (malīyah) not to the specific form (sūrah), and the sale does not nullify the wealth because it is transformed into its substitute, which is the price. This is contrary to the case of the enslaved debtor who is permitted to trade, where his master or his executor cannot sell him in the absence of the creditors because they have the right of seeking earnings (isti`sā'), so that they may take his earnings; thus the sale would nullify their right, so they have the right to nullify the sale. Here the right of the creditors is only in the price, so the sale effectuates their right, not nullifies it; thus the executor's sale in the presence of the creditors and in their absence are equal. This is in Ghāyat al-Bayān. He said: 'the executor is liable for the price' means the buyer recovers the price from the executor because he is the contracting party, thus the warranty is on him; because the buyer only agreed to pay the price for the sold item to be delivered to him, and it was not delivered; so the executor, being an adult, took another's property without his consent, so he must return it. This is in al-Hidāyah. He said: 'because he is acting for the deceased', so he recovers from him like an agent, who recovers from the principal for what...
Source pages p. 639

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling and its proof concerning two interrelated issues: (1) the validity of an executor selling a slave from the estate in the absence of creditors, and (2) the liability when the sold slave is reclaimed after the price has been lost. The author begins by stating the ruling: it is valid (sahih) for the executor to sell a slave from the estate even when the creditors are not present. The proof is via qiyas (analogy) to the testator himself: if the testator, while alive, sold a slave in the absence of creditors, the sale would be valid; therefore, the executor, who stands in his place, similarly has this authority. The reasoning is that the creditors' right is attached to the wealth (maliyyah) of the estate, not to the specific item. Selling the slave does not destroy the wealth; rather, it transforms it into its substitute, the price. Thus, the creditors' right is preserved in the price. This is contrasted with the case of a mudabbar or a slave permitted to trade who is in debt: in that case, the master or executor cannot sell the slave in the absence of creditors because the creditors have the right to seek earnings (istis'a') from the slave's labor, and selling the slave would nullify that right. In the present case, the creditors only have a right in the price, so the sale is valid regardless of their presence. The second issue concerns liability: if the deceased instructed the executor to sell a slave and give its price in charity, and the executor sold it, took the price, and then the price was lost in the executor's hand, and then the slave was reclaimed from the buyer (e.g., as stolen property), the buyer recovers the price from the executor because the executor is the contracting party and bears the warranty ('uhdah). The executor then recovers from the estate because he acted as an agent for the deceased. This is analogous to an agent (wakil) who recovers from his principal for losses incurred in carrying out the mandate.

Key Terms
وصي (waṣī): Executor of the will; one who carries out the deceased's bequests and manages the estate.
غرماء (ghuramā'): Creditors of the deceased who have claims against the estate.
عهدة ('uhdah): Warranty or liability; the obligation to compensate the buyer if the sold item is reclaimed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer bears the loss because he should have checked ownership.
B) The executor bears the loss and cannot recover from the estate.
C) The buyer recovers from the executor, who then recovers from the estate.
D) The estate bears the loss directly, and the executor is discharged.

Key Takeaway
The executor's sale without creditors is valid; he bears warranty but has recourse against the estate.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Guardian's Sale of Minor's Real Estate: Conditions and Ruling
Defines excessive overreaching and lists conditions for guardian's sale of orphan's real estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of ghabn fāḥish, distinguish between the early and later Hanafi positions on guardian's sale of real estate, and list the six conditions required by later scholars for the validity of such a sale.

Core Idea
A guardian's sale of orphan's real estate is strictly regulated: later Hanafi scholars require one of six conditions to prevent exploitation.

In Hanafi law, a guardian (waṣī) managing an orphan's property is a fiduciary. The sale of real estate (ʿiqār) is especially sensitive due to its value and permanence. Early scholars allowed sale at market value, but later scholars—reflecting a critical refinement—tightened the rules to protect the orphan. This lesson unpacks the definition of 'excessive overreaching' (ghabn fāḥish) and the six conditions that must be met for a guardian's sale of real estate to be valid.

Guided Path
1
Define ghabn fāḥish
Identify the examples in the text: selling the minor's property worth fifteen for ten, or buying property worth ten for fifteen. This is the threshold that invalidates transactions.
Note the different view of Imam Muhammad, who deems any such transaction absolutely impermissible.
2
Distinguish father vs guardian
Note that the father (ab) has wider discretion: he may buy/sell at market value or within tolerable variation. The appointed guardian (waṣī) is more restricted.
The father is presumed to act in the child's best interest unless proven otherwise.
3
Understand the historical shift
Recognize the two layers: 'jawāb al-mutaqaddimīn' (early scholars) permitted sale at market value; 'ikhtiyār al-muta'akhkhirīn' (later scholars) added conditions. The muftā bihī is the later view.
This shift reflects increased caution (ihtiyāṭ) in later Hanafi fatwa.
4
List the six conditions
Memorize the six conditions from Qadi Khan as cited in the text: (1) buyer desires double the value, (2) minor needs the price for maintenance, (3) deceased's debt cannot be paid otherwise, (4) execution of an unconditional will, (5) property expenses exceed income, (6) property is dilapidated.
Conditions 1-4 are from the text's list; 5-6 are additional. The text groups them under 'qāla qāḍī khān'.
5
Apply the hierarchy of remedies
If the minor needs funds, the guardian must first sell movable property (ʿurūḍ). Only if insufficient can he sell real estate at market value or with slight overreaching, never with ghabn fāḥish.
This shows the principle of least harm and priority of preserving real estate for the orphan.

And it was interpreted as his selling his property from the minor, while it is worth fifteen for ten, or he buys the minor's property for himself, while it is worth ten for fifteen. This according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf - may Allah have mercy on them. As for according to Muhammad - may Allah have mercy on him - it is not permissible in any case.
As for the father's sale of the minor's property to himself, it is permissible for the equivalent value and for what is tolerated in mutual dealing. As for the minor's real estate, if the guardian sells it to a stranger for the equivalent value, it is permissible. This is the answer of the early scholars. The choice of the later scholars is that it is only permissible if the buyer desires it for double the value, or the guardian [sells] from himself, and his purchase if there is an apparent benefit in them, like selling what is worth nine for ten, and buying ten for nine. I say: As for real estate, there is no doubt that the advantage in purchase is doubling, and in sale is halving, because one cannot sell it to others except for double as mentioned, so how can it be permissible for him to buy it for himself at less? I consider an increase of two in ten, and a decrease of the same in other than real estate, is sufficient for advantage, because it is excessive overreaching that people do not tolerate. End. And by this it is known that the validity of his purchase is not exclusive to movable property. So understand. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar.
[1] His saying: "And the choice of the later scholars... etc."; Qadi Khan said: As for the view of the later scholars, it is not permissible for the guardian to sell real estate except with conditions:
1. One of them: that a person desires to buy it due to its low value.
2. Or the minor needs its price for maintenance.
3. Or the deceased has a debt that cannot be paid except from its price.
4. Or there is in the estate an unconditional will that requires its execution from the price of the real estate.
5. Or the sale of the real estate is better for the orphan, such that its land tax and expenses exceed its revenues.
6. Or the real estate is a shop or a house that is about to collapse and fall into ruin. If the minor needs to pay its land tax, and if there are movable goods in the estate along with the real estate, he sells what is other than the real estate; if the need is not satisfied by what is other than the real estate, then he sells the real estate for the equivalent value or with a slight overreaching, and it is not permissible for the guardian to sell with excessive overreaching that people do not tolerate in its like.
And he gives his wealth in mudaraba, partnership, and bida'a
for the minor's need for its price, or the deceased has a debt that cannot be paid except from its price). They said: And by it is given fatwa. As for the father, if he sells the minor's real estate for the equivalent value, if he is praiseworthy among people or of hidden status, it is permissible. So the statement that the sale of real estate to a stranger is only permissible when the mentioned conditions are realized, such as the buyer's desire for double the value, etc., indicates that his sale to himself is not permissible, because real estate is among the most valuable properties, so if he sells from himself, the suspicion is apparent.
( And he gives his wealth in mudaraba, partnership, and bida'a
And likewise, if the guardian buys something for the orphan, his purchase is not permissible with excessive overreaching. This is if all the heirs are minors; if they are all adults or they are present, the guardian is not allowed to sell anything from the estate except by their order.
Source pages p. 642

Commentary
This passage from Radd al-Muhtar clarifies the conditions under which a guardian (wasī) may sell the property of a minor orphan. It begins by defining 'excessive overreaching' (ghabn fāḥish): selling the minor's property for less than its value (e.g., selling an item worth fifteen for ten) or buying the minor's property for more than its value (e.g., buying an item worth ten for fifteen). According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, such transactions are invalid due to the exploitation. Imam Muhammad, however, holds they are absolutely impermissible. The father of the minor has greater latitude: he may buy or sell from himself at market value or within the range of tolerable variation. For an appointed guardian, the early scholars permitted sale of real estate at market value. The later scholars (mutā'akhkhirūn) imposed six conditions: (1) a buyer wants it for double its value, (2) the orphan needs the money for maintenance, (3) the deceased's debt cannot be paid otherwise, (4) an unconditional will requires sale, (5) the property's expenses exceed its income, or (6) the building is deteriorating. If the orphan's need arises, the guardian should first sell movable property; if insufficient, he may sell real estate at market value or with slight overreaching, but never with excessive overreaching. The text then addresses the father's sale: if he is trustworthy, selling at market value is permissible. The reason for stricter conditions on the guardian's sale to himself is the obvious conflict of interest. Finally, if all heirs are adults and present, the guardian has no authority to sell without their permission.

Key Terms
غبن فاحش (ghabn fāḥish): Excessive overreaching in price that people do not tolerate in similar transactions.
وصي (waṣī): A guardian appointed to manage the affairs of a minor orphan.
عقار (ʿiqār): Real estate, immovable property such as land or buildings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the six conditions for a guardian to sell orphan's real estate according to the later Hanafi scholars?
A) A buyer desires it for double its value.
B) The orphan needs the price for maintenance.
C) The guardian desires to buy it for himself at a lower price.
D) The property's expenses exceed its income.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi scholars permit a guardian's sale of an orphan's real estate only when one of six specific conditions is met, and never with excessive overreaching (ghabn fāḥish).
